The Red Page
Sonnets of Empire
Empire: The Growth of the Spirit.
Out of the dust and clash of war it grew,
Of sea-dogs’ stormy boast and iron pride,
Of lust of Eldorados far descried,
Of liaic of Philip and his hell-fire crew,
Of joy to follow on where Glory flew,
Of gallant galleons harried and defied ,
Of will to die the death that Nelson died,
Of all the dream the soul of Chatham knew.
And now, although the Viking days be done,
Though mercy flower from blood our
fathers poured,
Though lovelier visions light us than of
old,
Yet death is on us and our race is run
If in our scabbard sleep the lightning
sword,
If folly lull us with her dream of gold.
Nelson.
White soul of England’s glory, sovereign
star!
Ne’er shall disaster beat her down, nor
shame,
While still she sees thee by the leaping
• !
That kindled o’er Aboukir, near and far,
Or feels thee quivering through the onset’s
jar
That filled the North xoith fear of Eng-
land's name, :
Or trembles with the joy of all the fame
That died and cast out death at Trafalgar.
Thy name loas lightning, and like lightning
dy
Thine onset shivered, far and swift and
fell;
Ever thy watchivord holds us, and
whene’er
The fierce dawn breaks, and far along the
sky ■
Roars‘the last battle, all shall yet be well —
We keep the touch, thy hand and soul
are there.
Bless Eli j Are . the Strife-makers.
Blessing on him, that in his goodly hate
Of England's breed, hqth smitten and
slain her foes,
Her lust of pomp, her sloth that aped
repose,
The pride that cankered all her queenly
state,
Her youngling brawls and feuds of olden
•! date,
Envy, the jade that pricked her boys to
blow':,
Folly and ease, and all the gleaming shows
That lured her vision from the eyes of Fate.
Never was England great as at this hour
When grief hath lit her soul, and o’er her
leaps
The wings of God’s dark angel hover
and clang,
When pure by trial, she rises up in power
And oti> Hell’s serpent turns her steadfast
gase
And steels her arm to crush his loathly
fang.
—Australia to England.
By all the deeds to tliy dear glory done,
By all the life-blood spilt to serve thy
need,
By all the fettered lives thy touch hath
freed,
By all thy dream in us anew begun;
By all the guerdon English sire to son
Hath given of highest vision, kingliest
deed,
By all thine agony, of God decreed
For trial and strength, our fate with thine
is one.
Still dwells thy spirit in our hearts and lips.
Honor and life we hold from none but
thee,
And if we live thy pensioners no more
But seek a nation’s might of men and ships,
’Tis but that when the world is black
with war
Thy sons may stand, beside thee strong
and free.
Australia, 1914.
The night is thick with storm and driving
cloud,
Lurid at instants through the blackness
break
Quick gleams of war across the perilous
lake
From yonder isles that awe and magic
shroud:
Far in the Northland smite Thor’s hammers
loud
On steel that warlocks for her spoilure
malcc,
Till lo! from sleep Australia starts awake
And lifts the queenly head that sloth had
bowed.
Not yet her eyes are clear: throughout her
brain
Stitt swtirm the antic creatures of her
dream,
The idiot jests, the sports that kill the
soul,
Yet shall not night lay hold on her again.
For through the rack she spies the morn-
ing gleam
Clear on the sword that lights her to
tier goal.
From “Sonnets of the Empire,” by Archibald
T. Strong (Macmillan, 35.).
J*
Archibald T. Strong proclaims himself a
minor master of the sonnet in his fine series
of Sonnets of the Empire, Before and Dur-
ing the Great War (Macmillan. 35.). His
scheme was evidently to trace the spiritual
growth of the British Empire throueh the
lives of the great men who have made it;
but the war intruded, postponing the con-
clusion of the design. In the ante-bellum
davs that seem so far away a poet could
envisage tne whole of such a grandiose sub-
ject as this and conceive its final, conclu-
sive sonnet. But now the scheme is broken:
there cannot be, for years yet, the comple-
tion of the edifice. As Strong says, “The
present struggle opens a new, and, we may
hope, a greater chapter in our race’s his-
tory.” But this disruption of the unity of
the subject is compensated for. by a series
of magnificent sonnets of wartime. It is a
pity, however, that the volume end
on such a weak note as Strong’s “The Keep-
ing of the Door,” already published in
The Melba Gift Booh.
Incidentally, the sonneteer apologises:
“As in the present crisis it seems the duty
of every man of military age who publishes
a book to explain why he is writing instead
of fighting, I may state that I have tried
to follow the latter way, and that it is not
my fault but my bitter misfortune that I
have been thrown back on to the former.”
Ai. apology that should find place in every
other book that henceforth is published in
Australia.
The series of sonnets starts with Alfred,
and leads down, so poor is our knowledge
of the Empire’s land-marks, through many
names that are to most of us merely names,-
to the inconclusive sonnet on Belgium. Thus
this book is not only poetry but a liberal
education. The quality of the sonnets varies,
as is but natural, but nowhere is Strong
guilty of bad craftsmanship, His work is
dignified and concise; he does not love
words for themselves: their work is to con-
vey thought or create an image ; and the
series is throughout infused with a brave
and splendid patriotism, worthily expressed.
The three sonnets upon Australia, one of
which is here .quoted, are permanent testi-
monies to the Australian faith that should
be ours. Our appreciation of this volume
is evidenced by the number of sonnets that
insisted upon quotation.
E verywo m an ' s Business
Angela’s Business (Constable, per Dym-
ock’s Library) is Every woman’s business.
Angela is a temporary spinster, who has no
intention of making her adjective permanent.
Henry Sydnor Harrison, whose Queed and
F.F.’s .Ej(es marked him as an author of pro-
raise, has not improved upon his earlier
successes—nor has be badly disappointed
us. He still keeps to his meticulous exami-
nation of the microscopic souls of women;
his hero is still quaint and “literary” ; and he
j s still humorous; but the humor is also
“literary,” and the cleverness is rather
laborious. Often he commits the cardinal
s j n 0 f th e novelist: he is dull. But his sub-
ject is an interesting one, and it is treated
with the fullness of a scientific document.
Angela and Mary Wing are the types
selected for dramatic contrast. Angela
thinks that if a woman gets wrapped up in
business—and all that—she’s apt to miss
some of the best things of life. For herself,
she {) e ii eves that Just Making a Home is all
the business a woman could possibly attend
to. Her business is The Business of Supply-
ing Beauty and Supplying Charm. She is
mere iy a Nice Girl, with not a thing to do in
the world but to sit and think about men—
one 0 f the Waiting Women of Romance.
She is one of the myriads of girls waiting
over the world: —
“Girls brought up by simple parents who
assumed that life was still as simple as
they. g j r i s made to stake their whole lives
on the vague expectation that, because they
are girls, some man or other will be sure to
want.them some day; a million girls waiting.
da Y by da y and year by year, while all they
have to offer life fades away under their eyes.
•• • - Surely it had not been Angela’s fault if
she was, early apprised though through a
sweet mist, that she had but one faculty of
any value in the world's market,; not her
fault if, amid general approval, she innocently
spent her youth and idleness in tricking out
her value and bringing it steadily to the
attention of the only beings who could have
USe for it Least of all was it hei . fault if
her particular business and her odd special-
ised training were bounded, not by marriage,
» weddmg-day.”
The assumption isj- of course, that all
nice womanly girls would, on marriage,
develop into perfect wives, by instinct, un-
complicated by any expert training or advice.
By refraining from being anything but
womanly a girl would, when wedded, imme-
diately become a competent wife and mother.
Angela had been taught to put a preposter-
ous over-valuation upon the mere possession
of her sex -
Mary Wing is her foil. A thoroughly com-
petent woman, an independent school-teacher
who fights her own fight uncompanioned
through life. Yet Harrison hardly plays
fair: he snatches the best qualities of
Angela to complete Mary’s otherwise one-
sided and too-specialised soul. Mary Wing
is not the j ust antithesis of Angela; she is
ra ther the ordinary clever woman who earns
her own living. Harrison, to be fair, should
have given Mary none of Angela’s softnesses,
presented Mary’s type with a hard honesty,
and then let us take our choice. Or, per-
haps, Mary Wing should have been the in-
termediate, balanced type between the man-
aging woman and the woman who. wants to
be managed. <
Unfortunately, too. for his moral, he pre-
sents us with a hero who is insufficiently
manly. According to Weininger, Charles is
just the effeminate type that Mary Wing
would unconsciously seek and live happy
with ever after. Harrison ought to have
shown us as Mary’s mate the blatantly
masculine bloke.
War Plays
In Across the Border (.Holt, New York;
3s. 6d.) Beulah Marie Dix, a U.S. dramatist
with some past performances, writes an
unusual war drama. The scene of the first
act is laid at the front—any front at any
war; and the characters speak English, but
they are no more intended to be English than
they are intended to be German or Servian.
The soldiers wear uniform, but not the dis-
tinctive uniform, in cut or color, of any one
of the nations now at war. Thus you can
take your choice, though that is really no
choice, since the combatants on only one side
are represented, and there is no villain in the
piece. The hero, a junior lieutenant, has to
cross dangerous country, a tkousand-to-one
chance against death, to bring reinforce-
ments. He goes, leaving his companions to
speculate on his chance. Three rifle shots
ring out. “God A’mighty, they’ve got him!”
exclaims a trooper.
But in the next act the junior lieutenant
enters a quiet cottage, apparently far from
the firing line. He demands a horse. He
has been wounded, and is dazed. The people
seem peaceful and strange. As the lieutenant
threatens them with his revolver, “the w r all
behind him is seen, as it were, to melt.
Behind him, and overtopping him, stands a
dark man, powerfully built, who, without
hurry or noise, pinions him by the elbows.”
To his surprise the lieutenant finds that the
flowers in the cottage are spring flowers, yet
when he left for reinforcements it was
autumn. Other elements of strangeness
startle him, until the master of the house
asks him, “You would never be afraid to
die?”
“What are you driving at?” the lieutenant
asks ; then, controlling himself: “No. Of
course, I shouldn’t be afraid.”
“Ever thought of what it would be like,
after you were dead?” the master asks.
The lieutenant replies: “No.”
Then the other says: “Ever thought that
it would be like this?”
The lieutenant recognises that he has been
killed, that he is now de.ad. But now he has to
be made to understand the real meaning of
war. He is taken by the master to a bleak
place of ice and wind. The lieutenant bad
been a good soldier, and done all the things
that even good soldiers do in war. But he
says that everything he had done, the burn-
ing of villages, the turning of women and
children out into the night, the sort of
things of which the reports from the front
have made us familiar —all this he had done
“according to rule.” And there he is made
to see the results of every one of his acts.
“All we ask,” says his guide, “is that a man
should take a quiet look at what he’s done.”
And the things he is made to see are not at
all nice. But because the lieutenant is,
after all, a rather decent chap, he is allowed,
on his own plea, to go back to the earth —
to tell them all about it.—
He is found lying in bed in an improvised
field hospital, apparently dead. He revives,
tries to tell an officer of what he has seen,
and is gently laughed at for his delusions —
“clean off his head!” He wearily closes his
eyes. A girl—the girl he had met in the cot-
tage “across the border,” appears. She
has come to take him home. _He dies. And
the surgeon,, over-worked, seeing at the door
two Red Cross bearers, with a stretcher, on
which lies another blood-stained figure, says,
“Well, we’ve no time to stand sentimentalis-
ing. Get that bed clear! This is war.”
The drama is vivid and actual, the charac-
ters are clearly individualised; and the
moral —that war is not only hell, but an ex-
ceedingly foolish hell—is brilliantly stated.
There is, too, in the figure of the lieutenant
a touch of genuine poetry. Across the
Border is the sort of war play that we need
in Australia —and don’t get.
d*
It is time Stephen Phillips stopped—or
was made to stop. His Armageddon (Lane,
per Dymock’s Library) is futile. Phillips
uses all the old stage props, the vision of
Joan of Arc, and various views of Hell, with
Beelzebub (whose purgatory is to go through
existence with a comic name), Belial,
Moloch and Satan. There is no necessity
to drag in Hell or Satan in a war drama:
all you need is a trench and a Hun. The
play is written partly in prose and partly
in verse: the prose is poor prose and the
verse is occasionally poetry—and even the
poetry clogs the action. When the mother
and the sweetheart of a soldier learn that
he has been killed, the shock does not find
utterance in brief, unfinished sentences but
in pages of blank verse. The girl would not
remark that she “ stood tiptoe upon youth’s
primrose-hank ,” and “Blew warm kisses o’er
the sea of time.” Nor would the soldier’s
mother mention that she had “labored on
that growing soul as patient as a sculptor
on his marble” and contemplate the differ-
ence between “ the sigh of a girl and a
woman’s agony.” Nor would she look for
metaphors like these: —
Child, xohen a wave long-gathered, and so
vast,
Bursts on the rock, with what a moan at
last
On melancholy shingles it recedes!
The satire is clumsy, too. Stephen shows
us the chief of the German Press Bureau
in Berlin dismissed for inadvertently pub-
lishing the truth. Wrong psychology! The
German believes his lies are the truth, just
as he believes his might is right. Would a
German plead against his dismissal his
“long, honorable career l of ceaseless lies..:.
his stainless record of fabrication”? And
would he defy anybody to charge him “with
telling the truth on any single occasion”?
Yet sometimes the poetry is poetry, and in
place. There is this picture of Belgium:—
It is a land haunted , not habited....
I stand for Belgium; she asks vengeance
here ....
For the old man shot down at his own door,
The girl polluted and the woman raped,
For children that implore us icithout hands,
And figures like disfeatured statues left.
That Phillips might have made a drama out
of his subject is indicated in the epilogue,
where, in Hell, Attila hints that there is a
moral force greater than armed might, and
that this moral force will win the war.
I am aware in mid-rage and mid havoc
Of some strange influence, I know not what;
A Power that is not Force — stronger than
Force —
And soft as summer overcoming me....
What Lord can so subdue the Lord of Huns?
I met not Him when first I scoured the
Earth.
Satan, too, is affected: • -
What beam is this that searches us at last,
And troubles Hell? Soft — yet it more
afflicts me ■. % ' \
Than the fierce fighting that did scar my
face,
When I with all my angels fell from Heaven.
I may not quail; but I begin to suffer
In this beginning of some final light,
In which I fear at last to be absorbed.
Now all my being is in deep travail,
Under a dreadful fall of gentleness,
A flower-soft Influence omnipotent ....
My Power, my Power, why art thou leaving
me? \
Armageddon has already been withdrawn.
The wonder is that ever it should have been
produced.
The Spring number of The Studio is Old
English Mansions, a collection of reproduc-
tions of pictures of these survivals from a
more artistic age. Von Tirpitz will be
pleased to discover how much architectural
beauty there is still in England as target for
his bombs.
Dowell O’Reilly objects to Dunsany
prose: —
The Red Page (29/9/T5) quotes some-
thing by Lord Dunsany called “The Raft-
builders,” which is printed as prose. But is
it? Consider this passage:—
See now oblivion shimmering all around us,
Its very tranquillity deadlier than a tempest.
How little all our keels have troubled it.
Time in its deeps swims like a monstrous whale,
And, like a whale, feeds on the littlest things—
Small tunes and little unskilled songs
Of olden golden evenings—and anon
Turneth whale-like to overthrow whole ships.
See now the wreckage of Babylon floating idly,
And something there that once was Nineveh;
Already their kings and queens are in the deeps
Among
The weedy masses of old centuries
That hide the sodden hulk of sunken Tyre
And make a darkness round Persepolis.
His Lordship gives that passage to the
world as prose. It contains exactly one word
of clean,''wholesome prose guaranteed unde.r
the Pure Food Act —“Among.” A really
beautiful word, when you come to think of
it —in such surroundings. How solid and
restful it seems, emerging above that vol-
canic torrent of rhythmic mud. The mind
clutches at it—clings to it —till finally over-
whelmed by 'f
The weedy masses of old centuries
That hide the sodden hulk of sunken Tyre,
And make a darkness round Persepolis.
Lord Dunsany’s blank verse does not
appeal to me, but among his prose works
“Among” will live: it is a little gem in its
way.
Dunsany is in good company, for R.S.L.
points out that C. J. Dennis’s contribution
to Melba’s Gift Book, an article entitled
“The Year Around in Timber-Land,” though
printed as prose, is almost entirely composed
of iambic pentameters. Naturally I The
author of the blanky Australaise, even when
writing prose, cannot help dropping into
verse. It looks rather well, too, when
chopped up into verse-lengths.
And then he rambles in a plaintive tone,
Asking the Fates what strange thing ailed his mate.
“Full forty head of fowls she had,” he wails.
“What more could woman want? Three pigs she had,
An’ bacon hangin’ on the kitchen wall.
What ailed her? Hadn’t she the two best cows—
The best a woman ever sat beneath?
And yet—ah, it’s a queer, strange world, it is,
An’ I should know it after eighty years— ;
An’ thirty since my missus was alive.”

MISS ADDINGTON,
Modern Miracles
—The beautiful daughter of—
Mrs. R. A. Addington, Argent St.,
Broken Hill, is declared to have
been cured of St. Vitus’ Dance by
CLEMENTS TONIC
RECENTLY in France a peasant woman whose little girl had
been bedridden with paralysis for years, had occasion to
visit a churchyard which contained the statue of a well-
Known saint. Some of the mould from the tomb adhered to her
Handkerchief, which, upon touching her child, cured her. This
tact is vouched for upon reliable authority.
Miracles are not all confined to the older countries, for we have
heard also of similar remarkable incidents in newer countries,
ver y often in Australia. A few years ago a certain section
of Broken Hill was electrified by the recovery from apparently
incurable St. Vitus’ Dance of the daughter of Mrs. R. A. Adding-
ton. of Argent Street. It was on the 10th October, 1911, when
first the mysterious cure was reported. The mother, being seen
by a special interviewer, and, overcome by gratitude that her
daughter was healthy again, told the facts at once. She said
that the disorder occurred through her daughter falling from a
tram. This gave her a great shock, and so affected her nervous
system that eventually St. Vitus’ Dance ensued. She became so
bad that it was impossible to hold a knife and fork, and going out
of the house was out of the question. This remarkable condition
continued for some time, medical skill being useless Mrs
Addington declared, WHEN SUDDENLY, AS THE RESULT OF
USING CLEMENTS TONIC, a wonderful change took place. She
gradually improved, and soon regained her normal condition
Nearly two years have elapsed since her complete recovery and
all that time she has enjoyed the best of health. “I have great
faith in Clements Tonic, and always recommend it,” added the
mother, ‘‘and you can make what use you wish of this.”
ALL STORES AND CHEMISTS SELL IT EVERYWHERE.
See you get the Original Medicine. Do not accept any “just as good”
remedy. There is only one real Clements Tonic.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Woman Speaks.
There is a spot, a dreadful spot,
Beside a moaning sea,
Where one dear head was bowed in death
That meant the world to me.
I may not shed a coward’s tears ;
I may not nurse my pain ;
I have to face the long,' dull years
Until we meet again.
I have a life, an empty life,
Shaped to no trivial end ;
A life that must be spent, and yet
Is not all mine to spend.
And mine the duty so to live,
Until the lights grow dim,
That at the end I am not shamed
To go to be with him.
We have a land, a glorious land,
That breeds the hero strain ;
For which to die, for which to live,
Through any depths of pain.
They did not count the cost, our men,
When came the time of stress ;
They gave the best they had to give,
And we can do no less.
For us the task, the weighty task ;
For us, by help of God.
To set our sons’ feet in the steep,
Straight path their fathers trod.
That so they face the toils of life,
Or face a hail" of shell.
As not unworthy heirs of them
That stormed the Dardanelles.
There is a spot, a halloweu spot,
Beside a summer sea,
Where those ran gaily on to die
Who died for you and me.
God, make us in this stricken hour
Put all of self aside;
And aid our grim resolve —to live
As bravely as they died !
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
S. TALBOT SMITH.
Dreams and Dust.
Ah me! Again the almonds are in flower!
I know that I have said that once before;
But that was in a somewhat brighter hour,
For she was here —and she is here no more.
No matter how, or why ; no matter if.
Let us suppose .that she was but a myth
Of sentimental fancy, like a whiff
Of blossom-scent, for verse to trifle with.
If she be not, I am no longer I;
And, if that love were dreaming, life’s a
dream,
From which to wake is what we call to die.
And things we cherish are not what they
seem.
I climb the hill, and find the dim retreat,
And seek the ashes of our fires long cold;
I even seek the print of little feet,
And fancy feigns to find them as of old.
And so the dust seems precious, and endued
With vital thrill, and glowing memory ;
And I would guard it from all footsteps rude,
So sacred is this silent shrine to me.
And, if the dust is sacred, for that now
I have lost memory of a love-lit dream,
Where are our values? What is left to vow
Before the shrine of living Love supreme?
I think I see some light. These blossoms pure
Are our fine dreams, that charm, and pass
away;
In perfect beauty only they endure
Until the living fruit has seen the day.
True, true; yet, how so often they may twine
Their bridal-wreaths of fancy, never more
May that lost love-of-dreams again be mine.
Heigho! But there are others, to be sure!
MAJORCA, SPAIN.
JAMES LINDO WEBB.
That Brute Bill.
I met a lady weeping. She
Complained : “Bill is not kind to me.
When I’m home late the door he’ll slam.
He does not care how sad I am.
“I love the theatre,” she said,
“But Bill is such a dunderhead.
When Egbert brings me home at night
Bill uses talk that isn’t right.
“I have a friend who says that he
Is anxious to be kind to me;
But Bill’s so jealous that I dare
Not go with Arthur anywhere.
“I have one weakness, I confess,
A weakness for becoming dress ;
But Bill says furs (you know these men)
Don’t fit a weekly four-pound-ten.
“In motors I adore to drive;
It’s good to feel that one’s alive;
But Billy thinks I’d go too far
In Toby Smith’s new motor-car.
“Platonic friendships I admire,
But Bill says where there’s smoke there’s
fire.
When all one’s instincts are so good
It’s hard to be misunderstood.
“Jim was so good to me when I
Went down to Melbourne last July ;
But Bill says he will punch Jim’s nose
First chance. I hate low threats like those!”
Her foot was small, her ankle slim,
And I felt envious of Jim.
She had a captivating head.
“I think my heart is broke,” she said.
I yearned to comfort her —but how?
Gently I kissed her puckered brow.
My sympathy leapt up in flame :
She was a sore-afflicted dame.
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.

THE LEAK IN THE PUBLIC CASH-
BOX.
Broadly speaking, the argument against
Private Enterprise in certain directions is
that it is a greedy cormorant; that it gener-
ally fleeces the public, sweats the worker,
and, withal, gives indifferent service. And
the justification for Sosli is that by public
management a community can secure a fair
deal; that waste can be eliminated, to the
great benefit of the taxpayer, both as con-
sumer and producer; that the worker can
get a just return for labor performed under
good conditions, and reliability and efficiency
take the place of chaos. These are the
standards by which Sosli must be judged
generally and in detail, since in so far as it
conforms to the plans and specifications will
it be justified as a part of public policy.
Belief in Sosli is not quite the same thing
as belief in Euclid’s theory that if the
sides of two triangles are equal their angles
are equal, or in the doctrine that when a
man is dead he is no longer alive. Neither
that theory nor the doctrine can be altered.
They represent immutable truth, irrespective
of time and space, being of equal certainty
in Greenland and Canberra. Yet a Sosli
cold-drink shop in the Arctic Circle might
very easily prove a rank failure, just as a
Sosli cod-liver oil refinery at Australia’s
national capital would certainly be a
pathetic illustration of lunacy. Sosli is a
good working theory provided that the
ground over which it operates is suitable
for its development, that direction and con-
trol are intelligent, and that the commu-
nity is sufficiently vigilant to insist upon
the protection of public interests. There
was a time in the history of most nations
when national defence was run on Pri-
vate Enterprise principles—when barons and
other persons kept soldiers about their
premises and hurried them around to take
part in any scrap that might be going. But
as these individuals were also accustomed
to break out on their own account and the
land was generally filled with battle and
bloodstain, conditions were thankfully
changed, and Sosli principles came into
vogue. These have often been disastrous
enough, but no one ever dreams nowadays
of an army controlled by Private Enterprise
or a navy by Admiral Individualism. On
the other hand, the person who imagines
that the collective principle of Defence will
flourish on rotten administration, on the
disregard of efficiency and on an insolent
contempt for the public, is harboring a great
delusion. It is not the principle that is
wrong in cases of that kind —and the world
has seen many such cases—but the abuse of
trust by those in whose hands the principle
has been placed for keeping, combined with
the somnolent indifference of the people con-
cerned. This indifference is the most sinister
ot all influences at work to turn collective
effort into failure; for, after all, Sosli in
any form is but a reflection of the civic
alertness of the community.
* * *
Take, for example, the Australian Post
Office. _ A post office run by Private Enter-
prise in Australia is almost unthinkable;
but it would be very little worse, and might
conceivably be better, than a Sosh post office
which eats up £500,000 a year of the tax-
money> which in the largest city of
the Commonwealth provides the most awful
telephone system on the face of the earth,
which employs thousands of superfluous
hands, and is so tied up with red tape as
to be a more dreadful example of circum-
locution than the British War Office. There
is no possible excuse for this. It represents
a Sff'oss administrative scandal, a betrayal
of the public at every point, and a heavy
indictment of the successive Governments
that have allowed such a monument of in-
eptitude to be piled up. The explanation of it
all is obvious. The Post Office has been too
big. a thing for the people who have got hold
of its administration and control. Drift has
been easy and will continue to be easy
unless the people stir themselves and see
that this great national institution is not
allowed to be the sport of politicians or the
material on which some party nominee,
pitchforked from a Eiberal or Labor Caucus,
can try his ’prentice hand or display his
VG j e ~i? a sility. As things are now
and have been for years the Australian Post
Office, with absolute monopoly of the tele-
phone, postal and telegraph services of the
continent, is a sink for public money,
squandered in hatsful because, apparentlv. it
is no one’s business to see that anything
else happens. Even if the nation were as
rich as Crcesus it could not stand this sort
of. thing. As the position is to-day and cer-
tainly will be to-morrow, the obligation
upon the Government to honestly set about
ending the outrage is so definite that every
hour of delay will condemn Parliament as
recreant to a pressing duty. Even if the
people are careless no Australian Govern-
ment can any longer be indifferent to what
becomes of £lo.o€o of revenue per week. It
is beginning to look as if the money wasted
by Governments in Australia would pay the
interest charges on the war borrowings, and
go a long way towards meeting the other
heavy charges that will have to be borne
after the war is over.
* * * *
ffhe need in Australia for brains and cour-
age in administrative and executive positions,
and for an alert public opinion, has always
been acute, but never more so than now. In-
competent muddlers and conscienceless adven-
turers have for a long time been conspiring
to turn public enterprises into burdpns in-
stead of vehicles of relief—-into conspirators
with Private Enterprise for the impoverish-
ment of the people. Westralia’s shocking
exploits with steamers and butcheries; the
\ ictorian closer settlement and irrigation
undertakings; N. S. Wales with its large
sums wasted at .Yanco, its Norton Grif-
fiths shindykit, its dreadful lime works, and
the rest of a disastrous outfit—all these
things call for hard thinking and enlightened
action. To call them experiments in Sosh
and then fall to sleep is merely to encourage
incompetence to go to the Devil. The
poisonous feature of it all is that considera-
tions of Party hang like a nail over every-
thing connected with public enterprise. If
Labs, are in and Jones, of the other side,
challenges the post-office administration or
the brick-kiln or the butcher’s-shop. then he
is overwhelmed with shouts of opprobrium.
If the Libs, are on deck, and the Labs, raise
a howl about the same thing, the Libs, yell
denunciation of such scandalous tactics. "So
tlm weary old farce goes on, each side strug-
gling for party advantages and petty verbal
triumphs, while the public interests are
obscured and vital national concerns are
imperilled. Can anyone believe for a moment
that with a system of non-party Elective
Ministries there would be tolerated by Par-
liament an annual loss of £500.000 bn the
Post Office? That a Postmaster-General
would be considered fit for his job who
allowed such v a thing? Or that the mess
would not be 'denounced as unendurable by
men of every-kind of political faith? To
suppose the contrary would be to believe
that the democracy had a preference for re-
presentation bj idiots.. And it hasn’t. Our
Parliaments are not composed of lunatics —-
though it often looks like it —but of men
who are as well aware of the existing scan-
dals as the rest of the community. The
trouble is that they are tied up hand and
foot by a system which holds them and the
in a thrall that is absolute, and
which, unless they burst it, will bring to dis-
aster every one of the finer civic ideals of the
Australian people, and leave the country a
prey to the logroller and wirepuller.

VICTORIA.
THE SENTIMENTAL BLOKE.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
If We Should Go Down.
The American writer Owen Wister, who,
revisiting it once more, found Germany in
May and June, 1914, “a constant pleasure
to the eye, a constant repose to the body
and mind,” put then to himself the ques-
tion : “Suppose a Soul, arrived o’n earth
from another world, without any mortal
ties whatever, were; given its choice, after
a survey of the nations, which it should be
born in and belong to? In May,
June and July, 1914, my choice,” he
answers, “would have been, not France,
not England, not • America, but Ger-
many.” That much introduction is neces-
sary to the man who has compiled the fol-
lowing sweet serenade for the Saturday
Evening Post, of Philadelphia—the paper
Benjamin. Franklin founded :
Here is an embodiment, a composite statement, of
Prussianism, compiled sentence by sentence from the
utterances of Prussians, the Kaiser and his generals,
professors, editors, and Nietzsche, part of it said in
cold blood, years before this war, and all of it a
declaration of faith now being ratified by action: —
“We Hohenzollerxs take our crown from Go.d
alone. On me the Spirit of Gor> has descended. I
regard my whole task as appointed by Heaven. Who
opposes me I shall crush to pieces. Nothing must be
settled-in this world without the intervention of the
German Emperor. He who listens to public opinion
inns a danger of inflicting immense harm on the
State. When one occupies certain .positions in the
world one ought to make dupes rather than friends.
Christian morality cannot be political. Treaties are
only a disguise to conceal other political aims. Re-
member that the German people are the chosen of
God,
“Might is right, and is decided by war. Every
youth who enters a beer-drinking and duelling club
will receive the true direction of his life. War
in itself is a good thing. God will see to it that war
always recurs. The efforts directed toward the
abolition of war must not only be termed foolish,
but absolutely immoral. The peace of Europe is only
a secondary matter for us. The sight of suffering
does one good ; the infliction of suffering does one
more good. This war must be conducted as ruth-
lessly as possible.
“The Belgians should not be shot dead. They
should be so left as to make impossible all hope of
recovery. The troops are to treat the Belgian civil
population with unrelenting severity and frightful-
ness. Weak nations have not the same right to live
as powerful nations. The world has no longer need
of little nationalities. We Germans have little
esteem and less respect for Holland. We need to
enlarge our colonial possessions; such territorial
acquisitions we can only realise at the cost of other
States.
“Russia must no longer be our frontier. The
Polish press should be annihilated, likewise the
French and Danish. The Poles should be allowed
three privileges: to pay taxes, serve in the army,
and shut their jaws. France must be so completely
crushed that she will never again cross our path. You
must remember that we have not come to make war
on the French people, but to bring them the higher
Civilisation. The French have shown themselves
decadent and without respect for the Divine law.
Against England we fight for booty. Our real enemy
is England. We have to crush absolutely perfidious
Albion, subdue her to such an extent that her influ-
ence all over the world is broken for ever.
“German should replace English as the world lan-
guage. English, the bastard tongue, must be swept
into the remotest corners until it has returned to its
original elements of an insignificant pirate dialect.
The German language acts as a blessing, which, com-
ing direct from the hand of God, sinks into the heart
like a precious balm. To us, more than any other
nation, is entrusted the true structure of human ex-
istence. Our own country, by employing military
power, has attained a degree of Culture which it
could never have reached by peaceful means.
Tile civilisation of mankind suffers every time a
German becomes an American. Let us drop our
miserable attempts to excuse Germany’s action. We
willed it. Our might shall create a new law in
Europe. It is Germany that strikes. We are morally
and intellectually superior beyond all comparison.
We must fight with Russian beasts, English mercen-
aries and Belgian fanatics. We have nothing to
apologise for. It is no consequence whatever if all
the monuments ever created, all the pictures ever
painted, all the buildings ever erected by the great
architects of the world, be destroyed. The ugliest
stone placed to mark the burial of a German grena-
dier is a more glorious monument than all the cathe-
drals of Europe put together. No respect for the
tombs of Shakspeare, Newton and Faraday.
“They call us barbarians. What of it? The Ger-
man claim must be Education to hate, Organisation of
hatred, Education to the desire for hatred. Let us
abolish uni ipe and falso shame. To us is given faith,
"^P e H' ; but hatred is the greatest among
To be saved from the lieel of a race which
has this creed—is it not worth enlisting for?
Is it not worth emptying the pockets for?
The Australian Kid in the Cow-yard.
Again the daily papers are dotted with the
cow cocky and the toiling kids and expres-
sions of fierce indignation at this horrible
exploitation of children. About the broad
facts of this wretched business there is no
possible room for doubt. In all of the dairy-
ing districts of Australia and Maoriland
the families of very many butter-fat farmers
are disgracefully overworked—-robbed of
sleep, drained of vitality, stunted of body
and soul. The scandal is growing as time
rolls on. It was gross 10 years ago; it is
grosser to-day. There has been no ameliora-
tion of the conditions —nothing but talk,
sympathetic and denunciatory phrases, ex-
pressions of disgust that sucb things should
be. Is that the end ? Are the men who
have time after time exposed this abuse to
understand that the community refuses to
recognise any social obligation? It really
looks like that. Yet everyone who knows
intimately the sordid condition of cockydom
also knows that reform is demanded, not
only in the interests of the youngsters, but
equally for the sake of the women and of
the children to come. Bad as the juvenile
slavery is, the women in many dairying
areas are even more cruelly abused. And
most of the men, too, suffer irreparable
damage, _ mentally, morally and physically.
Any citizen who imagines that he is-going
to with this accumulated mass of
evil by confining himself to yelps about the
wrongs done to children is cherishing a large
delusion. The proof thereof is- that after
years and years of yelp the situation is
worse than ever. The thing has got to be
tackled, at the base and the causes of the
family enslavement rooted out. If there is
any-way. of applying a corrective and still
leaving the land problem as it is, somebody
ought to come forward and say how it can
be* done. _ Pending that explanation the
drowsy kid and toil-wracked woman of the
Cow areas must stand as part of the price
the community pays for a land system which
in every other country where the salient
features of the same system prevail has
produced the same crop of social wrong. Can
anyone give the _ name and address of a
single cow-cocky in Australia or Maoriland
who, taking up land at the prices prevailing
of recent years—£2o, £5O, up to £7O and £BO
an acre—has not sweated himself and his
wife and his children? And in nine cases
out of 1,0 such sweating has not been
from choice, but has been forced upon him
by iron circumstance. Taking all wages
into consideration, those paid by dairying
are the/worst. The land owner has a large
reservoir of helpless, dependent labor to
draw on, and it is into his pocket that in
the epd there drifts the difference between
the meagre earnings of the serf and what
should fairly be the full reward of labor
Meanwhile it is good to be told that Aus-
tralia must produce more wheat and butter
and cheese —more fat lamb and bacon and
potato. And also more stunted, .sleepy
youngster. If that is not put into the bill,
then -the list is written in a coward’s hand,
because Australia cannot get one without
the other, as things are now.

V* A System with Holes in It.
The Defence administration is well-meaning
but involved and wooden-headed. The authori-
ties seem to have understood from the first
what a military camp should be, yet con-
ditions are still so bad in certain of them
that hardened bushwhackers fall away after
a taste of the life they offer. Recruiting cam-
paigns are in progress, and simultaneously
recruits are handled so that they become


Involuntary anti-enlistment agents. There
is something wrong also with the financial
organisation. Here is a sample case. A
young man who enlisted at the beginning
of the war was wounded four days after
the landing at Gallipoli, and invalided to
England. From that time to the present,
he asserts, he has not had a farthing of
pay. Incidentally, on his own allegation,
none of his relatives’ letters to him have
been delivered, other than those sent direct
to England. Nothing, again, could be more
short-sighted than the official attitude to-
wards venereal cases. By discussing the
problem of venereal disease in hushed tones,
or by refusing to discuss it altogether, by
omitting to make it compulsorily notifiable
on the grounds that both patients and
doctors would refuse to notify, and by other
devices, the community has established this
trouble on a pinnacle of infamy. If every
politician who acquired the disease were
at once indicated to the public, the Parlia-
ments of the Commonwealth might be badly
depleted. The same applies to the Bar,
medicine and other callings. Only one citi-
zen has his misfortune literally howled into
the ear of the public, and he is the man
who is risking his life to keep the public
in security and comfort. If a private citizen
acquires syphilis the fact is glossed over at
every turn ; in the case of a private soldier
he is put behind a barb-wire entanglement if
the mishap occurs at home, and if it occurs
abroad he is shipped back under conditions
that leave no doubt in anyone’s mind as to
what is wrong with him. _ This paper is
all for compulsory notification of venereal
disease. But it has no faith in a system of
compulsory notification only for the coun-
try’s defenders. To put it on the lowest
possible plane, it must do a lot to_ frighten
men off enlisting, since cold statistics go to
show that 35 per cent, of men between 18
and 35 may look to become affected as
society is at present constituted. All round,
a certain disregard for the rights and pre-
judices of the soldier, once he has been
secured, appears to be the keynote of the
Defence Department’s failure, where it has
failed; and the thousands of able-bodied
ycung men who have not joined the colors
provide the best proof that the country
can’t afford that sort of attitude.

The Relative Value of Pumpkins and
Pictures.
A current British magazine presents a pic-
turesque yarn by J. S. Cormack, N.S.W.
irrigation officer in London and thereabouts,
concerning “Rivers in Harness —Australia’s
Latest Wonderful Scheme to Supply her
Farmers with Water.” It is a profusely-
illustrated story, and all about Yanco, with
views of the dam and the weir, and all the
rest of it. Says Cormack
There is now nearing completion in New South
Wales a vast irrigation scheme, worthy of recognition
on account of its magnitude, its engineering wonders,
its great cost, and as a commendable example of an
enterprising Government’s effort to popularise the
land by making it productive and attractive.
True enough, so far as the cost goes, anyhow.
Yanco represents £5,500,000 of Cohen’s
good cash. But by a coincidence it happens
that at the moment the magazine was print-
ing, Carmichael, M.L.A., was taking evi-
dence as a Royal Commissioner in order to
find out exactly what else Yanco represents.
There seems a lot of confusion on the subject.
While Cormack is assuring the British pub-
lic that Yanco will grow anything prolifically,
residents have been endeavoring to persuade
Carmichael that when they sow wheat they
get tares. Also, Cormack’s assurance that
“Australia takes a paternal interest in the
welfare of the settlers” is apparently not the
view taken by the secretary of one of the
ffiofll unions, for this person informed Car-
that the members considered they
had been grossly misled by the flamboyant
literature compiled by the Irrigation Commis-
sion and by the aforesaid Cormack. He also
spoke about Court proceedings and claims
for damages. Carmichael, who seems to
have tackled his job with proper seriousness,
has already gathered enough evidence to indi-
cate that somebody with a business-like head
on his neck is urgently needed to take hold
of this concern and the incidental p.n. Mean-
time, it seems about up to the -Government
to temporarily call off its picturesque spruik-
ers, and get ready, to make the work-a-day
facts approximate the official fiction. Ay the
beautiful illustrations on earth will not turn
that five millions-odd into anything but a
bill that must be paid. And you can’t do
the paying with pictures. You can only do
it with pumpkins and such grass things.

The Distressful History of J. Quigg.
These cases in which I.W.W. speakers who
object to military or naval service are being
prosecuted for sedition and the like, recall
the case of John Quigg, the grocer, who
opened a shop in a Melbourne suburb about
six years ago. Quigg was a convert
to I.W.W. principles of national non-
resistance, and logically carried them into his
business. Years of scraping had led to the
accumulation of £650 —and the shop was
stocked. The first customer was a woman
wearing a large apron. She priced several
articles but bought nothing, and as she
walked out Quigg noticed that a side of
bacon went with her. About half an
hour later 17 women carrying bags appeared,
and while Quigg was weighing out a pen-
n’orth of mixed-spice two whole cheeses took
wings. Casting a casual eye at the potted-
meat counter Quigg noticed that things had
been moving rapidly there, too. By the
time the women had given the glad news five
minutes’ run, a large crowd blocked the
thoroughfare and stock was passed out by
active small boys, while Quigg stood on the
counter and denounced the community for
being cohsumed by Greed. Asked why he
did not send for the police, Quigg replied
that police were dhe instruments of capi-
talistic Oppression.,, and people who had for-
gotten to pay for their supplies would reim-
burse him when they realised how essential
was social co-operation. “Bet yer life!”
remarked a bottle-o as he shouldered half a
sack of potatoes and put a jar of treacle in
his pocket. It was very tragic, and when
Quigg called upon a lawyer next day to pre-
pare his will he was la broken man, who
scoffed at the I.W.W. bitterly. _ Six-and-
giGHT asked him gently if he did not see
that the suburban grocery was a microcosm
of the nation—that the loot might easily be
on the wholesale scale. But Quigg only
wept salt tears of woe. Before the week
was out the Coroner investigated something
that had been fished from the Yarra.

The Commodities of Usury.
“Garnishor” on the law of it:
Last Thursday, in Sydney, judgment was given in
Equity in a suit brought by the executors of a Mrs.
Neale against an individual named Geoko.e Lees
Simpson, to recover £17,000, money advance .
Simpson, did not deny that he had received the
money, but he said that Mrs. Neale was a money-
lender within the meaning of the Act, and that,
since she was not registered as such, he (Simpson)
could not be compelled to repay. The Judge, after
saying things about Simpson which he later on said
ho regretted, was reluctantly forced to admit that
Simpson was justified in law. The case illustrates
the point that even the best laws can be twisted to
the worst uses.
The Act, which is copied from the English Money-
lenders Act of 1900, was passed “to grind the tooth
of usury, that it bite not too much.” The State
assumes that the moneylender is a necessary social
evil, and it defines a moneylender as “every person
whose business is that of moneylending, or who holds
himself out in any way as carrying on that busi-
ness.” It excludes pawnbrokers, Friendly Societies,
Benefit and Loan and Building ditto, bankers, insur-
ance cos., and in general any business not having for
its primary object the lending of money, which final
qualification is a saving one for solicitors, and so on.
Mrs. Neale was a Woman with large cash interests,
and so had a lot of investing to do. Instead of let-
ting a solicitor do it for her she took an office of her
own, attended to her own business affairs, and adver-
tised “Money Lent.” This advertisement caused, her
to fall in, because she thereby “held herself out” as
one who was carrying on the business of money-
lending.
The law, having decided that usurers are danger-
ous, compelled them to register themselves. It lays
down that a moneylender must operate under three
conditions: (1) He must register himself. (2) He
must trade in his registered name only. (3) He
must trade at his registered place of business only.
If he fails in the observance of any of these three
conditions the contract is void and illegal, and the
usurer cannot recover.
This Act was really passed to- control the usury
which was rampant in the West End of London.
Shylock, by adopting a chain of different aliases and
addresses, would first snare his borrower ; then, apply-
ing pressure, he would recommend the borrower to
himself under another name for relief, and so on till
the victim was bled white—often by blackmail. The
Act remedied that. If the usurer made a name for
himself as a blackmailer he had to abide by it.
Lots of semi-social circumstances formerly utilised by
Ikey Mo for doing business were cut off at the meter
by condition No. 3. It was no longer profitable to
lend to voung gentlemen flushed with wine and cards
at late hours of the night, as the loan, not having
been made at plaintiff’s “registered place of busi-
ness,” became unenforceable. The after-dinner loan
died a natural death. Sudden accommodation upon
racecourses or to momentarily-stricken City men at
exorbitant rates, and the thousand kindred instances
one can think of, ceased to be profitable.
Finally, if the Court thinks that any bargain made
under any circumstances with a “moneylender” is
one which is “harsh and unconscionable,” it will con-
demn or review it. The rule in these latter cases is
put thus by Lord Chancellor Loreburn, who says
that “a transaction may fall within this description
in many ways. It may do so because of the bor-
rower’s extreme necessity and helplessness, or
because of the relation in which he stands to the
lender, or because of his situation in other ways.
What the Court has to do in such circumstances is to
see whether in truth and fact and according to its
sense of justice the transaction was harsh and un-
conscionable.”
Mrs. Neale’s estate was not the victim of an
unjust law, but of an excellent one. The miscarriage
of justice was caused by two strokes of bad luc.;.
(1) By advertising “money lent” she became tech-
nically a “moneylender.” (2) She fell in with
Simpson.

The Teuton in Our Midst.
The Commissioner who examined into the
state of affairs at Liverpool Camp reported,
amongst other things :
The impropriety and inexpediency of the appoint-
ment in war time of a person of German name
and German parentage to a position in connection
with-the defence of the country are so apparent as to
call for no comment.
Theo reference was to Dr. Schlink, an
Australian of German origin, and one of
the Camp doctors. The evidence went to
show that he was that phenomenon,
a man of Teuton parentage and, at
the same time a loyal Australian. The
result has been an outpouring of rather
maudlin sympathy. A big medical society,
for example, resolved unanimously:
That this meeting has supreme confidence in Dr.
Schlink as a patriotic Australian and an efficient
medical practitioner, and expresses its deep sympathy
with him for the cowardly charges that have been
made against him.
Hard cases make hard laws, and the posi-
tion of Dr. Schlink is one more instance
of it. The microscope has been put on him
and he is seen to be unstained. Despite his
name, his family and his associations, he
contrives to be anti-German. But, because
something like a miracle has occurred here,
there is no reason to abuse those who justi-
fiably assumed that nature might perhaps
follow its ordinary course in his case. On
the contrary. The best service Dr. Schlink
could have done Australia at the beginning
of the war was to keep out of any military
position where he would be liable to pre-
judice recruiting or weaken the confidence
of the recruits in Defence administration.
Men of German parentage who have lived
all their lives wholly amongst Australians
and not in German communities may be
loyal; but the experience of Belgium,
France, and especially of Russia, goes to
show that the vast majority.of those who
have lived really German lives are eager
traitors. The retreating Russian army
has been suffering unspeakably from the
fact that Russian Poland is full of osten-
sibly good- Russians who are of German
descent, and whose sympathies, whether in
war or peace, are German. It has been
pointed out by the war-correspondents that
all through the recent advance on Warsaw,,
and beyond it, the Germans were kept in-,
formed of every petrol store, forge find,
food and cash depository in every district
which they reached. In no respect have
Germans who have adopted foreign nation-
alities omitted to help their country ot
origin during the present war. There is
consequently only one safe course tor
non-German countries to take m regard
to resident Gerriians, and that is to
bar them, if they are . so daring as
not to bar themselves. It is better that 19
loval but indiscreet Britishers with names
like Hans and Fritz, should have their
feelings thus hurt than that the life of one
Australian soldier should be endangered.
' ■» ' — 1
Defence-Minister Pearce last week made a
sorry reply to the report of Justice Rich
on the Liverpool training camp. He should
have confined himself to giving the nation a
pledge that the causes for complaint had
been removed, and that the persons respon-
sible for the defects had been scrapped.
Instead he committed himself to long, verbose
argument) designed to show that whatever
went wrong with the works the system was
not at fault. This is the invariable atti-
tude of the tribe of Tite Barnacle all over
the world. When anything goes wrong the
men on top show that if their plans had
been carried out by other people, the men
at the bottom would not have been inefficient
or wronged, and the public would have had
no cause for complaint. It is a fatuous posi-
tion to take up. The duty of the men on
top is to see that the machine they are
running works smoothly, whether it be a
post office, a railway station, an army or a
school. If the thing breaks down some-
where in the middle or the end, and letters
systematically go astray, or trains fall oft
the lines or the schoolmasters omit to give
instruction in arithmetic, then it must be
the funeral of the persons who run and are
responsible for the show. The fact is that
Pearce’s system has enjoyed an enforced
immunity from criticism, and the less fre-
quently he opens his mouth about it now,
and vigorously he sets himself to
the task of tidying-up, the better it will be
for his reputation and incidentally for the
Australian people.
The Yon Tirpitz department lias been mad-
dened by the British naval policy of with-
holding details concerning the “downing” of
German submarines. In only a few cases lias
the death of an enemy sub. been announced
at the time, and then it was usually a boat
with a pirate record that got an obituary
record. For instance, the Admiralty stated
in March that there was “good reason to
believe that the German submarine U 29 lias
been sunk with all hands.” Three months
later the Press Bureau was informed that
“the German submarine U 29 was sunk by
one of his Majesty’s ships.” Concerning
his lost subs, in general, Yon Tirpitz knows
nothing save that they set out with murder
in their hearts and never returned to their
base. It is the mystery that maddens him.
The silence of his pirates’ watery graves
makes the decorated admiral exceedingly
“wet.” Heaven only knows wliat his “wet-
ness” will lead to; but mortals can at least
guess. The sinking of the Arabic —a British
passenger ship bound for a neutral port—
was only a hint of German possibilities in
“frightfulness.” When the Kaiser’s immi-
nent propositions of an “honorable peace”
are received with derision, Yon Tirpitz will
probably send submarines out with instruc-
tions to destroy every vessel they can find in
neutral ports, in the German belief that the
civilised world will go down on its marrow-
bones and implore the Allies to desist from
angering the Lord God Hun any further.
■ « ——
The complexity of modern warfare is seem-
ingly beyond the comprehension of the
people who think that victories can still be
won with patriotic songs. The most useful
part of modern war is its tidying up. A
French general a while ago congratulated
the laundresses and told them they were
helping just as well as their husbands and
brothers who were fighting. Had they , been
within reach, he would no doubt have said
the same thing to the harness-makers, boot-
makers, clothing manufacturers and others
who are toiling to make good the wastage
of material. Australia can and should send
more men to the war. But every extra man
who goes is another wage to pay, another
body to clothe, another pair of feet to shoe.
This is a war of equipment, and in the face
of better equipment the most magnificent
courage must fall back—or down. The
grey, thousand-mile-long line of Russians,
now going sullenly back, league by league,
before the better-supplied Germans is a
gigantic witness to that truth. And it is
witness also to the folly of relying, as Aus-
tralia does, on the outside world for the
necessaries and safeguards of civilisation.
With almost the whole world ready and
eager to help him, the Bear must still go
back, because his hated enemies are between
him and the material he needs to fight with.
In face of the report made by the Aliens
Board after inquiry, _ Premier Massey’s
threat to introduce legislation for the pur-
pose of compelling the Council of the Uni-
versity College at Wellington (M.L.) to
fire Von Zedlitz, Professor of Modern
Languages, looks like a foolish surrender to
the worst kind of blither.. Either Von
Zedlitz is a suspect or he is not. If he is
reasonably s<T, his place is behind barbed
wire; if he is not, then to pursue him with
the boot because of his name is not Austra-
lian or British justice. A scholar-of Trinity,
Oxford. Von Zedlitz was imported to the
College gbout 12 years ago, and turned out
to be; a keen wit -who knew his job. Later he
married a Maorilander. The loud, scoffing
remarks he and Laby, now of Sydney Uni.,
used to make about the Maoriland Univer-
sity’s antiquated system of degree exami-
nations by absentees possibly account . in
some measure for the later outbreak against
him. A good many present “patriots” have
certainly been hurt by the shafts of satire
which lie has hurled at their lack of patri-
otism in times of peace.

Bigjohn Forrest barked across the House
of Reps, last week, during some conscription
argument: “You are all frightened of youf,
skins; afraid of losing votes!” It gives the
outside public a-sort of, shock to find, through
some such outburst as'" Bigjoiln’s, that the
professional politicians are still at their old
games. Because the so-called “war news
monopolises the space in the papers, the
reader hears practically nothing of the tor'
rents of rubbish that are being talked m the
inaccessible place where the politicians spend
their unwatched days. Hansard— that
most indecent of publications is studied and
filed in newspaper offices after various ar-
ticles and fugitive paragraphs have been
wearily written for a totally uninterested
public. And that’s about the end. of it. But,
stowed away in the House which no sane
human being wants to go near, the party
gabblers wrangle on through the weary days
and weeks, while the smoke of a burning
world goes up to the sky. .They may. ie
afraid of losing votes; but they certainly
don’t mind risking their country.
Last week Premier Peacock, of Victoria,
knuckled under to his alleged supporters in
tlm Conservative Corner and abandoned the
main plank in his election platform—the
Greater Melbourne Council Bill. - He ex-
cused his action on the grounds of war and
financial stress, and set out to deal with
that part of his measure which simply can-
not be postponed—the matter of tramway
control—by introducing a Bill to provide a
temporary, Government-appointed liust, ot
five persons, to manage the cable-tram sys-
tem for a year from next July- a ° d
hand it over to a Greater Melbourne Council.
Peacock’s real trouble was that he found
his proposed franchise for the new body
wasn’t democratic enough for the Baboi
opposition, and was too democratic for the
Tories in the House and in the City Coun-
cil • while the Corner party—mostly country
Conservatives—'wanted all the metropolitan
tramway systems nationalised, m the hope
that the profits therefrom would provide a
surplus for country works. This idea had
the cordial approval of all landlords cspeci-
ally those in the House of Fat, and as
Peacock wasn’t out for fight he fluttered
down.
Belgium being in high favor, the N. S.
Wales Government a few months ago
engaged a Belgian to come to Sydney as
director of the new Conservatonum of Music ,
and if a protest is not made quickly and
emphatically, it looks as though t e on
importation will be responsible for a dozen.
The new Director had not unpacked his
pyjamas before he entered into a. defence of
German music and announced that he did
not approve of British musical methods and
proposed to run the Conservatonum m the
“Continental” manner. The profession might
have made the best of this “tarnation that
Australia was going to be dosed with heavy
music entirely unsuited to its sunshine and
its spirit. But Mr. Verbrugghen follows
that up with a petition to the Government to
import for him his own quartette, presumably
to be teachers as well as professional
players. He has no idea yet what material
there is here; but without knowing it he
tells us that it would take five years to
produce such a quartette as he left behind
him. Mr. Verbrugghen’s quartette may
be a poor thing compared with what we
already have in Australia; and even if they
are masters of their instruments it by no
means follows that they have any capacity
for teaching. Yet on his mere say-so, and
before he has done a thing to prove himself,
the State is asked to pay for the importation
and presumably the maintenance of his
friends, whoever and whatever they may be.
The proper reply to Mr. Verbrugghen is
obvious. N. S. Wales has brought him in
to teach and train musicians, not to import
them. He is a manufacturer, not an
indenter. If he feels thus early either that
the material is not good enough or that he is
not good enough, his course is quite clear,
and it ought not to be necessary to more
plainly indicate it to him.

“Phil” on the swallowing of Jonah and the
fish without argument: —
\ page has been added to the history of heresy
laws by a decision of the British Court of Appeal.
This Court has ruled that a legacy of £lO,OOO to
the Secular Society was a valid bequest. Apparently
by way of obiter dicta the Court declared that dis-
cussion of such subjects as the divine government of
the world ought not in these days to be treated as con-
trary to public policy. This is the first time a
British Court has sanctioned testamentary bequest of
funds for the purpose of critically examining Chris-
tian dogma.
After English kings had for four centuries un-
successfully employed the rack and firestick to sup-
press “heresy and blasphemy,” an Act was passed in
the time of William 111. with the object of extir-
minating these things root and branch. That Act is
still in force, and as late as 1911 was employed. In
South Aus. eight years ago Chief Justice Way had a
legacy case to decide. William Jones, of Eagle-on-
the-Hill, had died leaving property worth £ll,OOO.
He had directed by his will that the annual
income from this should go to his son and daughter
for their lives and at the death of the last survivor
the estate was to pass to the Incorporated Body
of Freethinkers of S. Australia. While the litigation
was in progress it came out that the society had
long ceased to exist. Therefore the point at issue
was removed. Way, C.J., however, said that had the
society been in existence the Court would have re-
fused to recognise the legacy on the grounds tnat the
doctrines of a freethinking society were opposed “not
only to Christianity, but to all religion; and
although the law is tolerant of every form of belief
and the discussion of all questions relative to re-
ligion, trusts for purposes opposed to Christianity,
using the word in its broadest sense, are illegal.”
And so it, had been for over 200 years.
Lawyers will await with interest the full text of
the latest judgment, since, as there has been no
repeal of the old Act, it is difficult to see how the
Court could uphold the will. Perhaps their Honors
just smelt the Act and thiew it out of the window.

The Great International Bash.
Mile by mile the Bear is retreating into his
cave, desperately fighting, and leaving to the
Hun a fire-swept wilderness. Soon the rains
will come and after them the snow, and
then will the sum of human woe in
Central Europe be such as modern
Europe has not known before. It is
a fine thing, War! But admiration of
the monster is not for the generation that
sees it glutted with blood, drunk with the
lust of killing. That is one of the privi-
leges reserved for people who have, grown fat
and comfortable and careless in peace. Esti-
mates of the number of casualties suffered
by the contending armies in this mighty
struggle are published from time to time; but
no tally, even approximate to truth, can be
kept of the toll of War on civilian popula-
V Ol °? - crop of misery, disease and
oeath which flourishes in the atmosphere of
battle. What Poland, Galicia and Western
Kussia are like to-day cannot be imagined by
men and women at a distance. Perhaps the
tragedy is not to be fully realised even by the
people themselves in that dismal area, for,
mercifully, there is a limit to imagina-
tion, just as there is to physical endurance.
It is said that Germany has of late made
frequent attempts to bring Russia to dis-
cussion of peace. How much truth there is
in the various yarns relating to this sub-
ject can be only a matter for speculation
more_ or less idle. It is probable that
Kussia has been told that the olive branch
is ready; beyond that it is not wise to
believe anything. With Ivan quiescent the
position of the German would be greatly
improved. With the Russian armies still
active they must, even though greatly de-
pleted, be a terrible thorn in the Hun’s
side. A winter campaign on the enemy’s
territory can, in the nature of things, only
be looked forward to by the German with
horror. For despite his magnificent re-
source, splendid equipment and remarkable
powers of organisation, he is wasting; and
no one realises better than the Kaiser that
to-day, in spite of his successes in the east
and the iron front he maintains in the west,
he has won nothing except an ash-heap; that
Germany is- a vast hospital, with. rapidly
extending cemeteries; that even if victory
came her way in the end, Germany would
be a bleeding wreck. The Royal House of
Prussia knows more about war now than it
ever learned from the memoirs of Fred-
erick. Yet the Ilun is still a powerful
opponent, and the task of dragging him
down will last for many a day. But if the
Allies stand firmly together the brute cannot
escape the muzzle —and he knows it. During
the week he is understood to have assured
the United States that the campaign of
submarine murder will be modified. This
is rather hard luck for the officers of the
German navy, who had been getting in some
useful torpedo practice, and for the children
attending German schools, who had been
accustomed to get a holiday when a batch
<?f babies were blown up by the gallant
sailors of Von Tirpitz. On the other hand
it indicates that the Hun has amended his
policy of friehtfulness to such an extent as
to admit the unwisdom of mere indis-
criminate butchery. The explanation of
why he has done this is not clear, though
the climb-down, coming so soon after getting-
one in the eye at the Gulf of Riga, suggests
that old Von Tirpitz is publicly suspected
of being a lath painted to look like iron.
He may even be wearing false whiskers.
“Colonel” Bob Lowe, the old-time pro-
prietor of Harmston’s Circus, has been up
against his luck lately. For some months
ho has been in hospital at Yokohama, Japan,
and has had a leg amputated.

Potash and Perlmutter,” manufacturing
clothiers, opened for business at Sydney Cri-
terion on Saturday night and did a roaring
trade. The success is chiefly due to four
or the specialists. The other members of
the co. give adequate support; but the
Play is carried by Abe Potash, Mawruss
1 erlmutter, Mrs. Potash and Marks Pasin-
sky, the buyer. The quartette are so
sharply focussed that the other people of the
play merely provide a sort of blurred back-
ground. Ihe plot is slight, providing just
enough material for a series of frantic rows
between the two excitable partners, whose
Jewish kindness of heart makes them go bail
tor the handsome young Polish Jew clerk
when he is arrested by U.S. marshals on*
suspicion of being concerned in a police mur-
oer. 1 otash’s daughter having fallen in love
with Dads employee, and the valuable girl
dress-designer being in love with his music,
the partners stand to him, even though it
brings them to the brink of ruin. They nearly
go over the brink when the foolish Potash,
who has been informed of a supposed flaw in
the legal formalities, makes the clerk abscond
to Canada. However, the fugitive races back
oniwf a , cc< ?, rfl in time to save the

J-hen the handsome dress-designer bestows
herself in holy matrimony on Perlmutter.
«t SUch , things are only put in to round
ott the play. It is the partners’ business
quarrels and interviews that make it. Any
man who has ever seen two worried old col-
leagues nag away at each other until the
inevitable explosion, must appreciate the
I. and P. comedy. It is the essence of the
complicated insanity we know as commerce,
and is being distilled in real earnest in
thousands of offices every day.

“The Girl in the Taxi” is back at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, with Dorothy Brunton in
the star role _of Suzanne. Compared with
the capable girl who was imported to play
the part, and who only laid it down at the
insistent call of matrimony, the Australian
actress excels in every respect but the
capacity to suggest maturity. She does not
look like a. matron who has won an unde-
served prize for virtue, though she might
be a lovely flapper who has deceived some-
one into giving her an innocence certificate.
Jacqueline is played this time by a new-
comer, Miss Cecil Bradley. She presents
the broken walk, the dentifrice smile, the
arm waved briskly across the front of the
body—hall-marks of the ingenue of the
moment —and she has also a very nice voice.
Gwen Hughes looks beautiful as Rose
Charcot, but as an exponent of a life of
unbridled vice she fails to get there. Her
metier is to radiate an atmosphere pleasing
but at the same time strictly respectable.
Florence Vie, on the other hand, is uncon-

At this critical stage in the Empire’s his-
tory it pains The Bulletin to observe that
the S. M. Herald and Daily Telegraph are
in bitter conflict. Talking of the latest addi-
tion to the Tivoli’s programme the Tele-
graph says:—
“After the Play,” a powerful one-act drama,
adapted from the French, was presented on Satur-
day, and, Judging from the cordial appreciation with
which it was received, it should be a very popular
addition to the programme. In strict accordance
with the tabloid principle, everything is rigidly con-
densed,. and the incidents of the plot—all of their,
highly dramatic—follow one another in rapid
sequence.
The Herald, on the other hand, breaks her
umbrella over the head of the hussy :
“After the Play,” with the superfluous French
label “Adapted from the French,” was put on as a
dramatic novelty at the Tivoli on Saturdav. The
talent of Mr.. Cyril Mackay and Mr. Arthur Styan is
wasted in this coarse imitation of Sardou’s “Friends.”
The Bulletin would hate to say a word that
would be petrol to the flames; it therefore
merely remarks that “After the Play” is a
powerful one-act drama on Saturdays and a
poor thing with a superfluous French label on
Mondays, a coarse imitation of Sardou’s
“Friends” viewed from the stalls and a con-
densation of highly dramatic events following
in rapid sequence when seen from the dress-
circle. Among other performers Terry and
Lambert and Clemons and Dean, all from
U.S.A., and Rochez’s monkeys (don’t know
where they come from) are the least likely
to be sacked within the next few days,

Save for an untimely burst of merriment
that greeted Muriel Starr’s announcement of
her love for the professed criminal she was
trying to trap, the false situations of “Under
Lover” seemed to hold Saturday night’s audi-
ence at Melbourne Royal spell-bound, as a
rule. Public taste evidently is quite easy
to spell. . Three months ago “Under Cover”
happened in Sydney, and The Bulletin
divulged even more of its plot than is laid
bare in the advts., so there is only need to
remark that the final “surprise,” which this
journal noted aforetime, is a stultification
He? darned show, like the climax
of Seven Keys to Baldpate.” But in this
case it is not presented as a practical joke.
It is supposed, to be accepted as a serious
fact which entitles Miss Starr to remain in
love with C. A. Millward, the impossible
Sherlock Holmes of the proceedings. It
is really more absurd than the idea of a
Sherlock Holmes being able to carry on the
business of grafter-catcher for a long period
without revealing his identity to the grafters,
or the idea of his scheming to avoid being
trapped, when his sole purpose was to trap
the blackmailer who was trying to trap him.
Whv all. the mysterious manoeuvres of Sher-
lock, seeing that he couldn’t achieve his end
until after he was charged with smuggling?

“UNDER COVER"—A NOD YANK FELODRAMA
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The days are gone when Beauty Bright
Chose Virtue for her lover,
And gave delight the livelong night
To noisy gods above her.
The hero bold, the maiden chaste,
Her dad the cursey farmer —
Their sort of play has been replaced
By Noo Yank felodrama.
Where dudes inane in johnnie tones
Once stammered out excuses,
Policemen now put telephones
To crime-detecting uses ;
The comic-footman, the soubrette
And ruined village charmer,
Have left the track ; they are not met
In Noo Yank felodrama.
Cute specialists in social crimes
The managers are staging,
And “crook” with “crook” these latter times
A war of wits is waging;
Surprise-stienes flit across the plot
Like moving panorama,
And moral purpose matters not
In Noo Yank felodrama.
Once black was black, and plays were built
On primitive emotions;
Anon a lot of ink was spilt
In giving girlies notions.
The bedroom story had its vogue
In nightgown and pyjamas ;
But now they cater for the Rogue
With Noo Yank felodramas.
In “Under Cover” there’s a blend
Of “crook” and bedroom story,
A false surprise that marks the end
Deludes both Rad. and Tory.
I’ll take my virgin aunt to see,
And trust it may not harm her,
This sample of the third degree
In Noo Yank felodrama.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

“Babes in the Wood," at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s, has been slightly curtailed since the
first night, but it still contains more stun
than is quite enough for any panto-vauc.e-
mime to go on with. Besides, the Hebiew
comedians and others have quite recently been
doing the same sort of thing at another house.
The panto goes eight performances a week
and its allotted span of life, has not been
mentioned thus far. What it wants is a
rapid improvement in the war cables, so
that all good patriots will feel encouraged to
come along and help to count the Kaiser out.
At Melbourne King’s they are about to
shunt “The Squatter’s Daughter,” which
commercial-minded drama seems to have
grown thin and lost a lot of its native color
in the space of eight or nine years. On
Saturday the Bert Bailey. co. will get a
drama more to its liking in “Aot Under-
stood.” This is of a domestic character, and
is expected to appeal to the Sex that has the
more graceful understandings, but is the
least understood.

The entertainment known as “After the
Girl” detained The Bulletin at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) on Saturday. From the
inauspicious start in Paris to the frivolous
finish in London, there is hardly a moment
when the overwhelming comedy element
rises above the level of clowning. Old stunts
like the maladroit Cockney’s mismanage-
ment of the French language are rung in,
and even the hoary business of a heavy
father expressing his anger with table-
smashing emphasis is inserted to cadge
laughter and applause. Certainly,, dressing
and scenery are admirable, especially, the
concluding pub interior, where evervthing is
tidied up with drinks; but those features
only serve to accentuate the banality of a
protracted girl hunt on the part of an obese
Amurkan citizen and his French missus,
with an English johnny, his servant and
various sundries whipping in. The whole
thing is so preposterous that conjecture as
to the outcome of the story is soon swamped
by a strong feeling of sympathy for the
mummers who are obliged to carry on the
joke. Phil Smith is most worthy of public
consideration in this regard, for he has the
most arduous part and does so well. that
frequently he makes a burbling triviality
sound like humor. Bantock is a Yankee
manufacturer on stodgy British lines, with
John Bull whiskers to show that he has a
patriotic bias in the matter. Bantock’s
stout French missus is Maggie Moore, who
would look more suitable if the part was
twins. Still she tries, to make something of
the flimsy role, and if she doesn’t succeed
“THE BABES IN THE WOOD.” AT
