“Jean Sibi” at Will Dyson’s show
Will Dyson, who has his show of cartoons at Mel-
bourne Centreway, pictures Kultur in all its con-
glomerate agony of cannons and dead bodies, and
makes it look disgusting in many fine creations.
Threatening, chinless Kaisers supported by stout and
arrogant Generals symbolise the German AY ar Spirit
as Dyson sees it, and he pricks the bubble of its
pride and vanity with a hand that never fails and
a satire that never errs on the side of mercy. Pen,
pencil and brush are employed in the righteous job
of baiting Prussianism, and sometimes the artist
uses a light wash of water-color to add piquancy to
his conceptions. The Prehistoric Ancestor in No. 8
seems the better for being green, and Krupp in No.
29 gains in roandness and reality. by having his
complexion mentioned in light, anaemic hues. In-
dian ink unrelieved, however, is strong enough to
reveal Dyson’s opinion of War Materialism and Wai
itself, as savagely depicted in “Honor is Satisfied.
Elsewhere Dyson vigorously damns his old enemy,
Capitalism. The partisan representation is invariably
trenchant and delightful; but it shows base in-
gratitude to Capitalism, nevertheless. For, without
the existence of Capitalism, Dyson’s art might have
long sighed for a fetich to conquer. As things are,
it has lived on the Fat of the land for a long time
now. i.

Friar Tuck sings
about the Kaiser.
One of the new turns at Melbourne Tivoli
is a breezy novelty and a charming nature
study, so to speak. Stafford and Co. com-
prises Stafford in person, a plump lady of
limited importance, and a couple of dogs, the
pointer that stands in the centre of the little
sylvan scene when the curtain. rises being
the silent spirit of it. Stafford is a matter-
of-fact actor with a talent for whistling bird-
notes, and making noises like grindstones and
things. And his arrangement of his uncon-
ventional turn is artistic in all particulars,
not excluding the final incident of a dawg
knocking down the lady who falls back upon
Stafford. One so rarely gets an impression
of artistic effect from a plump lady falling
backwards that this exception is worth not-
ing. Marshall, who chalks caricatures with
his left hand, is a bright turn, though he
lasts a trifle too long. Annie Kent, “vivaci-
ous comedienne from America,” cuts very
little ice as compared with the returned Ida
Barr, who leaves vivacity out of her calcu-
lations. Ida is just massive and magnetic,
and her voice deserves something better than
ragged rhymes set to raggy tunes, one of the
melodies being built for a funeral march.
The rest of the bill is crowded with familiar
turns, good and otherwise. Some of the
otherwises, sad to say, are subjected _to
encores, which drive men out to get a drink
after the pubs are shut. Now that pubs
close at 9.80, the encore nuisance calls fox-
drastic legislation.
Two of the Tait entertainments have left
Melbourne to tour around. The Belgian Band
finished at the Auditorium last Tuesday, and
Louise Mack, late of the Athenaeum Hall, is
bound for the West. The chatty Woman
from the War opens at Her Majesty’s, Perth,
on the Hth. Meanwhile she is writing up a
diary book of her travels in this land of her
birth—which, she says, strikes her on her
return to it as being “rather like a big
picnic.”

Adelaide Tivoli, which H. D. Mclntosh
has been visiting, and which has Crawford
back as manager, has suddenly returned to
its own Variety line with Jack Cannot,

Follies. Meantime Brandon Cremer again

drama for farce, himself plays lead in the
venerable “Charley’s Aunt.” The King’s
has got right out of the song-and-dance
groove; the Maxwells cycling, Stevens bal-
ancing, Campbell juggling, and Merlyn in
an alleged conjuring turn, help to build up
a strong programme. Branscombe’s Dandies
(“orange” variety) are at the Town Hall
for a time. Violet Paine has uplifted her
fine voice at the Queen’s Hall in costume
recitals from a lot of operas.

From an Adelaide critic: —
W. J. Wadham, who used to paint Maoriland
scenery in a kind of partnership with A. Sinclair,
has come back to Adelaide with a fine bunch of
press-notices and a range of landscapes, many of them
already exhibited at various London galleries. His
one-man show, now open in the Society of Arts’
large room, has pictures from far-west Canada, Hol-
land, Loch Lomond, Tasmania and M.L., Central
and South Africa, and bits of green England. He
has an imposing list of Patrons (English fashion),
and of sales to various galleries. His art seems to
have mellowed since 20 years ago. The coloring is
less vivid. The Scotch and Dutch scenes are charm-
ing, and the whole collection, with two exceptions,
is priced with a modesty that other Australian artists
have outgrown. One of the exceptions is a curiosity
—a Welsh coast scene that seems outclassed by most
of its neighbors; yet it is the only one proudly
claiming to have been “exhibited on the line, Royal
Academy, 1913” ! As a foil to the perpetual land-
scape Mr. Wadham has hung a few figure-paintings
—mostly Early Victorian and quaint—that he has
picked up on his travels.

Mr. Smeaton: I am told that whisky once saved

Cordial!
Commonwealth Attorney-General W. M.
Hughes in a memorandum concerning the
War Census says :
It is now, unhappily, only too probable that this
dreadful war will continue for at least another year.
....Two things are certain —one, that we must con-
tinue to fight with every ounce of energy we
possess; the other, that we can do this only by a
complete organisation of all our resources.
Penalties for lying or evasion run up to
£5OO and 12 months on skilly. There is a
large chance to earn the free board and resi-
dence when you come to the question,
“What other occupation (if any) could you
undertake?” The papers must be filled in
and returned before September 15, or the
nearest date thereto that the postal arrange-
ments will permit.
The death of F. A. Badgery, M.L.A. for
Wollondilly (N.S.W.), closes a curious tri-
angle. It was he who refused to surrender
the party selection in favor of William
McMillan so that the latter might get a leg-
up to the leadership when Wade could be
shifted. McMillan then turned to Wil-
loughby and lost his party the seat to “Ted”
Larkin. That threw McMillan out of the
hunt for keeps. Larkin went to the war
and was killed, and now Badgery is dead.
He was a 63-year-old member of a pioneer
stock-breeding and stock-selling clan.
Two Victoria Crosses have just come
South Australia’s . way. Lance Hawker,
who gets one, is a grandson of George
Hawker, the founder of a large family, and
his widowed mother lives on Bungaree
station. His father (killed in the hunting
field) was a naval man who went into the
Boer war from Adelaide as a private sol-
dier, just for the fun of the thing, and nearly
a dozen uncles and cousins are busy in the
present trouble. Hawker’s Cross was for
fighting three German aeroplanes single-
handed. Only three months ago he got the
D.S.O. for bombing a Zeppelin shed at
Ghent. The other Cross belongs to Corporal
Cosgrove, of the Munster Fusiliers, for
going out single-handed and pulling down
wire entanglements on Gallipoli. There
seems to (be not much doubt that he is an
old Prince Alfred College boy who went
from Adelaide with the Light Horse.
The proposition of the N.S.W. Govern-
ment to take long-sentence prisoners from
other States as permanent boarders shows
what a grand thing it is to have earnest
financial reformers at the head of affairs.
As the Ma State (whim grabbed the wheat)
has a certain amdunf of hominy to spare
and some empty tcells, the commercial in-
stinct naturally suggests putting these
cotnmodities to profitable use.
Failing ability to raise a loan, the Ministry
might easily make a few bob a week by
running an inter-State penitentiary. There
are' all sorts of possibilities in the venture.
Pinchgut could be put to its historic use;
old Darlinghurst gaol might have its shabby
glories revived; Cockatoo Island regain
some of its ancient fame. Thus in quite
an unexpected way the birth-stain might be
turned to good, old stocks of leg-irons being
dragged out from dark places and rattled
before the world to show that we are no
new-chums at the business.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Avis” (from somewhere in Mesopotamia) :
Our fellows are laughing yet. We’re at
Basra, or Bussorah or Busrah (any old way
does), in what was until lately Turkish
Arabia, at the head of the Persian Gulf. The
show was run by Chaldeans, who have been
round about the neighborhood since Noah’s
time. For our benefit “Julius Caesar” was
put on —“Julius Caesar” in broken English,
most of it. It was funnier than “Charley’s
Aunt.” Ju. was portrayed in flowing robes,
black stockings, dancing shoes and a pince-
nez. When the followers of Mark Antony
and Brutus got to grips, the Chaldeans,
Syrians and Arabs put up a willing go and
kept at it till the prompter rushed on and
tore them apart. “Kipperlerlyy” was sung
as a finale. The audience included most of
the Australian Flying Corps.
The father of Flo Graupner died early
this year; and as the old man was 88 and
the youngest of four brothers, Dr. “Billy”
Maloney, M.H.R., an old friend of the
family, doubts the truth of a recently-
printed yarn that “the Dr. Carl Graupner
who boasts that he can provide the Huns
with the most murderous gas oil record
is an uncle of Flo Graupner.” So far as
the family in Australia knows, the last sur-
viving uncle died 30 years ago. Dr. Maloney
adds: “Poor Flo, it will be remembered,
broke down utterly in health years ago;
and her family has given her all the skill
that science could offer. Alas, without avail!
'Now the destruction of their factory by fire
has limited their resources; and an appeal
may be made presently to the public, : with
whom the little woman was so great a fav-
orite. Remembering how many she assisted
in her career, I feel sure when the appeal
is made it will be answered so that there
will be no further need to fear for her
future.”
“Nibbidard”: The effort of a corpse to
give a bad “study” a lift is recalled to my
mind by the young woman who took the
part of juvenile lead on the occasion. About
15 years ago “Held by the Enemy” was
being performed at the Standard Theatre,
Sydney. It is a civil-war play, in which
soldiers clank and swank to and likewise
fro, amid much giving of passwords and
countersigns. In one scene a dead hero is
carried through on a stretcher. The sentinel
whose lines were indispensable to the tran-
sit of the corpse with proper military pomp
was a shockingly bad study. As he stumbled
through his words, everyone on the stage
usually prompted him in turn. The climax
came when at last the corpse, stiffly com-
posed under a white sheet, lost his temper
at the delay in the play’s action, and hissed
out the final words required. The retort
they brought from the sentinel, who wanted
to know who was playing this blanky part,
anyhow, was the success of the evening.
E. J. Carroll, the Brisbane showman, is
the latest to feel hurt about it: —
Australia is getting a beautiful advertisement from
the old convict hulk Success! Out of curiosity I
went along, and the gentleman who was “shooting
the spruik,” as they say in U.S.A., was something
long to be remembered. Just to pepper up the show
a bit the owners have added the Kelly gang since I
saw it in Australia, and the dramatic manner in
which the spruiker explained that Ned Kelly’s mother
in her good-bye to him before his death on the scaf-
fold said: “Die like a Kelly, Ned,” was something
beautiful. It is time the Australian authorities took
a hand, and either sank the vessel or hauled it back
home and chained it up there. At present it is being
employed to give entirely wrong and injurious ideas
about Australia, in a country that has too many nefa-
rious ideas about us already.
Saker, the captain husband of Winnie
Volt, one of Williamson’s “Come Over
Here” girls, was killed at the Dardanelles
recently. Saker toured Maoriland with his
wife, and when war broke out just escaped
internment. He was travelling to Dunedin
from Ch’ch, and the police got him tangled
up with a more than commonly dangerous
German, with the result that he was
bumped into the cells. It was Colonel
Malone, since killed in Gallipoli, who per-
suaded the local police that Saker was true-
blue.
CAUSE AND EFFECT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Prom rural parts came Joseph Tupp
Upon his annual burst;
For twelve months he had hoarded up
His present gorgeous thirst;
In city bars he drank delight
Of battle with his beers each night.
At half-past nine his roving eye
A playhouse sign descried;
He bought a ticket, heav’d a sigh
And turned to go inside.
A man who wore a large cigar
Stepped forward Joseph’s way to bar.
“No good, you can’t go in,” he cried.
Tupp paused a while to blink.
“W’y no’?” he asked. “Because,” replied
The other, “you’re in drink.”
“O’ course I’m drunk, or else,” hic’d Joe
“I wouldn’t wanner see yo’ show!”
Victoria.

From an Australian nurse in England •
Not even now can the English drop their follies.
One of our own brown boys—the dearest, bravest old
growlers m the world—grinningly told me what hap-
pened to-day outside in the grounds. He was with
a couple of Marmaduke Percies—all three on
crutches. Arrived some ladies who had met the two
Marmaduke Percies before, and were very affable
Of course they introduced their friend, and in the
course of conversation his rating came under suspi-
cion. “Are you not an officah?” asked a lorgnette
with great frigidity. “No—a private,” he confessed ;
and a large abysmal silence descended. By degrees
he fell till he was addressed as “My poor man,” and
they wanted to know if he had enough tobacco. Yet
he is a Marmaduke Percy, too, and could buy and
sell the others, in spite of being clad in the sickening
habiliments of Billjim. But they were good chaps,
the others. They gritted their teeth and conducted
a masterly retreat.
AN UNSATISFACTORY BILLET.

“Eye-Witness,” from another field of
Blood:—•
The trouble and tumult began when a damsel of
interesting aspect was seen to precipitate herself out
of a doorway, and rush along Bourke-street in the
direction of Swanston-street, holding her head on,
apparently. People she passed in her wild career
noticed that her face was deadly pale, her eyes
were round with terror, and she was liberally blood-
stained.
In the course of 27 seconds a bare-headed man shot
out of the same doorway, and went in pursuit of
the maiden. He had what appeared to be a lethal
weapon in his hand, and his face was set like
cement.
The girl climbed on the dummy of a tram point-
ing north, still holding her head, as if that was her
one hope of retaining it. The man discovered her
there. The girl jumped off, and fled round the car.
The man dashed after her. Twice the girl ducked
and doubled, wilder horror in her eye, the blood-
stains in evidence, and her face ghastly in its pallor.
With a desperate swoop the man secured her.
What he might have done next one can only
conjecture, for at this moment a massive soldier,
bent on preventing homicide within the city limits,
stepped off the dummy, and smote the aggressive male
on the chin. The villain who had pursued her
backed 12 paces, falling all the way, and slid into
the gutter.
The girl, holding her head on with splendid reso-
lution, looked frantically right and left, and ran
up Swanston-street, going north in a splendid sprint.
The man in the gutter sat up, and shook off the
warrior, who was still bent on averting bloodshed.
“Go way, you damfool!’’ he wailed. “I’m a den-
tist. That’s girl’s escaped from my chair. She’s
got three of my instruments in her idiot mouth.”
Then the pursuit was resumed.
* * * *
The more or less blind appointment of a
Belgian as Director of the new Sydney Con-
servatorium and his announcement that he
doesn’t _ think much of British methods and
institutions and proposes to be “Continen-
tal,” recalls that many Britishers ar.e clamor-
ing for a national Protection sentiment in
music. They resurrect an anecdote that Isi-
dore de Lara tells about a young lady fiddler
who had been schooled at a local academy of
music -one of the endowed London acade-
mies. The girl came along to the critic
with a bit of Brahms, and when he asked
her to play an English composition she art-
lessly replied that she had nothing English
in her repertoire. This commonplace re-
minder of Britain’s shocking lack of patriot-
ism in the matter of music is moving a lot of
I reetraders to talk of protective action.
“Something ought to be done,” they say, to
encourage the local producer of sonatas and
things. The fact that the market is swamped
with foreign music and foreign teachers (who
naturally push the wares of their respective
countries) had occurred to these Freetrade
apostles years ago, but now for the first time
they propose to make a move. The war has
done thus much for a few Britishers. If it
laste a very long time they may begin to talk
of Protection for local manufactures in gen-
eral, and finally even the thickest-skulled
hreetrade jingo may get infected with a
vague suspicion that practical patriotism de-
mands a Protective tariff as well as compul-
sory military service.
“Private Jim” : I have been in camp three
weeks, and have seen more stoush than
many a soldier actually at the Front. The
goods are delivered every night in the open
air, and the combats are mostly produced by
some trouble or other about
Jam. Hitherto I had the
impression that Jam was a
harmless article of food;
but, with the abolition of the
wet canteen, the dry grocery
suddenly became more potent
than beer. The allowance
is one 21b. tin to 10 men,
which means 3.20 z. per man.
It seems incredible that such
a paltry ration should rouse
the fighting instincts of men,
but the fact is so well
assured that it is
now the firm belief
of most of us that
the rum served out
to the Australians
before landing at
Gallipoli was doped
with boiled cur-
rants.
INDISCRIMINATE YOUTH.
SHE: "The Boucher hoy is going round boasting of having kissed your wife after the last
waits.”
HE: “Nothing to brag about. Eve done it myself at his age.”
Cachit”: The layman confounds typhus
with what is generally known as typhoid.
The two are wide apart. Typhoid is water-
borne ; typhus, far more deadly, is handed
around by the louse. Servia has just been
ravaged by typhus, but is now said to be
clear, which means that the louse has been
stamped out. Typhus has been the dread of
every army since the hosts of the Pharaohs
went looting. It played old Harry with the
Romans, and while Napoleon’s armies were
sweeping across Europe typhus sent tens of
thousands more to Kingdom Come than did
tbe bayonet. It may be so yet in this war,
for the struggle is being carried on under
conditions favorable to its distribution. A
fighting army doesn’t get up in the morning
from clean sheets and have a bath and a
shave and comb its hair, nor does it brush
its clothes and put on fresh underwear and
change its socks. Instead, it springs hur-
riedly from its verminous straw, swallows
its breakfast and spends day and night in
the togs it put on a month before. It only
washes itself by instalments, and is a frowsy,
prehistoric-looking thing that sits down and
scratches itself like Adam’s grandfather.
The Germans have tackled. the louse with
regiments of barbers and gas and portable
machines for sterilising clothes, for they
know that to an army that is verminous—
as ali armies are—the arrival of the louse
with typhus aboard means death. Thev
a F e . ao . w claiming that Kultur has
absolutely conquered the insect. and
they feel so pleased about it that re-
cently flags were hoisted throughout the
land. Looked at without prejudice it seems
a better excuse than the torpedoing of a
passenger', ship with women and babies
aboard.

“Kodak”: In common with the rest of .
humanity, I am looking on through tear-
dimmed spectacles at the stupendous farce-
tragedy of all time—poor, old, muddled Ger-
mania Gott strafing everybody and trying
to mend the torn pants of History with
guncotton. J
, ‘‘Carfax’: So the Maoris have boon
ing a haka in their trench at Gallipoli, h ave '
they? Veil, they haven’t. Impossible I!
start with. The haka requires space and the
King s uniform left behind. People often talk
of haying witnessed the haka. Have thev u w
seen R performed in theatres, at exhibitions
at Rotorua and outside the country p A
Certainly they have ! But the sort of haka
the tourist consumes is about as much lite
the real thing as a Methodist bunrush is 1 ke
a cannibal feast. Asa matter of fact verv
few pakehas now living have sampled Hip
genuine article, and the Maori himself is ,
bit shy of touching it. Of all savage dances
it is, m its native state, the most awful The
next generation under ordinary ci'rcnm
stances will know nothing about it—unless
the Maoriland Government rises to the ocoa-
sion. A splendid opportunity is at hand or
wil be presently. Let the Maoris who come
back from the war be landed on a remote
part ot the coast, and let their women be in
the bush a couple of miles distant with urn,is
full of te kai. Then if everyone else boEd
and the transport put out to sea the subse
quent proceedings would be haka. A Maori
kinematograph operator of mature years
could be engaged in advance, and if he kept
his head and his handle moving there would
be embalmed a film of priceless value to
students in later years. The liaka of by-
gone days only reached its proper stage of
realism .when the fighting men of a tribe
were returning from a little affair. I saw
one once, and didn’t sleep for a week after.
Such a film as is here suggested might be
made the nucleus of a State picture-palace,
the income therefrom to go towards extin-
guishing the public debt. The audience?
Why, there would be a rush of tourists from
all parts of the stricken earth.
“T. Winkle” : The bishop was at the rail-
way station to give his benediction to an
imported vicar who with a wife was on bis
way to a northern diocese and solitude.
“Now,” said his Gaiters, “gocd-bye. Re-
member the place you are going to is not a
grand one. But it is healthy and comfort-
able. And _ whatever you do, don’t pretend
to be superior to the Australians, because —
well—because you are not. None of us
are. Now jump in! And don’t forget.”
Was this a sign of the Great Awakening or
of the spread of heresy?
* * * *
The earthly affairs of Hannah Maria
Neale, deceased, have been occupying a con-
siderable amount of the time of the Sydney
Equity Court during the last few months.
Various people borrowed large sums of
money from Mrs. Neale at various times,
and they have recently resisted attempts on
behalf of the directors of the estate to re-
cover the principal moneys and interest. The
borrowings are not denied ; but the borrowers
raise the point that Mrs. Neale carried on
business as a money-lender without having
registered herself, and that, therefore, the
agreements and securities made in respect
to the loans were illegal and void under the
Act. So the fate of very large sums of
money is balanced on a point of law, with
Costs drawing substantial dividends from
the proceedings all the time, and Equity
Judge Harvey making blistering remarks
about the people who raise such a defence.
The High Court will probably have the last
word.
THE MEMORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
hisses are like ripe fruit that will not keep.
Stored for a while, maybe, they somewhat mellow :
But kept on red lips they are apt to creep
To meet the parched mouth of some other fellow
Knowing these things, I did not let one waste,
But gathered twenty gross in happy haste.
Alas, that time and tide will wait for none!
The tram arrived, our homeward journey started ;
Our web of dreams in pleasant moonlight spun
Tore into shreds directly we had parted.
Our young romance was ended. All the same,
1 wish I could remember that girl’s name.

“Lancet” : Lister House, named after the
famous British surgeon, rises enormous on
the south-east corner Of Collins and Exhibi-
tion streets, Melbourne, for the housing of
Sawbones. Already there are Forty Th—-
Therapeutics in the Doctors’ Commons of
Collins-street East. I’d like to have
time to write a history of departed
Melbourne medicoes. What appalling
masses of ignorance (according to pre-
sent lights) would be unearthed! _ Our
great Fitzgerald said: “When Lister’s
antiseptic ideas came out, I pooh-poohed
them; but now I would be more cautious
even than Lister.” An eminent and amiable
Melbourne practitioner died of a broken
heart because of the ladies he had innocently
killed. Iiev. Bromby, as a committee-man of
Melbourne Hospital, once asked : “When are
the floors washed?” A surgeon: “Never.”
Bromby thought he was beautifully sarcas-
tic when he retorted: “Well, this is the first
time ever I heard anything against the vir-
tues of soap and water.” Even he lived to
learn that soap and water wouldn’t be of
any great service just there.
INCURABLE.
“That was hard hick of Chesty—outed
because of his feet!”
“Why, whatever was the matter with them?”
“Cold!”
Recently several brutes have written to
the newspapers to express the disgust of
patriotic men that women should attend
patriotic concerts without bringing their
knitting along. This idea that an obligation
is imposed upon every woman to keep her
hands full of socks, pyjamas and other war-
like necessities, in order to assist our forces
to victory, might easily be extended. Men
are just as capable of turning out knitted
goods as women if they like to study the
game; and when the nation is properly
organised on these lines the great dailies
will be able to publish something like this:
Mr. David Storey, who was the first speaker,
handed his knitting to Professor David as he ad-
vanced to the front of the platform. Exhibiting the
certificate of his rejection by the military authorities,
he made an impressive appeal for men, and still
more men, who, he declared, were wanted even as
urgently as loan moneys by the State Government.
Mr. Bruce Smith did not neglect work even while
he was speaking. A neat pile of finished sandbags
was the evidence of his industry, and he added two
more to the heap while he spoke. He pointed out
that the birth certificate which he had had neatly
sewn in the front of his waistcoat explained the
reason why he was saying “Go” instead of “Come.”
Mr. Willie Watt read a letter from Mr. Holman,
of Sydney, stating that his services as a recruiting
agent were required in Australia, and declared that,
on the eve of enlisting, that letter had decided him
to remain, in order to make recruiting- speeches and
pyjamas (loud cheers).
The curiosity which many present at the meeting
felt about the occupation of Dr. Arthur (who during
the other speeches sat upon Mr. Smith’s sandbags and
wrote with great haste) was satisfied when he ex-
plained that he was busy with his fifty-first letter to
the press offering to take charge of the organisation
of the whole of the available men of Australia on a
military basis.
At the conclusion of the meeting there was a
large muster of recruits, no fewer than 57 volunteers
offering to make pyjamas. .
Senator Millen, who, subsequent to the meeting,
provided our representative with instructive figures
making a comparison between the total losses in the
Battle of Waterloo and the number of men who have
already been provided with socks knitted by members
of the Senate and State Legislative Councils, ex-
pressed the opinion that the result of the meeting
was a triumph for the voluntary principle.
A person signing himself “H. Thompson”
has wept half a gallon or more into the
Argus about the iniquity of the income tax.
Thompson, it seems, has to get along
without work on a net income of £l6
per week, derived from property. He
is a widower with six children. His
sister-in-law has
to support her-
self and nine
children, two of
them in employ-
ment, and eight
of them over 16,
on £l5 weekly.
In view of these
harrowing cir-
cumstances
Thompson wants
to know how
he is going to
make both ends
meet after the
income tax has been
shorn from him. As
for his sister-in-law,
the outlook appears so
appalling that unless
somebody comes to the
rescue she will have
to get rid of her gar-
den e r. Apparently
Thompson is quite seri-
ous. At any rate, the
Argus is, because it
gives the wail pride of
place in a column de-
voted to “Hard Cases.”
Can nothing be done
for these afflicted
people? Say, a special
tax on all _ married
men under 23 in re-
ceipt of £2 weekly, the
proceeds to be applied
to the relief of the
plentiful Australian
Thompsons and their
frequent sisters-in-law.
How the bachelor
Thompson with £l6 a
week is going to face
the situation the Lord only knows. *
All the pluck is not in Flanders and Galli-
poli. About five o’clock one morning' last
week the watchman discovered a fire in the
flax and hemp cargo in the Union Go. s
Waimarino, lying at Victoria dock, Mel-
bourne. He rang up the fire brigade, and
a squad, with the chief officer and such of
the crew as were on board, tossed the bales
aside while the water hissed and spat on the
steel floor of the lower deck. Just under that
floor was a quantity of benzine and petrol
shipped at 'Frisco, and had the fumes there-
from become ignited every man would have
gone skyward. At six the danger was over,
but it was a crowded hour.
These are anxious times for great art
centres. Rome and Venice have their air
squadrons on the watch for the flying. Huns;
but the Holman Government’s housing set-
tlement at Kensington (N.S.W.), being
naked to the skies, lately took counsel and
changed its name in a night from Calico-
town to Stannumville. It is hoped that the
ruse will be successful.
HIS AMBITION.
THE AIRMAN (proudly): “Makes you feel
grand up here, doesn’t it? ‘Monarch of
all I survey,’ and that sort of thing."
THE NEWSPAPER MAN (still a bit scared):
“Lord, yes—makes you want the Earth,
doesn’t it?”
Maoriland’s Government statistician has
been digging into the cost of living in the
Loan Land, and finds that Wellington stands
out as easily the best town in which to get
quickly separated from cash. This is mainly
due to the awful rents demanded by P-
Enterprise. The cheapest place to live in
is Waihi, and that is entirely due to the low
rents common to mining towns; and the
low rents of mining towns are due partly to
the miner’s right, which reduces the value of
land to a few shillings a year, and partly to
the fact that the miner and the miner s
missus are expected to (and mostly do) live
in any old sort of hut. What passes for a
four-roomed house in Newcastle costs lig-
and in Broken Hill 10s., while in congested
Sydney the landlord extracts 18s.; for what
costs in Melbourne 14s. 6d. the Ballarat
miner pays 6s. Gd. (Ballarat rents, by the
way, are the cheapest in Australia) ; and
the Moonta miner gets a four-roomed humpy
for 7s. 6d., while in Adelaide the same
amount of inconveniences cost los. Accoid-
ing to Knibbs, Perth is the only Australian
capital city whose rents are not higher than
those of every other considerable town in the
same State. Kalgoorlie beats Perth by the
price of a beer and a half.
Who makes the best spy? A German
expert in the business, Carl Steiber, sup
plies the answer: “A born aristocrat with a
bad record and a worse reputation.” Accord-
ing to Hamil Grant, who lately wrote an
important book on the subject, the German
has no peculiar genius for spying; but-the
system has been drilled into him, so that he
now holds the world s
championship. “Among
the Germans the trade
is not accounted more
dishonorable than any
other,’’ Grant says.
On the other hand,
Napoleon, who used
spies largely, disliked
and despised them.
One of the secret-ser-
vice men of that era
left his bones some-
where in Tasmania —
Jorgen Jorgenson, the
subject of J. F.
Hogan’s book, “The
Convict King.” In the
course of his extraor-
dinary career, from
monarch of Iceland to
transported Tasmanian
prisoner, Jorgenson
was for a time on the
Continent in the em-
ploy of the British
Foreign Office. He
killed two birds with
the one stone. He not
only sent confiden-
tial reports about
“Boney” to the For-
eign Office, but sim-
ultaneously collected
material for a very
readable book of Euro-
pean travels. There
are six big volumes
of unpublished manu-
scripts in the British
Museum. Hogan has
built many a Bulle-
tin \ paragraph upon
them; but is a
lot of interesting matter still untouched.

“Sheila”: Kink O’Malley reminded the
Reps, the other day that in Germany, if a
man is a loafer, he is sent to a place where
he is made to work, his earnings being given
to his wife. Would it not be worth while to
print a few thousand posters explaining this
instead of throwing about objurgations from
Macbeth who (unlike McGoorty) is caviare
to the most of the good fighting material
which now decorates the exteriors of Aus-
tralian pubs? These chaps would then
thoroughly understand what the Menace
means.

“Southland”: Another long-distance cham-
pionship has been gained in Maoriland, and
the Domestic Service Gup is firmly held
at a place called Otapiri. Twenty-five years
ago a girl entered the service of a locaL
settler. She washed up until she got mar-
ried. Then her sister stepped along and
filled her shoes in the kitchen until she, too,
met the bonds. Thereupon sister No. 6
leaped into the breach and stayed in it tor
eight years, when she also went the way
of all sisters in that sedate household. Now
sister the fourth has entered upon the fifth
year of her novitiate; and, 10, some day the
bridegroom will come and she will say yea,
and sisters the sixth and even unto the
seventh will be called to the trysting-:place.

OPTIMISTIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The German troops are said to be far from opti-
mistic. —S. M. Herald.
Now, while our hopes go up and down,
And Ivan’s line is shaken,
Thank God, there is one Russian town
The Germans haven’t taken!
Though Warsaw fell in evil days,
Our friends antagonistic
(The Sydney Morning Herald saj's)
Are far from Optimistic.
With silver spires aiid golden domes
It stands against the dawning,
Its mat the snow that softly comes,
The grey clouds for an awning;
A town of fantasy and dreams,
A city realistic —•
For many later years, it seems,
I’ve dwelt in Optimistic.
Though brightest hopes are over all,
Though storm or battle rages;
The pessimistic cities fall,
In peace or war through ages ;
But I will take my oath (and back
My words in combat fistic),
No matter where the foes attack,
They won’t take Optimistic.
N.S.IV.
HENRY LAWSON.
THE ECONOMISTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Prime Minister, seconding Mr. Asquith’s
appeal, invites Australians to be systematically eco-
nomical while the war lasts. There are few of us
who are not lavish in at least one direction. The
people will recognise how much better it is to econo-
mise themselves than to have economy forced upon
them. —Melbourne daily.
I took it in my head to weigh
The butcher’s pound of steak to-day.
It went twelve ounces bare, and so
I up and let the fellow know.
I looked upon the milkman’s quart
And saw it was a trifle short;
I made the wretched man admit
That he had also watered it.
Likewise the baker’s products fail
At making good upon the scale.
The baker looked me in the eye;
He didn’t blush, he didn’t lie.
This paring is by every one
For patriotic motives done.
“Economy!” the leader cries,
And nobly they economise.
victoria.

GULLIVER AMONG THE POSTAL LILLIPUTIANS.
In the Postal Service there are 18,500 permanent and 11,500 temporary employees. The temporary employees drift on in this light and pleasant employ-
ment month after month, year after year Circumlocutory methods entail endless and costly delay. As a common instance, an application for a telephone
may be subjected to no less than 32 handlings. — R. McC. Anderson, appointed by the Government to advise on the business-management of the Post Office

REDUCE FLESH WITH SEA
LEAVES.
Any man or woman who is troubled with
a burden of excessive fat can easily reduce
to any desired amount by following the
simple, harmless plan of eating sea leaves
obtained from plants growing in the sea off
the coast of Frame. Natives of this part
of the coast readily get the plant itself, but
for the convenience of those not so fortu-
nately situated most chemists keep them in
compressed or tablet form under the chem-
ical name of salith leaves, and it taken
according to the simple directions that
accompany them will invariably reduce
flesh at the rate of two or three pounds a
week, and at the same time greatly improve
the general health. It is interesting to
note that when salith leaves are used the
skin remains firm without any tendency to
become flabby or wrinkled.*
PERSONAL
ITEMS
r l rickety, M.L.C., of X.S.W., who to-day
writes up 70 against his name, has been in
politics for 35 years. His health was very
doubtful when he joined the Stuart Ministry
in ’ s 5, Jonl; he has survived all his colleagues
except one—.George Reid. Dibbs, Copeland
and Abbott were robust men in those days,
but Trickett went to all the funerals.
Birth centenary of explorer John McDouall
Stuart: September 7. Stuart, born in Fife-
shire, arrived in S. Aus. in 1S39, accom-
panied the intrepid Sturt inland in 1844.
planted the Union Jack in the centre of
Australia in 1860, and crossed the continent
from the Southern to the Indian Ocean in
1861. He died in 1866 and was buried in
Kensal Green. Adelaide has a, marble monu-
ment of brave Stuart, and there is an alleged
Stuart statue on the Lands Department
building in Sydney.
General Bridges’ grave at Cairo has an
Australian canopy—it is in the shade of a
prickly-pear. However, it is only tempo-
rary; the body is being brought to Australia
for interment at Canberra.
“B.B.” : Who’s the tallest monument to his
country in the Australian army? For a start,
there’s H. E. K. Croaker, of Woodville
(N.S.W.), a mere 6ft. Sin. His weight is
13st. Bib., and he bears up under a burden
of 19 years.
Killed in action, Arthur Oakes, the only
son of Anglican Archdeacon Oakes, of
Bathurst (N.S.W.). Graduating ALA. at
Sydney University, the dead soldier joined
a big accountancy house in Sydney and sub-
sequently took over control of its Queens-
land business. When the war trumpets blew
he scrambled into the uniform of a light
horse trooper, and before leaving for the
Front was married. When the enemy sent
him west he was only 28.
Australian aviator Harry Hawker con-
tinues to gather embellishments in England,
the newest adornment being the record height
certificate for scaling, alone. 18.393 feet. The
effort must have well-nigh given him a
vision of Peter at the Gate. Another Aus-
tralian airman in the limelight in England
is lY W. Snook, who last month was
awarded a pilot certificate in the Royal Fly-
ing Corps at Hendon after only five weeks’
tuition. In peace times Snook is a motorist
who stirs the dust around Sydney.
The three monitors which were of such
assistance in the winter operations on the
coast of Flanders were the design of an
Australian, Yaldemar Tuxen, who also
supervised their construction. Tuxen is a
native of Ballarat and the holder of the
certificate of the Alelbourne Working Alen’s
College for marine engineering. The other
day he sent along certificates from Vicars,
Ltd., that the monitors were his job—this
in order to obtain his diploma from the
Working Alen’s College. When he got the
ticket in 1906 he was told that before he
could get the diploma he would have to
furnish proof of practical woik. And, of
course, to do the practical work lie had to
leave Australia.
The ousting of Dr. Barrett from his mili-
tary-service job in Egypt is a further knock
for Alelbourne’s musical autocracy. Barrett
was the one god in the Permanent Orchestra
scheme which is associated with the Lady
Xorthcote Fund, and Alarshail Hall was his
prophet. Or rice versa. When the "dicta-
torial doctor wont to Egypt to work won-
ders in military-hospital management,
Music was understood to he sighing for its
guardian’s return. Now Music may leave
off sighing. for ►Senator Pearce has
announced that its guardian may return to
Australia, as far as the Defence Department
is concerned. Ramsay Smith, the stiff-
hacked S. Australian doctor whom Kingston
imported years ago when a strike happened
at the Adelaide hospital, is relieved from
further service at tiie same time.

Bacteriologist Bull, director <>l tlm
germery at Melbourne University, has made
a frightful discovery—that, of all the sort
drinks now being rushed after 9.30 p.m. in
the absence of beer, the inadequate and
soapy-headed ginger-pop is the most likely to
be the feeding-ground of bacilli. Bull has
been a long time in bis job at the 1 niver-
sity, and throughout has always managed to
keep the public’s eye on the eager lookout
for the efficient control of epidemics, clean
beer-glasses, and so forth. One of the first
fieet of Melbourne motor-cars, by the way.
was run (at intervals) by Bull. It was a
steam-driven motor-buggy, with a boiler
under the seat and an unpleasant habit of
sizzling in unexpected places.

DEPUTY-
GOVERNOR
KELL, of the
Commonwealth
Bank.
It. G. Casey, of Goldsbrouglrs and other
money-making resorts, has lost a nephew at
the Front —Percy Peyton Jones, killed in
France. Peyton Jones the elder was a top-
rung engineer in the Victorian public ser-
vice, but he has been dead these many years
and his widow lives in England. Young
Jones received his military training there,,
and was a captain in the 7th Yorkshires
when Fate tore up his ticket. This branch
of the Jones family does not wear a hyphen,
but it never travels without its Peyton.
Ex-Governor Brassey having gone in the
Sunbeam to the Dardanelles to do Bed
Cross work, tin* Turk is understood to be-
taking special precautions to prevent him
falling off into Constantinople.
Kruse, who followed the very useful Cox
Taylor at Broadmeadows Camp, is now at
Flemington,. teaching the swaddy the diffi-
cult art of extracting trench-soil without
pain. This Major—“Gus”—is one of four
sons whom old Johann Kruse, the Haw-
thorn drug-compounder, left behind him as
a mark of confidence in his adopted country.
Jolmnn the Fiddler is the most distinguished
of these; Felix and Bertie still keep tin-
old druggery going at Burwoocl-road. Felix
is also known as something of a fiddler,
though lie finds more money in pills.
M hen a \ ie. Light Horse crowd of a year
or two back halted at midday during manoe-
uvres, news arrived that the officers’ tucker-
cart had lost itself in the hush. While the
Colonel led his brethren in prayer for tin-
driver, a camp hawker turned up with a
basket of cakes and the situation was saved.
The Colonel was busy carving with a pocket-
knife when an orderly dashed up and an-
nounced that Governor Fuller was in the
offing. Hastily dropping their confectionery
behind a tree the little party advanced to
rhe presence. Fuller expected some ham and
chicken, but it was explained that there was
nothing but cake; so he said he was very
fond of cake. The Colonel was Tivey, who
goes in command of a big lot of Victorians—
a stout, reddish man who saw blood in Africa
and can write D.S.O. after his name if he
wants to.

From ‘‘Bad Australian.” who helps to
keep the train which crosses the Andes from
shaking itself to death
My friend the good Australian was born where
the climate (days such havoc with the white man
that few people live after reaching 97. He was lazing
his life away in tropical Bolivia when news of the
war leaked through,
('limbing aboard a
mule, he tackled a 10-
days’ journey to the
railhead, and reached
it just in time for a
native ■ doctor to get
busy on the effects of
a heavy fall down a
hillside beneath the
mule. A fortnight
in bed, and the long
tri. p was continued
t o B u enos
Aires. There
he was told to
say his pray-
ers ; but, in-
stead, he stag-
gered aboard
one of the Nel-
son liners, and,
hanging on to
his vital spark,
got ashore in
the Cold Coun-
try. There a
surgeon took up the
job where the ♦borig-
inal vivisectionist had
left off. Since then a
Bananalander lias be-
come an officer, and
alongside my inkpot
there is a treasured
mud-stained note that
Bill sent me from a
hole in Flanders.
AUSTIN

THE SERGEANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’re sittin’ ’ome in comfort, reatlin’ papers by
the fireside,
Or you’re talkin' to your kids about the way the
Empire’s saved ;
When you’re praisin’ up your Kitcheners, your
Frenches an’ your Joff res,
Will you put your paper down and think just why
the men behaved ?
Was it through some bloomin' gen'ral pressin' buttons
from a distance,
lira win’ maps and flashin’ wireless, twistin’ regiments
like cogs,
Passim down his blank.v orders to a bookv schoolboy
captain,
Who hands 'em down the trendies like as we was
dirty dogs?
-Vo, it's the sergeant! the sergeant!
The poor, dam’ orderly-sergeant!
lie ain’t no blanky engineer.
Put ’e's jus’ the man for cogs!
Oil, 'e's up at early mornin, ere the bugler blows
reveille,
Cursin' men and kickin’ ’ammocks, knockin’ habits in
their head,
Gettin' growls from raw recruities who can see the
world’s agin ’em,
Settlin’ petty little squabbles till ’e’s blanky nearly
dead.
lie mustn’t worry captains ; if ’e does, the game is
harder.
He just swears, an’ kicks, and does it —then begins
it all agon ;
An’ bis sweatin’, tearin’ labor never figures in
despatches,
But when they starts the scrappin’, then, bv Gawd,
’e knows bis men !
Oh, it’s the sergeant! the sergeant!
The blanky, orderly-sergeant.
’hi don't figure in despatches.
Put '<; makes an’ uses men.
Von kin have your bloomin' heroes, iron dukes, and
dashin’ leaders,
But jest give ’em dirty rabble, and you'll see ’em ’it
the wall.
Its the man wlio makes recruities turn out trim and
decent Tommies
That pulls the army outer ’ell, or it ain’t pulled out
at all.
And ‘e's on the go till “lights out,’’ gettin’ cursed at
by liis captains,
Puttin’ action in bis pupils—gettin’ cursed by them
as well;
Damned on all sides from reveille till the “Last Post’’
sounds above 'im —
An’ the only place for sergeants in the end, of
course, is ’ell.
Oh, sergeant! Salute the sergeant!
The eursed-at orderly-sergean t !
You do your work in spite of all
And you finish up in ’ell.
There’s no ’eadlines in the papers when ’e's raked in
by the Devil,
But 'e’s useful, and Nick knows it, and ’e gets ’im on
the job
Drillin’ stokers, trainin’ tempters, puttin’ angels
through their paces,
And ’e's made a Grand Old Army, you kin bet your
bottom bob.
Vos, they’re winniiT all the battles, but old Nick gets
all the credit,
And the sergeant goes on worldn , buttin’ in like.any
ram;
lie don’t want your bloomin’ papers, coronets or
tinware medals—-
ll is team is workin, workiif, he doesn’t give a
damn.
Oh, a sergeant ! Just a sergeant!
A blanky orderly sergean t !
Tie’s just got to make the ai my,
Put nobody gives a damn!
EtiYl'T.
SARDINE.
A PRINCE'S SACRIFICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Ciown Prince’s sacrifices in the Arsronr.e have
been among the heaviest of the war .--Cable.
With scorn they, often speak of him,
The German Prince, a hero slim,
Whose vacant face to some implied
An aching emptiness inside.
To prove him great I will parade
The sacrifices he has made.
When Frenchmen fiercely held their ground,
And piled the Germans mound on mound,
This- Prince a burning spirit shone,
Bushing his mangled legions on.
One life a hero gives, but be
Ten thousand lives gave willingly !
We hear of noble men who give
Their all that England still may live;
lie gives his all in horrid war,
Then generously sends for more.
One spills his blood ; this hero will
Spill rivers, and be bleeding still.
Xav, little orphans, do not weep.
Ye widows, a good comfort keep !
Tom fathers will he buried soon,
Your husbands, dead, outstare the moon!
Yot yours to grieve in sore device —
This is the Prince's sacrifice!
VICTORIA.

This good Australian co. has done the right
thing during the past year and got in more
capital; and, seeing that shareholders were
allowed to subscribe at par, the operation
was not very painful. Hitherto, though the
capital has been steadily increased, it has —-
for many years, at any rate —only been
done out of reserves; and the growth did
not keep pace with the business. Hence in
1913 the total funds (capital and reserves
combined) amounted to only 105 p.c. of the
year’s net premiums; and that was dis-
tinctly on the small side. Since then 50.000
new £1 shares have been issued, and 10s.
called up on them; and 3s. per share was
also called on the previously issued 200,000
shares. Thus all shares (250,000) are now
paid to 10s. —all, that is, except a few lag-
gard hundreds. So the funds have been
raised to £183,897, which is equal to 135 p.c.
of the net premiums; and that, though
below the average of first-class cos., is a
fairly satisfactory proportion, especially in
view of the very safe class of business done.
The following table shows the co. from
another point of view:—
Ex- Loss Exnense
Premiums. Losses, penses. Rate. Rate

The funds in hand are thus sufficient to
meet claims almost equal to the aggregate
losses of the past five years ; and the position
is really stronger even than that, because to
meet losses in the current year there will
also be available the revenue of the year
less expenses. The loss rate, it will be noted,
is a good deal higher than it used to be; but
so far as aggregate profits are concerned,
this is more than counterbalanced by the
greatly increased turnover. There was a
time when the co., which then restricted
itself to N. S. Wales, confined itself almost
wholly to the very pick of risks. But that
class of business is restricted, and the co.
finds that, even if it can’t make the same
rate of profit out of second-class risks, it
cam at any rate make a profit that is well
worth having. Last year was notoriously
a very bad one for insurance offices; yet the
co. did so much better than ever it did
before that the more progressive policy has
amply justified itself. All the same, it is
doubtful whether the co. is wise in paying
out so much to shareholders. It needs a
larger lay-by to meet the rapid growth of its
risks; and it can't have its <nlded ginger-
bread and eat it too.

Taking all things into account, the result
for the year is astonishingly good. There
is a shrinkage in profit, certainly, compared
with the previous couple of years ; but re-
membering that a lot of wool was this year
shipped unsold after the agents had been
put to considerable expense, and remember-
ing also that property sales in S. Aus. have
been rare birds, one couldn’t have been
surprised if the profits had been £lO 000 less.
Even then they wouldn’t have shrunk more,
in proportion, than those of the Bank of
Adelaide, whose profit to the end of March
this year was £53.023, as against £71.946
in 1914 and £81,138 in 1913. But, though
the two concerns have a good deal in com-
mon, Elder, Smith’s have one big advantage
this year—they don’t balance till June, and
by the middle of August, when their audit
was completed, it was clear that the coming
season was going to be just about as good
as last season was bad. Therefore con-
tingencies for which the Bank had to pro-
vide Elder. Smith and Co. didn’t have to
think about. Even so the latter co. couldn’t
hove shown such a profit if it hadn’t been
tor the mountainous price of fat stock,
which has meant sweet commissions for the
auctioneers.

deposits. Elder, Smith’s give no inkling of
how they have borrowed ; and that is a most
important matter.

The past six months’ dividend on the ordi-
naries is at the rate of 10 p.c. p.a., with
a bonus of 6d. per share. It seems very im-
probable that the co. can go on doing this
sort of thing. How it managed to do so
well in the past six months is a bit of a
mystery. Anybody, of course, with an old
ark capable of bucketing across the deep
seas has been sure of fat earnings: there
are tales told of ships that have paid for
themselves in a couple of years—or would
pay for themselves in a couple of years if
they could keep going that long and earn
what they have been earning lately. Rut
there isn’t any visible reason why the inter-
state liners should be coining money. How-
ever, there’s the fact. And it must be the
war; and if the tide of war can carry pro-
fits up so high, the unpleasant suspicion
miist remain that the inevitable ebb of war
will presently carry them to a very low
lewel. In one respect the shipping cos. and
ad other cos. which show war profits are
likely to be badly hit. Wages boards and
arbitration courts have been steadily putting
up wages ; and in some businesses they will
be pushed up still higher on the strength of
war profits. Thus the Broken Hill cos. the
other day offered their men an extra shilling
a day all round, and the men refused it,
wanting a bigger cut out of the spelter
money some of the cos. are getting. These
profits will end suddenly. But the high
wages based on the exceptional profits won’t
end nearly so suddenly. Even the wages
fixed by the boards during the recent borrow-
ing boom will run on long after the bogus
prosperity induced by the borrowing has
mn out. Therefore the man who places
much dependence upon recent profits is liable
to be disappointed.

It is rather amazing to find that the turn-
over for the year was £2 260,498, as against
£2.065,905 in the previous 12 months. Cer-
tainly there was an increase of £360,000 in
the property sales; but that the business
apart from property sales should have fallen
only £160,000 is a great tribute to the prices
in the live-meat market, where this co. col-
lects a big part of its commissions. And so
far as one can see those prices are not going
to be very much lower for a long while to
come; which means that this co. can reason-
ably look forward to the prfesent fat earn-
ings from that branch for a year or two
more. As for property sales, it is danger-
ous to generalise. If all the world were wise
everybody would want to buy at the same
time. It is just because there are
probably as many fools as wise men
in the world that there are sales all the
time —sales made by the fools when it is a
good time to buy, sales made by the wise
men when it is a good time to sell. Still
one may doubt very much whether there are
going to be as many sales during the next
seven years as there have been in the past
seven ; and one may doubt still more whether
as good prices will be realised. From past
values there should at any rate be deducted
the capitalised sum of the extra taxation we
will have to carry on account of the war;
and that in itself is going to amount to
something pretty serious. Further, the
dearness of money will depress the price of
land, just as it must depress the price of
shares or any other security. These lower
prices, while they are likely to mean fewer
transactions, will also mean smaller com-
missions in that branch of the business.
Fortunately this shrinkage in property
values does not seriously affect or threaten
the assets of this co. Certainly it has free-
hold property, £126,500; but what little of
this is not represented by the co.’s very tine
city building and wool stores is in saleyards.
etc. Sundry debtors, which item apparently
covers all the advances, total only £43,828.
The co. in this line of business which can
go through a year of mixed difficulties such
as the farmers and squatters have had and
maintain a turnover of more than two
millions, while making advances totalling
less than £44,000 at any one time, must have
an astonishingly sound lot of clients, or its
management must have an astonishingly
persuasive way with it. Besides the free-
holds and the sundry debtors the co. has
£35,244 liquid, while its liabilities of one
sort and another —mostly, perhaps, cus-
tomers’ balances —total £43,115. The fol-
lowing shows how the various items have
moved :

Although the co. is in so easy a position
it proposes now to issue 20,000 shares at par
and at the same time declare a bonus
of 4s. which will pay for them. Present
holders will thus get one new share for
every five old ones. Though the 10 p.c. divi-
dend will then call for £12.000 a year, things
will have to slump horribly if the co.’s very
heavy-Weiglit Board can’t pay that rate com-
fortably. But it will have to do a lot better
than 10 p.c. to justify present market prices :
and whether it will continue to do a lot
better is mainly a question of continuous
good management.

The figures continue to show no improve-
ment ; and it is doubtful whether the position
is even as good as the table represents it to
be. The market evidently has very definite
views about it. According to the table,
which of course is based on the published
accounts, the capital is all there and £24.455
besides, after writing off goodwill, which
figures in this case as “Parliamentary rights
and concessions.” But the market, instead
or allowing that there are any reserves at
all, writes the 20s. shares down to about
V s - od. I lie bulk of the trouble arises from
electric competition. A hydraulic main may
travel a street. Obviously if the customers
in that street change over to electric lifts
the main becomes practically valueless. This
no doubt influences the market in appraising
the value of the shares. True, the market
doesn t usually take much notice —doesn’t
take nearly enough notice —of the value of
the assets which stand behind a co.’s shares.
Here is a cO. paying 8 p.c., yet its shares
are quoted at a discount of 7s. 6d. in the
£. There must be a grave doubt about the
future of a concern which shows such figures
as that.
The management is no doubt entitled to
say that it has nothing to do with the
market. Quite true, the market may be
manipulated. Some people may be bearing
it for their own purposes. The co.’s mis-
fortune is that if there are such people they
have such good material to work upon. For
the three years 1903-4-5 the average annual
net profit was £13,549; for the past 34
years it was £lO,OlO. Down to the last few
years repairs, replacements, etc., were ap-
parently paid for out of revenue and charged
to working account, and a plant renewal'
fund was steadily built up. To-day the addi-
tions to the renewal ftind are almost
balanced by drafts hpon it; Meantime,
while outgoings have steadily incteased, ahd
while Sydney has grown enormously, grbss
revehue shows rid improvement whatever.

There is something wrong. Shareholders
have been busy discussing whether they
should or should not join up with their com-
petitor, Standard Waygood. But if they
don’t do that, clearly something needs to be
done. Mere turning down of proposals for
amalgamation won’t suffice.

It looked as though the co. had four years
to go; but the State Government played a
card which didn’t come out of the pack, and
that made all the difference. In return for
the privilege of supplying water to town and
mines the co. was to maintain its works in
good condition and at the end of 28 years
hand them over to the State without com-
pensation. When all but about seven years
of the 28 had passed—in other words, when
the State was within seven years of getting
this co.’s undertaking for nothing—the Gov-
ernment proceeded to spend a third of a mil-
lion on another water scheme. Now the
directors of this co. say:—
Shareholders were advised in the last report that
the TJmberumberka scheme was completed, and that
the Govt, had made it compulsory for the mines and
town to take their supplies from the Vmberumberlca
reservoir.
If a private person had done this, after
entering into such an arrangement as the
State made with the co., there would be no
mincing matters—we should say that he was
guilty of sharp practice, if we did not actu-
ally call him a rogue. No doubt the Gov-
ernment had a complete right to build other
works and supply water. But to grant the
co. a concession to supply water to the town
and mines for 28 years on certain terms, and
then to come in and compel town and mines
to buy their requirements from somebody
else, looks so much like obtaining a water-
works by trick that the difference doesn’t
matter. This is assuming that the co. has
put the case properly in the foregoing suc-
cinct statement.
The co., besides the works, has £52,721 net
of liquid assets and about £30,000 of other
assets, including plant which may or may
not have to be handed over. There are
200,000 shares. It has been a wonderful
money-spinner, and its record has been none
too good; that is to say, it has used the
power the Parliament gave it to make exces-
sive profits out of the business. But while
that may take it out of our sympathy, it
doesn’t justify a Government in giving a
concession to a co. to do a certain thing on
certain terms, and then rendering the con-
cession valueless while exacting the terms.

ALUMINOFEIUUC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. Gd. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 3s. iid.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

The older the world gets the worse its chew-
ing machinery seems to be made, and the
more important is good chewing machinery
reckoned to be. The co.’s own statement
of its profits is of course accepted, though
they depend largely upon the valuation or
stock and book debts. These two items
represent practically all the capital, and
there is no way of testing whether they are
conservatively valued or not. All that can
he said is that the co. has been running
nearly 20 years, and on its record appears
to have been managed prudently. Unlike
most Maoriland cos., it gets along with no
debts worth mentioning; its total liabilities
on all accounts are under £l5O0 —a sub-
stantial reduction in the 12 months, due
partly to the equally satisfactory reduction
of book debts. # #

The New Burragorang, Yerranderie
(N.S.W.), reckons it is on a sound financial
footing. During the half-year 379 tons ore
were raised, but some of the best blocks
have been depleted. Some of the ore carries
silver-lead values up to £5O; but no divi-
dend has been declared on the 60,000 shares.
Profits are, however, helping to pay for
driving and sinking and other dead work.
At last New Langi Logan (Vic.), off its
No. 2 shaft, has got a bore up into wash.
Manager Toole reports a prospect of 6gr.
of gold, no water, and that the wash is the
right height for the Cathcart gutter. If so,
this seems to knock out the Langi South
claim that it has the Cathcart gold and
Cathcart wash off its intermediate level.
“Loss, £3965,” sums up six months’ work
at the old Johnson’s Reef, Bendigo. “Due
hank, £5131,” against a fixed deposit of
£1260 is another unpleasant item in the
accounts. All this is due to the old com-
plaint—low gold contents of ore and three
big mines to run. No. 1 mine is pretty well
given over to tributors. In No. 2 the work
of six levels and all the trying on the Garden
Gully and the Paddy’s Gully lines could not
keep the debits from mounting up. The
same luck struck the co. when prospecting
round the Collmann fault, but there is
always a chance of plum-pudding stone in
this part of the property, so John Veale
won’t be satisfied till he does some more
driving round this part of the old claim.
The co. not only lost one of its best sup-
porters in Isaac C. Dyason (George Lan-
sell’s old associate), but George Petrie’s
fall 2600 ft. down the Collmann and Tacelii
shaft robbed it last week of a plucky chair-
man.
Great Extended Tunnel, Wallialla (Vic.),
in its hunt after a new shoot of gold on the
northern extension of Cohen’s reef, has
driven its tunnel a respectable 2000 ft.
Three reefs have been cut, but they haven’t
as a neighbor a dyke such as hugged the
quartz in the Long Tunnel and accounted
for its wealth. Drives have been put in
north and south on all three reefs, but the
co. now goes back to the middle formation,
which lives on the contact of the slates and
the sandstone. The quartz broken so far is
poor stuff, but there are scores of worse
prospecting goes than the little Extended.
*- * * *
Although Great Southern Consols, Ruth-
erglen (Vic.), has mopped up £409.000
from calls and mine products, it has given
only £19,000 in dividends. Yet it moves on
majestically, after shedding its infusion of
Adelaide and Sydney blood. Pees now flow
into the already well-lined pockets of two
or the Staugliton family and an Armytage.
T. E. Thomas, one-time boss of the defunct
Great Southern, has taken command. After
an overhaul in March he shut down on pro-
ductive work to put in a south reef drive
to cut off the too abundapt water. This
spell stops the panelling of green wash with
its usual tally of calls and heavy costs.
When the present job is done he will reach
the drift at No. 3 shoot and can draw on a
fair-sized block of payable ground. Farther
along, off No. 4 shoot, Thomas is into a
narrow gut of the lead. At Rutherglen this
means that the gold has been swished out
into the stretches ahead. Off No. 5 rise
the wash is widening, and hope again sup-
ports the office boomster. What dirt is
exposed in the mine faces is expected to go
IBdwt. If Thomas gets that and has the
pluck to hold off panelling to allow the wash
to drain, he ought to churn out profits —till
ho bumps into the poor patch that sooner
or later gets all Rutherglen mines down.
Alfred Bennett drives Suffolk United, Ben-
digo, along very, very soberly. As soon as he
finds development work falling short lie gets
in tributors. These make about a living
wage, and yield him a small royalty to test
new ground. With the aid of an odd call he
pokes about from 1000 ft. to 1600 ft., and as
lie now gets dabs of 3dwt. stuff he concludes
there may be something doing shortly.
Money is still being spent by Maori-
landers in pursuit of oil. The latest spend
is at Kotuku, Westland, where a local syn-
dicate has collected a bit of cash, an expert
and some boring tools. Millions are sup-
posed to be close handy. Hope snrings
eternal, but so far all the oil that springs
in Maoriland hasn’t made glad the heart of
anyone.
Maoriland has often laughed at its. own
insanity of importing sulphur from distant
lands at great expense when it has enough
of the stuff in the back yard to supply the
needs of half the world. There were works
of a kind at White Island until they were
blown up and the workmen killed about a
year ago, but most of the sulphur obtained
there was carted half-way round the earth
to be treated. Now a new company is being
formed to risk its capital at White Island,
and there is also some talk of trying to do
something with the extensive deposits at
Rotorua.
Kingston Petroleum Territories, in 400
shares of £l.O each, has been promoted to
buy an oil search-license over five square
miles in the Hundred of Murrabinna (S.A.).
This license cost £5 55., being £1 per square
mile and ss. for a miner’s right dated De-
cember 22, 1014. An oil search license is
good for 12 months. Apparently, then, this
license will expire in four months, and if
no work has been done on the property it
cannot be renewed. For this title the pro-
moters are taking £l2OO in shares and £l2OO
in cash. The prospectus states that “Ex-
penses of flotation, brokerage, etc., will be
borne by the company,” so that for an
investment of a fiver and two half-crowns,
the promoters ask the public to pay them
£l2OO in cash and £l2OO in paper. The
prospectus states: “This company’s land is
near Mr. Whaley’s bore.” The land is six
miles or thereabouts from Whaley’s bore,
and there is nothing to show that it might
not just as well be 60.
During the first eight months of this year
13 mines at Charters Towers crushed 30,091
tons for £120,524. The best returns were:
Brilliant Extended, 9144 tons, £30,607;
Mills United, 7600 tons, £27,025; New
Brilliant Freehold, 4138 tons, £15,836;
Papuan, 1444 tons, £13,324; Brilliant
Deeps, 4506 tons, £12,127 ; Clarks, 534 tons,
£6300 ; Victory, 855 tons, £4512. The total
return from the whole field was 34,107 tons,
£148,504. Brilliant Extended, Mills United
and the New Brilliant Freehold are each
holding from £lO,OOO to £12,000 in cash.
Generally speaking, prospects in the deep-
est levels have been disappointing, but the
Extended, Deeps and Freehold are all
developing sections of the Brilliant reef
with slightly improving prospects. Charters
Towers has indeed been quite hopeful that
the Extended in No. 20 East has entered a
payable shoot.
Westonia is wild with delight over Edna
May’s bore-hole developments. The drill
was pointed to strike the reef midway
between its two well-known bulges at a dis-
tance of 69ft. and to get through it at
125 ft. What actually happened is this.
At 69ft. quartz veins of low value were met.
showing that the irregular hanging wall of
the ore body was being traversed. When
the drill got into the reef proper, good
values were struck, and the core showed
the ore to be well mineralised and abso-
lutely free milling. The drill has been in
quartz from 69ft. to 132 ft.. and at the time
this par. was written on the spot was still
in it. Allowing for the angle at which the
bore was put in, the distance between the
points mentioned is equal to an approximate
width of 17ft. Two points anxiously awaited
by shareholders are claimed to have been
settled by the bore: (1) That the underlie
of the reef is normal, i.e., the same between
Nos. 3 and 4 as between 2 and 3 levels;
and (2) that the reef shows no diminution
in width or value. The co. intends to put
in two more horizontal bores from the face
of the No. 4 level crosscut, and also a
depressed one to strike the reef at a vertical
depth of 400 ft. from the surface. Meantime
the water difficulty remains, but this is to
be overcome hv the installation of an elec-
tric pump with a lifting capacity of 50,000
gallons per hour. The shadow of the Edna s
water trouble hangs heavily over the
Deeps, as it is thought that the financial
resources of the co. will be insufficient to
cope with the expected heavy influx in
deeper ground, and at the same time sink
the shaft to the point where the reef is
expected to be struck. There are some
grounds for apprehension on this point; but
if the bore which the managing director has
recommended the Board to put down strikes
the reef and values are right, the co. will
have capital knocking at its door.
From Melbourne: —
The flag is hauled down at Westonia, one of the
latest Edna (W.A.) boom floats. It fluttered out of
Alec Peacock’s upper-storey window in April, but
was ragged by June, when it was shown that the
mine hadn't a golden thread. The co. started off
with a 60,000 share register, and was to part up
£3OOO to vendors. There were 22,000 paids and
38,000 cons., on which ss. was paid. After a wrestle
in the Courts over payment to the promoters, the co.
will yield 4s. 3M. in the ss. A sniffy shareholder
wasn’t satisfied with having his gamble at that low
cost, and put Peacock’s staff on the rack. Compare
the Bulla Bulling, another burst Westralia show.
There vendors sopped up £3500, brokers £563, and
the worker £665. Result: payment of 2s. 9d. in the
ss. accepted without a sniff by the sports who put
up the money for the gamble.
Barrackers for Edna May Central (W.A.)
were cheered the other day when an official
wire was received in Melbourne stating that
the ore disclosed in No. 2 winze, sunk from
the 75ft. level, was Bft. wide and worth over
3oz. Their optimism, however, was shat-
tered a few hours later by another wire
which contained the sorrowful correction
that the value was only 3Mwt. On this
poison-gas attack the bulls sneaked back to
their trenches.
* -X- * *
Some Melbourne money has been found
to test the Riverina South at Mulline
(W .A.). The show has two or three quartz
reefs, and in the last 17 years about £54,000
has been extracted, principally from one of
them. There is a small mill and cyanide
plant on the ground, but years of wear and
tear have about worn it out. The adjoin-
ing Riverina has a production of £46,000
to its credit, and was once sampled by 8.,
M. & Co. But they turned it down, on
account, it is said, of the reef being too
small for a co. of the tonnage they wanted
a mine for.
Ratrut Basin has arranged for the erec-
tion of its dredge in Siam. Ruwolt’s, the
Vic. builders, are under contract to ship the
pontoon in November or December, and it
is not likely that the war will push delivery
back much. On June 30 the co. had £9820
in hand and had paid £2049 on account of
the dredge; but as payments will have to
be made pretty frequently now, shilling calls
will start this month. There are 40.000
fully paids and 42,000 paid to 10s., with 10s.
callable, and 18,000 in reserve.

A Launceston (Tas.) syndicate has taken
an option over the Tasmanian Co.’s smelter.
It says it has a chemical process for treating
zinc ore.
Like one or two other disappointed Wes-
tralian mining cos., Brown Hill Extended
has cause to regret its connection with
Nigeria (Africa). Some time ago dazzling
accounts reached the Board of a nine square
mile silver-lead show and a four square mile
alluvial tin area, and it threw in some of
the co.’s money. It has been “backing
up” ever since, and last year the loss was
£lB3O. But for this the royalties obtained
from the tribute parties who for years past'
have been fossicking in the co.’s derelict Kal-
goorlie property would have just about
balanced the year’s expenditure.
One line from the report which induced
the Melbourne-floated Bulla Bulling Co. to
throw up its option on the Gibraltar leases
(W.A.) : “Prospecting demonstrates the en-
richments to be erratic, limited and uncer-
tain.” The Bulletin’s Westralian man
can say, “I told you so” if he feels like it.
From Woodlark Island (Papua) :
Sirce the Australian syndicate threw up its option
on the Woodlark King the owners have got out 30
tons of stone which they have crushed for 5120 z.
melted gold; concentrates and sands to come. An-
other lot of 45 tons, got from driving north, gave
90oz. The Federation has crushed 170 tons for an
average oi loz. 12dwt., and the Marua United has
200 tons ready, and ought to get 30dwt. a ton from
it. Of course, Woodlark would have felt better if the
B. H. Block 10 had taken over the Kulamadau ; but
it doesn’t reckon itself a back-number yet by any
means.
From the other man who examined the
lot: —-
“First Man” persists in claiming that the Edna
Central’s mining and milling costs are the lowest on
the Westonia field (W.A.). They are, on paper; but
the hollowness of the achievement lies in the fact
that, despite the small amount of work done, develop-
ment charges are almost as much as mining costs.
It is easy to debit development with legitimate
revenue expenditure, and thus show cheap mining
costs. As for £5 being the true value of Edna May
shares, if that is so the “First Man” is losing the
chance of his life. He should oversell a few.
* * ■ * *
“Oof Bird”: Britain’s latest territorial
acquisition, German South-West Africa, is
worth hanging on to. The output of dia-
monds began in 1908 with 39,400 carats,
and rose by leaps to 1,570,000 carats, or
about one-fourth of that of the South
African Union, in 1913. One mine, the
Pomona, produced over 600,000 carats dur-
ing the latter year, and did so with less
labor and plant than many British Africa
claims required to produce one-tenth of that
quantity. Through the Diamond Regie the
German Government held a big interest in
the field. Between 1911 and 1914 the South
African Union increased its annual output
by 271,500 carats, whereas German South-
West African sales went up by 405,400
carats. And there are good copner mines,
too; but I can’t talk figures under that head-
ing. I know, however, that the Otavi mine
alone has been giving over 50,000 tons a
year, worth about 13 p.c.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A Good Corn
Remover.
Those who suffer from corns and hard skin
on the soles of the feet w'll find it a great
boon to apply a little Atla Resin to the corn,
which, when applied to the corn or hard skin,
and a little covering of cotton wool on the
the top to prevent it sticking to the stocking,
will remove the corn in a day or two. The
Atla Resin seems to act by getting thor-
oughly into complete contact with the .corn,
and thus being able to penetrate it, and not
to act on the surface only. It is certainly
the latest cure for these troublesome and
painful structures, and, according to all re-
ports by those who have used it, one of the
best.* [2
“S. S. Severn”: Re Mr. Ilagelthorn’s talk
that “it is a matter for congratula-
tion that immigrants are arriving to
swell our attenuated population” (B.
12/8/’l5). May I give one instance
of these, “our own stock,” whom our poli-
ticians are assisting to Australia? lie was
a burly, able-bodied male, in robust health,
between 28 and 30 years of age, and, accord-
ing to his own statement, single, with m>
ties. Occupation, since arrival four months
before from England, cleaning boots and
knives for 15s. a week at a boarding-house
in Brisbane. He made no secret that he had
emigrated because he had heard that they
were going to try conscription in England.
An Australian girl working in the same
house said, “It was up to you to stay and
tight for your country,” to which he replied
that because the Colonials were fools enough
to do it, that was no reason why he should.
This man is only one of many who undoubt-
edly left England to be as far from the war
as possible,' and I want to know if it is a
fair thing that we, the wives and children
of good Australians who in many instances
have given up everything to tight for the
Empire, should be taxed in order to pay
“assisted passages” of that kind of immi-
grant ?
