A
WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, August 30, 1915.
My Dead Arini, —-
Long before nine o’clock on Saturday
morning a large assembly of citizens shiv-
ered on Wharf No. 7. The day was not
yet aired, and a biting westerly nipped the
noses of the company and tore its feathers
from their moorings. Mayor Richards and
his wife were amongst the first arrivals.
The female millinery wore the hurried ex-
pression of an early start from remote
suburbs. Mrs. Shaw, of Strathfield; the
Wilfred Dockers; the Misses Gother Mann
—the Greenwich ladies whose parent was
in the navy long ago-—and a sprinkling of
official salt got aboard the ferry as soon as
the clattering gangway touched the wharf.
The voyage to Cockatoo Dock ended with
an exhilarating argument between the
skipper and the police oil the jetty. The
ferry made a bee-line for the landing stage.
White cotton gloves waved it off. The Dis-
tinguished Persons’ launch hadn’t yet ar-
rived. After both parties had expressed
their unbiased opinion of one another, the
skipper backed out. Then the launch, which
had the Munro Ferguson and other valuable
cargo aboard, swished up. A reception com-
mittee, consisting of a very plump gentle-
man with a pair of pink cheeks and a genial
manner, stepped forward. This was King
Salter, general manager of the naval ship-
building works at Cockatoo Island.
The Torrens, a torpedo-boat destroyer,
slipped off its dry bed into the tide like a
large grey water-fowl. Lady Helen having
smashed a bottle of vintage against its
sharp nose, the new craft did the right thing
in the most graceful manner. Cheers from
those who had climbed the ofiicial platform
(fortified with white, pink or green tickets,
according to that station in life to which
Providence and local politics had called
them) to watch the event. Workmen, who
had considered well over every nut and bolt,
cheered lustily in reply.
The Ferguson made a stirring speech from
the depths of a gorgeous uniform hung with
ropes of silver. “There’s been only one dif-
ference between Lady Helen and myself in
25 years, and that was as to whether Clyde
or Belfast builds the best ships. Perhaps
avo’ll settle the matter by saying that before
long Australia will build as good craft as
either.” Frantic cheers from the company.
Her Ex. was turned out in dove grey,
rather paler in shade than the sides of the
Torrens. Her white ostrich plumes had a
wild argument with the westerly, but left
the dock intact. State Governor Strickland,
with General Finn in all his finery, attended
the christening. Naval Minister Jensen, fine
and upstanding, handed the boat over to
Australia with the Federal Government’s
kind regards.
Even the hearty appetite of local Society
flagged in the presence of a spread soon after
ten o’clock. Still, the Australian baby’s
baptism had to be celebrated, and the com-
pany did its best. The Censor was amongst
those present, so I wouldn’t for worlds tell
you the size or the horse-power of the
craft or of the crafts that are building,
although Mr. Ivnibbs has printed it all and
when yon buy the information they don’t
even ask you if your name is Yon Tirpitz.
Aide Fane, in his best feathered hat,
dogged the G.-G.’s heels. Australia has put
more flesh on the big frame of the convales-
cent soldier, who means to have another cut
again soon.
During the recent visit of the Fergusons,
his Ex. again put up at the Warrigal Club —•
where men talk beef and wool —and she
climbed the Queen’s Club stairs to change
her hat.
The new Anglican Bishop of Goulburn,
Dr. Radford, was the leader of the High
Church party in the Synod, and there were
wigs on St. Andrew’s lawn when parsons
Boyce, Archdall and other Low Churchmen
got into action. Bishop Radford, who has
been in charge of St. Paul’s College within
the University, was feasted by Governor
Strickland before he put on his new muslin
sleeves.
The Rajah of Pudukota and the new Mrs.
I’udukota honeymooned at the Carrington,
Katoomba. before the lady packed up her
sheaves of diamonds for India. They got
their tremendous baggage safely abroad the
Sonoma, and make a wide detour before
reaching the ’groom’s little kingdom. On the
same boat were Mrs. Holman and Mrs. Hugh
Mclntosh, who unpack their Saratogas in the
IT.S.A.
Governor Strickland and the Victorian
Excellency Stanley have settled down again
after their recent scamper through N. S.
Wales. So have the small towns on which
two Governors threw their brilliant light at
once. Nymagee had never been dazzled
by even one Governor before; so when
a couple burst in, it did something
tremendous. Summoning its local school-
children it fired a royal salute. As no
ordnance was available a heavy fusillade of
Chinese crackers marked the splendor of the
occasion.
Orange, which has never forgotten that it
was well forward in the race for the Federal
capital, poured its old grievance into the
vice-regal ears. While the two Governors
lingered half way up Canoblas to admire the
view, local Mayor MoNeilly, Town-Clerk
Mulholland, Shire-Engineer Beaton, and the
resident gentry recalled the tale of a capital
that wandered from this Band of Promise
into a wilderness of dry bones and often
drier rivers. These tears shed, the party
climbed higher before descending to motor-
level to be whisked back to Orange, whose
waiting lieges were so numerous that the
Town Hall overflowed into the street, and
vice-regal oratory had to be turned on from
the balcony.
To Governor Stanley’s collection of stories
is now added one about the number of
bruises he gathered motoring on the Cadia-
road, which includes about 15 miles of
painful surprises. He can also estimate the
approximate amount of red dust he wiped
out of his eye on the way to Mount Boppy.
The tour was threaded with anecdotes of
Henry Parkes and George Reid. The
raconteur was Sydney Smith, who was the
first Minister of Agriculture ever N. S.
Wales had, and thus was crowned Pumpkin
I. in the lobbies 25 years ago. These days
he is the local director of the English com-
pany which tliusfar to its grief owns the
Nymagee copper mine.
A.D.C. Conant, who came this way with
the Victorian sub-royalty, stopped a" bullet
with his instep during the uproar at Ypres.
There is an interesting limp in consequence;
but it didn’t prevent the invalid giving a
good account of his wind and muscle up the
slopes of Canoblas.
Captain Segrave, the skipper of a con-
verted cruiser” which happened to be some-
where handy, has lost his heart. He went
with the Strickland party out West, and
helped them to find suitable adjectives for
Blue Mountain gorges. He is a linguist of
note, and had he been a talkative sailor
might have replied in the language of the
Allies and the gutturals of the enemy to the
Australian nights’ entertainment provided
by Syd. Smith.
Chaplain Rowe’s story of Gallipoli was
sandwiched between the best lantern slides
we’ve seen for a long while. There was no
confusion on the part of the operator, either.
For once a lecturer was not maddened by
seeing his best slides fall upside-down into
the wrong part of the story. The Town Hall
was so full at 7.30 that the sale of tickets
was stopped. A patient overflow hung on
the bleak front steps and scrambled for a
foothold on the threshold till Authority re-
lented, and allowed some of it to surge round
the rear of the platform. Marsh Little
sang a couple of war ditties, but his voice
wasn’t large enough to worm its way to tin*
back of the hall. Bentley’s band, which is
on the noisy side, blared forth appropriate
tunes and played the sub-regal party into its
seats with the usual anthem.
Private Downes, the lean, well-set-up
youngster who left Queensland to share in
the deathless fame of the third Brigade, got
a cyclonic storm of applause. Behind
shaded glasses this brave youth hides now-
sightless eyes. The naive and modest man-
ner in which he briefly recorded all he saw
of the first wild rush up the Gallipoli hills
set the audience aflame. He stopped in a
bail of shrapnel to help a fallen mate to
shelter; then, with a boyish, deprecating-
laugh—“And that’s all I saw of Gallipoli.”
The boy’s mother, who was in the hall, was
the proudest woman in Sydney at that
moment.
The sportsmen's affair in the Town Hall on
Sept. 4 is after £lOOO for the Australia Day
fund. Hon. organiser Eslick is especially
keen about the Queen of Beauty competi-
tion, which occurs at the fancy-dress ball in
the evening. The Elizabethan bazaar, dog-
show and general uproar will be opened by
Governor Strickland at 2.30.
The name of Archie Scot Skirviug is added
to the toll of the Dardanelles. The whole
family is now on the other side, for Scot
Skirviug, senior, must be nearing the Dover
cliffs on his way to work for the R.A.M.C.
The picturesque mother, whose titian locks
are as lovely as ever they were, was a nurse
in a Sydney hospital when the stern-looking
Scotch surgeon, who had been a sailor, fell
in love with her. She went to England to
be near her two sons when the drums began
1o beat. Father was the last to leave Syd-
ney, and the bad news will be waiting for
him at the end of his journey. The other
son is somewhere at the Front.
Three sons of the late R. E. O’Connor, one
of the first Judges of the High Court, are
doing their bit for Us. .Mother is in Ireland
having a look at son Alan, who is in the 3rd
Leinsters. Max is again on the slopes of
Gallipoli, after recovering from a wound early
in that historic struggle. And Dick was
ready to leave Maoriland with the Dominion
troops some weeks ago.
The Commercial Travellers’ concert in the
Town Hall was for the usual cause —the
bandaged kangaroo. The auction by A. R.
Cooke of cushions, curios and oddments in-
flated the takings moderately. But the
average concert audience yawns nowadays
at too much auction, and stealthily inspects
its watch.
Peter Dawson was the vocal star of the
evening; and his chest looked about two
sizes larger than usual owing to the billow
in his starched shirt-front. Annetta
George, the plump lady who is Mrs.
Peter, was not present owing to bronchitis,
so hubby took extra encores to swell the
programme. Organ interludes arc usually
regarded as first aids to low whisperings
between Him and Her at these affairs; but
Lilian Frost’s silvery way with the big
instrument was one of the charms of the
evening. Esther Kahn, the small, dark-eyed
composer of a “Barcarolle” and “Old French
Minuet,” was in the audience and had the
joy of hearing both melodies insistently
applauded. So organist Lilian gave the
audience another sample of local composition
by the same music-maker.
At the Allies’ Day committee meeting the
other afternoon, various privateering notions
were thrashed out. The veteran T. A.
Dibbs was warmly in favor of a Queen of the
Carnival. As any lady who is so disposed
can barrack for herself to the tune of £5O
worth of voting papers, there’s money in
the idea. Maoriland has tapped this allu-
vial vein with enormous success for patri-
otic purposes. The Day is fixed for Novem-
ber 19.
The Symphony orchestral concert filled
the Conservatorium with the sighs and
groans of fiddles and ’cellos on Saturday
afternoon. Slapoffski conducted with his
hands, and when necessary hushed the ’cellos
into soft growls by hurriedly patting the
atmosphere. But no amount of pawing or
patting from the stage would stay the creak-
ing and the slamming of doors which admit
of no fastening from the inside. Director
Yerbrugghen crept into his seat quietly,
almost furtively—maybe in terror lest some
angry music-teacher should dart out and
slay him for proposing to import the mem-
bers of the Yerbrugghen Quartette at the
State’s expense. The programme misin-
formed those present —a frequent habit with
programmes. So the huge and gloomy Sym-
phonic Patlietique, by Tschaikowsky, fin-
ished the afternoon’s service. It was pre-
ceded by a delightful rendering of Bach’s
“Air for the G string.” Mrs. Slap, revelled
in the perfect acoustics of the official cave
of harmony. The soprano attacked the
florid aria, “Ocean, thou mighty monster,”
in her best form.
A garden fete in aid of the Wounded Sol-
diers’ Fund, held at The Gunyah, Neutral
Bay, on Saturday, was the rendezvous of
some interesting old-timers and their
descendants. The sale was opened by Mrs.
Granville Ryrie, wife of Colonel Eyrie, who
represents North Sydney in the House of
Reps. In the days when Responsible Gov-
ernment began, what is now North Sydney
was St. Leonards, and among those who
gathered round the Oldest Inhabitant on
Saturday were Messrs. W. R. Sayers and
Frank Farnell, the eldest sons of the first
and second representatives of the district.
Australia is growing up. The first time
an expedition left Sydney to help the Empire
it went to the Soudan and brought back a
donkey as its trophy of war. There were
patriotic funds in those days, too, and “A
Little Boy from Manly” won immortality by
sending in a shilling. “Hop,” then just fresh
to these parts, seized the Little Boy to typify
Young Australia; and, the real youngster
being unknown, Ernest Laurence, who was
a little hoy of Manly, served as the model.
Now, when the flour-hag has been spilled on
his head, the model appears, more or less, in
the present great trouble, as the law adviser
of that Julius Blau with whom vai’ions
people think the Commonwealth Government
should have got to holts.
Mrs. Phipps, wife of the Mosman doctor
who is on service in Egypt, was having a
look at the Shamrock when the last news
came this way.
Young Wise, a son of Bernhard, Iv.C.,
now representing us in London, hasn’t shaken
off his recent bout with appendicitis too
well. He left India to convalesce in Eng-
land, and has been doing it by slow stages in
a nursing home.
The Fresh Air League, which sends wan-
faced women and babes to the country in
summer, is trying to attract your notice with
a garden fete. The John Hay dame has
placed her unusual flower beds at Crow’s
Nest at the service of the committee on
Sept. 2.
Artist Minns, the man who can draw for
us Black Sister Mary as no one else can do
it, may be expected to mount The Bulletin
stairs any day. lie was to have been here
early in August by the Benalla. A tire in
the hunkers, however, has lengthened the
trip from London. Minns comes to collect
more types of black brother on this peaceful
side of the maelstrom.
Journalist Quinn and his wife and daugh-
ter were moving across U.S.A. when they
sent a line this way. He is going to tear
up his N.S.W. agency business in ’Frisco to
plant them in A"York.
Queenie Macdonald, of the squatting Want-
abadgery family, is wearing the cap and
apron in a London hospital. She is an extra
voluntary pro., and not taking permanently
to the Nightingale business.
Dr. Arthur’s efforts to lead feminine
fashion into the stern path of thrift are
fainting away before the new spring goods.
In the tiliuy voiles they’re not extravagant
gear. Woman will steadily refuse to get
into a universal uniform so long as she
knows that there are a few men left to be
cheered by her best frock. The past winter
has shown that economy—within reason;
always within reason —has been stoutly prac-
tised. But the dreary pessimist could serve
no good purpose by dressing Woman in a
bolster of sugar-hags. What Australian
women are doing for the Red Cross shouts
itself from the housetops ; and no doubt the
Recording Angel is putting even the rob-
beries she has organised on the credit side
of her account in the eternal ledger. The
small debit —Spring fashions —won’t be
noticed alongside the vast bulk of her Good
Works.
The Army Service Corps committee will
be in charge of the tea-room at the Chamber
of Commerce exhibition, Circular Quay, on
Sept. 4. The Commerce magnates authorise
the A.S.C. to give the profits to the Corps’
comforts fund. The lion. sec. is Miss Hilda
Caro.
The Sheepskin Vest Committee had an
audience which was lean at least in num-
bers to listen to its first annual report. Bui
in person it was mostly plump and opti-
mistic, and it filled the Town Hall vestibule
with a cheery tale of great faith. Its cash
in hand is fairly substantial, but there’s a
bitter winter ahead, so it is after your last
7s. all the same. Chief Justice Cullen took
the chair, while John Mackay, chairman of
the executive, reminded those present and
absent that 20,000 vests have been pro-
mised to George Reid to hand over to the
British authorities as soon as possible. A
large and hearty speaker, A. 11. Moore, waved
aside this mere handful, and suggested
250,000 as a more suitable, gift from wealthy
N. S. Wales. After that. F. E. Winch-
combe. ex-Mayor Cocks, and others had
their little talk.
Mrs. Finlay, who used to be Ilaidee do
Lissa. has returned from California. The
new husband was acquired in U.S., and he
threatens to take very kindly to his wife's
gum trees.
Women writhed in a dense mass in the
Town Hall vestibule on a warm afternoon
last week. The “Women’s Commonwealth
Patriotic Association” was going to discuss
the Teuton host. Every patriot was fiercely
resolved that she’d never buy enemy goods
again so long as she handled Dad’s salary.
But if you had said, “Very well; then it
doesn’t matter, does it, if we block these
goods with a 100 per cent, duty?” there
would probably have been screams of pro-
test from the “Liberal” ladies. Mayor
Richards kept the peace at this corroboree.
The best speaker, in an academic sense,
was Dr. Mary Booth. Miss Annie Golding,
the dominant Trades Hall lady, offered prac-
tical advice to the audience; but, mostly,
meaningless platitudes fell like hail from the
platform.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, August 30, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Cliveden buzzed on Friday night with a
carefully-selected carnival. Nearly a thou-
sand tickets were sold at ss. each ; but the
sellers were _ very careful, my dear. Rank
outsiders, y'know, would have rushed a
chance of getting amongst the prize
merinoes for a mere five hob. But they were
not given a chance.
The affair proved to be an extreme un-
dress function. Some nice girls from out-
side, knowing the affair to he merely a glori-
fied bazaar, turned up in neat tailor-mades
and outdoor gear. They were eventually
found in a miserable bunch on a large lounge-
sofa, completely subdued and crushed. The
Cliveden dames stared at them coldly. “Who
are these girls—not in evening dress’?” was
the chill, unspoken curse they laid upon the
sufferers. All those in the know were in the
most radiant undress. The order was for
shoulder-straps and no spurs; fat, naked
shoulders and acres of bare back. There
were ospreys and bandolettes and jewels for
the adornment of the hair, but scarcely a
chiffon rag on the bodies.
State Governor Stanley weathered the
show with the assistance of Nigel Conant.
The Cliveden Maddens showed them both
round, and kept the rank outsiders from
biting them. The concert in the lounge was
the main attraction. Somebody murmured that
Daisy Jerome and other music-hall artists
were on the platform, so early birds made
a rush and perched in the seats, leaving the
late birds to stand on their toes and breathe
hard on each other’s bare back. There was
a lot of hack, the women being in the pro-
portion of 30 to one man. Willy Kelly,
usually debonair and cool when facing the
most fearful odds, looked positively scared
when he found himself alone and unsup-
ported in the concert doorway. Dame
Madden and daughter Lesly sat close at
hand. A crushed, buzzing crowd suddenly
discovered a spinning-jenny in the middle
ot the room. “Oh, quite laike Monte Carlo !”
cooed one expensive matron with a knowing
smirk. A real alcoholic bar was installed
in the corner. A Fenner girl, who used to
be so demure, acted as barmaid with real
vine leaves in her hair. She also had a
gilded wineglass hung behind one ear, and a
bunch of grapes on her side curls. Miss
McEvoy and Miss Rutherford dashed about
with trays and “chits” in approved hotel
fashion. Of course you couldn’t pay for
the drinks. Well, not cash, anyway. But
you could redeem your lOU afterwards.
Mrs. Leslie Macdonald and some girls had
a lingerie stall. One young man was heard
bleating through the crowd, "Where’s the
undies’?” The most embarrassing garments
were held up for inspection, and not even
a dainty nightcap went for less than three
guineas. Pink and mauve bebe ribbon did
the trick. The sale of gifts went on all
night in one bedlam, while Sassiety’s best
amateurs and a few professionals sang their
darndest in another room. Numbers of
naturalised Germans and Austrians were
present, and their cash was interned in
sweet Charity’s name.
Some Clivedeneers had offered their flat-
rooms for bridge, and to these roosts
lovers of quiet stole away, to play into the
small hours. The roof was available at half
a crown a head. But the night was boister-
ous, so this was not the success it might
have been.
The working women of Port Melbourne
claim the right to the pier-welcomes to the
homing Australian wounded. They have
got a sort of lien over the patriotic job by
reason of having been on hand to give food
and cigarettes and general cheer to the first
unexpected boatload. They certainly were
hustled from the gates by the guard; but,
despite that, they got as near as possible.
Later on, when the social frilled-lizards
awoke from their doze and wanted to rush
in, the women of the Port beat them down.
They stuck to their guns, too, with the re-
sult that when the next boat arrived it was
the Port contingent only that wore badges
of admission to the shed on the wharf. The
motor class sent 200 cars to carry the men
in triumphal procession round Melbourne;
but “the Ports” gave the heroes the first
refreshments and smiled the first welcome.
That deathless legion, the Arts and Crafts,
storm fresh heights of glory this year.
President Lady Creswell, who helped
to hatch out the annual show, cautiously
says they would not have exploded
into new rooms had they foreseen the
war; nevertheless things are trundling
along satisfactorily. The rooms are
upstairs in the new Elizabeth House.
Besides being big enough for exhibition pur-
poses, they have fine space for “the Red
Cross work for which the- local military
hospitals commend them.
When you arrive you will see Mrs.
Courtney, from Learmonth (near Ballarat),
sitting in the big bow window spinning wool
straight from the bale, and carding it before
putting it on her wheel. The carding is done
with something like butter-pats. All the old
ladies in Melbourne are foregathering and
humbling the ignorant young moderns with
such remarks as, “My mother used to do it
this way” ; and “ I’ve always seen the w’heel
turned that way.” They are having the
time of their lives. The Irish crochet that
the Carmichael dame loved is shown every-
where, for she left a shoal of devoted imita-
tors behind her. Artist George Dancey has
revived an ancient process of painting on
dry plaster for exterior decoration, which
is said to be sun-proof and damp-resisting.
He shows a large, indestructible painting by
himself. Yon could only disturb it with a
German siege-howitzer. Valeria Correll
shows her white sculped “Sirens.” Being
talented but languid, the lady achieves fine
work spasmodically, doing pottery without
a wheel and other clever things. She has
sculped (roughly) two fighters at close
quarters—an Australian khaki man appro-
priately on top. Tom Carter, now teach-
ing at the Working Men’s College, shows
some pottery of original design.
Miss Baker is trying to capture some of
the German toy trade. She has a table
full of cute things, wherein the detail is
good and the work as careful as that of an
old and devoted cabinet-maker. This gentle
toy-maker lives down at Black Rock, where
she is kept busy on orders for the coming
Christmas. Miss Alsop has a gallery of sil-
houettes. Bertha Merfield signs some big
decorative picture work. One deep, shadowy
gully is just the thing for the hot weather.
Mrs. Courtney supplies woven rugs and
spreads of all sizes. Miss Butchard has
tiny forest and hill scenes set up as Christ-
mas greetings. The bothered Christmas
buyers can get in early and have something
original and Australian for a crown. Sec-
retary Miss McGeorge is prepared to help
visitors after artistic things for all she is
worth —and all they are worth.
Sir Arthur Stanley is in modified mourn-
ing for his aunt Maud, who died the other
day. There’s a strong infusion of Christian
Endeavoring in the family, and the late Hon.
Maud Stanley was well known along that
road. Her specialty was the moral and
physical improvement of the London ’Arriet.
and she will be missed by many girls with
blue draggle-tailed feathers in their Sunday
’ats.
Rupert Clarke’s son-in-law, Reggie
Power, made a few people sit up and stare
when he admitted that he was going off to
fight somewhere as a private and wouldn’t
seek a commission or a discount or anything
like that.
Ernest Williams, killed at the Dardan-
elles, was a son of Hartley Williams, an
ex-Judge of our Supreme Court, now living
in England, and best remembered as the man
who didn’t get the State C.J.-ship. Ernest
Williams had the D. 5.0., and was a major
in the Northumberland Fusiliers. A sister
married Oswald Watt, the distinguished Aus-
tralian airman, a union since dissolved.
Another sister is the wife of Moleswortli
“Greystones” Greene, of Bacchus Marsh and
Broken Hill. Major Williams’s mother was
a daughter of the late Major Horne, a re-
tired British officer who settled in Arma-
dale when that suburb was a village.
Her Federal Ex. motored to St. Ivilda the
other afternoon to open a Red Cross fete at
St. Michael’s Ladies’ College, of which an
old friend of Lady Helen’s —a sister of
Admiral Poore —is principal.
Colonel Burston, the maltster who was
once mayor of Melbourne and is now in
Egypt, is presently to have a visit at Alex-
andria from his wife and daughter, soon to
leave these shores for England. There is
also a son at the Front, Dr. Roy Burston,
with whom the family hopes to foregather
for a few minutes. Mrs. Burston is going
t» England to keep a married daughter com-
pany in the absence on active service of
her British husband, Dr. Cook.
The death of Dr. Langley from spinal
meningitis is particularly sad. He and his
wife (she was one of the Clapp sisters)
were engaged for a number of years, and
now, after a brief 12 months of wedlock,
Mrs. Langley has been widowed through the
horrible epidemic.
Judge Moule’s handsome elder son. a
lance-corporal in the Light Horse, has fallen
at the Dardanelles, after being wounded at
the other Front. His youthful-looking
mother went invalided to Englanu from
Egypt, whither she had pilgrimaged to be
near her boy.
P. N. Tewkesbury, the mining man who
put his money into the Oriental Hotel, has
just been comforted by cable. His wife
and school-girl daughter Vera were on the
torpedoed Arabic, but were saved, though
without their trunks. When you remem-
ber the way Mrs. Tewkesbury dresses it
makes you realise how r horrible these wrecks
are;
A very modest Australian A .C., at present
at the Dardanelles, is James Rogers, a
Gippslander who won his high honor in
S’Africa by rescuing wounded comrades
under fire. Rogers could never be per-
suaded to show his decoration or talk about
the heroic incident. His sister is the wife
of John Wadsley, a farmer at Koo-wee-rup.
She proudly shows two portraits one or her
brother as he departed for the South African
war undecorated, and another of the
splendid khaki fellow as he left for the
Dardanelles, wearing the Y.C. and two other
medals.
The Bert Bailey co. at the King’s is losing
its energetic secretary and press agent,
Regina Varley, who is entering into a long
and permanent engagement with Monty
Grover about the middle of the month. The
bride-to-be is a niece of the late Violet
Varley, and is a petite edition of her relative
in many charming particulars. The wedding
is to occur in Melbourne, and it is understood
that a secluded bit of St. Paul’s has been
pegged out for the ceremony.
Kitty Boyes, a sister of Mrs. Ted Dyson,
is shouting hooray and throwing up her hat
(the brown one with the feather). Her
fiance, Captain Jackson, has cabled that he
is returning to Australia, and wedding bells
are ordered for September 28. The Cap-
tain is a Naval Reserve officer who is usu-
ally very busy with troopships.
Jacques 801, the Belgian tenor who
stranded on these hospitable shores, sang at
the big Cliveden show. A benefit concert
has been arranged and will duly happen on
Thursday night, September 2. Horace
Stevens, Mary Conly and the Jansen lady
will sing. Gwen Richardson is billed to
recite “What Will Ye Give to the Nation V”
and the John Madden pair have promised to
attend and lend an ear apiece. S’John, by
the way, is now sicklied o’er with his pale
forecast concerning the war. He guaran-
teed that it would end in August.
An assisted immigrant named Tony, of
Arrandale, was selected by the judges as the
best animal in the Kennel Club’s cat-show.
Tony is a chinchilla Persian owned by Lena
Robinson, the Malvern meow-expert. The
lady’s name is also associated with a widely-
known breed of Jersey cows.
George Upward, until lately clerk-assistant
to the Senate, and sometime Usher of the
Black Rod, Pole or Perch, has been having
a tough time in hospital at Cairns, North
Queensland. Upward has- for years dwelt
in a somewhat unfashionable region on the
St. Kilda-road, and his pedestrian habit pro-
bably has some bearing on the fact that he
is reported to have the Cairns bacilli beaten
to a frazzle.
The Red Cross has had to entreat people
not to send in any moreT soldiers’ mittens
made from old stockings. Many of the leg-
wraps were green with old age, some had
been darned so much that the original stock-
ing was a myth, and some —well, some
hadn’t been to a very good laundry. In
short, they were more shocking than stock-
ing, and as such were cremated.
State Ex. Stanley opened the show of
Will Dyson’s war caricatures in the
Centreway on Thursday. He remarked at
length that caricaturists are loved by the
giggling friends of the caricature, as well
as by their enemies, but rarely by the un-
fortunate victim. Stanley thinks, however,
that caricaturists are the salt of the earth—
which fact, I am sure, will cheer up the
trade greatly. He quoted the saying that
“a certain amount of fleas is good for a dog
—it keeps him from thinkin’ on bein' a
dog”—and then stumbled as if he had tem-
porarily lost his nerve. But it was a dry,
dusty day, and he had already opened two
vastly different shows since lunch.
Bigjohn Forrest had a birthday party
tendered him at the Grand Hotel last week
by his devoted wife. There were no candles
to the birthday cake, because G 8 would have
been required, and an old politician like
Bigjohn would rather use his breath to
make a speech than lumber round blowing
out lights. Besides, the patriotic hostess
expressed her opinion that it would be
wasteful in these warlike times. The Fanes
and Doris Blackwood drank Bigjohn’s
health with the L. K. S. Mackinnons,
Caseys, Grices, Fairbairns and l.undales.
Margery Withers, the young daughter of
the late Walter Withers, is painting some
nice stuff nowadays. Times are too risky
for her to attempt a show at present; but
she has some work on view as part of the
Arts and Crafts show. There are two head
portraits, vigorous and surprising in treat-
ment. One of a child in a bine snood,
laughing widely over square white teeth, is
infectiously merry. However, portraits are
not her only line, for she has signed two
autumn, briar-bramble scenes which are fine
and breezy, like the weather we’ve been
having.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
There was another Peace Riot at the Botanic
Park on Sunday, and the Premier has threatened
that if the Peace enthusiasts don’t go a bit slower
in the way of inflammatory language there will be
trouble. The members of the Peace Alliance dissent
fiercely. They are prepared, seemingly, to fight
anyone but the Germans.
The Shiels-Brookm.rn wedding occurred on Tues-
day. The bride-elect was the victim at a tea-party
at the Queen Adelaide Club a day or so before the
Mrs. Theodore Bruce has gone to Egypt, to be
near her son, who is with the 4th Light Horse.
The A.R.C. Grand National Meeting began on
Saturday. The Governor was there (he had just got
back from his Broken Hill trip) ; also, several racing
men and a few racing women. But it was a dead
festivity, on the whole.
The newly-launched League of Loyal Women is
opening an inquiry office where ordinary, every-day
information on the matter of addresses to troops at
the Front, etc., will be answered.
Capt. Henry Kent Hughes, of the Ist Battalion
King’s Own Yorkshire Light Infantry, who has been
killed in action in Belgium, belonged to the family
whose name is all over the world. He was a grand-
son of the late Edmund Hughes, a one-time S.M. of
this State. A brother, a captain in the Royal AVor-
cesters, was killed at Mons, and another is a mem-
ber of the Royal Flying Corps.
The Public Library has accepted a copy of the
“Diary and Letters of Mary Thomas.” The writer
was the grandmother of Evan Kyffin Thomas, of the
R roister proprietary, who has edited the book.
Mr. J. 11. Humphreys, the secretary of the Pro-
portional Representation Society of Great Britain,
is in Adelaide, lie gave a demonstration of the tech-
nicalities of the system at the State Electoral Office
on Friday, and will give a lecture on the same sub-
ject on Wednesday night. Furthermore, he is to
speak about Belgian relief work at a Belgian concert
which the Wattle Day League has arranged for
Monday afternoon.
Captain Lance Hawker, who has added the V.C.
to the D.S.O. which he already had, is a son of the
late Lieut. “Harry” Hawker, R.N., and a grandson
of the late G. C. Hawker —one of the State's first
pastoralists. His younger brother, who is in the
Royal Field Artillery, has been in the thick of the
fighting since the first days of the war, and has also
been twice mentioned in despatches, and his eldest
brother Seymour, who married an Adelaide girl, and
has settled on part of the original Bungaree station,
is attached to the force that is in training in tin?
Exhibition camp. It is a useful family record.
News has come through that Lieut. Henry Marten,
the younger of the Humphrey Martens’ sons, has
been killed in action in France. He was in England
when the war broke out, and volunteered at once,
since when he has been in the trenches almost con-
tinuously. He was attached to Sir John French’s
staff, and for two months was second in command
of General Smith-Dorrien’s Composite Guard. It
is only quite recently, that his people heard he had
been mentioned in despatches.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’ f WAS Rory’s eyes were bright with
laughter,
’Twas Rory’s heart a while was
mine—
Och ! Queer are dreams that follow after
A little sup of fairy wine.
’Twas summer-time. The men were reaping;
I heard them sing as I went by.
Sure what was yon small thing lay sleeping
Below the thorn bush thin and dry?
I ler dress was tufts and ends and tatters,
A fringe of green, a flounce of rose,
Like things the ragman Autumn scatters
Across the garden as he goes.
Och, now! And why would Ibe waking
The like of her that sunny day?
“And is it rest, good soul, you’re taking,
Or stopped to smell the new-mown hay?”
She looked up quick to hear me speaking,
She rose upon her crooked feet:
“ ’Tis far across the world I’m seeking
The blue-eyed girl that I must meet.”
“And if you met her would you know her?
’Tis blue for sure my own. eyes are.
What thing is this that you would show her,
Small woman that have come so far?”
She looked me up and down and over.
“ ’Tis light you are to think and speak.
There’s blossom on the standing clover
Will turn to brown, sweet hay next week!”
'Twas something in her old hand shaking.
Dr in her small, brown wrinkled head,
I could not turn myself to making
A mock of that strange thing she said.
M.v heart was like the summer weather,
A quiet thought between us fell.
We walked the long, green road together,
And stayed to drink at Finnard well.
Twas she drank first. A thin branch droop-
ing
Let in the sunlight on her head.
And. sure, her shadow, bent and stooping,
Made all the water purple-red.
Deli, heart! ’Tis idle to be thinking
What spell she set upon me there,
I could not hold my mouth from drinking
For all the gold a queen might wear.
'Twas in my blood the strangest feeling.
’Twas in my head the strangest tune —
Twas Rory’s self that found me kneeling
Beside a thorn tree in the moon.
The moon was white and high and lonely
And Rory’s eyes seemed cold and far—-
“Dch, Rory ! Am I dreaming only,
Dr is it by the thorn you arc'?”
Tis five long days that I came after
And tracked your steps along the hill,
1 heard the small men at their laughter,
Their running feet were never still.
“I tracked you through the summer weather.
I tracked you through the thunder rain
And up the whins and through the heather
And by the thorn trees back again.”
I seemed asleep and near the waking
From some ill dream the dark had grown:
A cold thought in my soul was aching:
His eyes were hard like eyes of stone.
Dch, heart!” I said. “The moon is keeping
A quiet road across the hill,
And half the soul of me is sleeping
And half the dream is with me still.
“And down the night I hear them calling -
Man dear, you will not let me go—
There’s music rising now and falling
And death lights in the thorn and sloe.”
The moon was white and high find lonely,
And Rory’s eyes seemed far away—
"Och ! Tell me am I dreaming only,
Dr have they led my wits astray?”
A song blew up and down and over.
And by the thorn I heard her speak :
“There’s blossom on the standing clover
Will turn to brown, sweet hay next week !”
N.S.AV.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
The Fires that Never Go Out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ql FLU, isn t it, how one’s brain re-
sponds to the stimulus of suggestion?
Hear a song, and the refrain of a
second comes lilting through the years, and
images of places and long-forgotten people
crowd on the mind. Strange tricks of the
mental telephone exchange these. A rainy
day, a gleam of sunshine, a bird’s whistle,
a beer —anything and everything seems to
build bridges over time and space and weave
quickly-shifting patterns on memory’s loom.
An idle gaze on the prim suburban fireplace
to-night, and other fires, long cold, but once
so warm, were lighted yet again.
The upper half of the galley door to lee-
ward is open, and the big bos’n, not wait-
ing to shoot back the lower bolt, lifts a
shivering boy through the aperture and
dumps him on the floor. “lie vants a varm.
doctor, grunts the squarehead—he has got
his seaboots filled for his pains—and
splashes away aft. The negro cook sits
propped against the weather side between
his cooking range and bread locker. The
evening’s work is over, and presently he
will crawl awav through the bulkhead into
his berth beyond.
The boy rests upon the thing called a
table, grateful for the warmth of the fading
fire, thankful for the heavy, steam-laden air.
The negro leans over, pushes a poker into
the coal and rattles out some sparks; then
without shifting his seat reaches a pannikin,
and filling it with coffee hands it; to his
guest. The lad swallows tin* black, tepid
liquid at a gulp. llis wet feet are warming
now; his ears beein to thaw. What a good
fire it is! And Africa stares mutely at
the square of darkness framed in the half
door—no word, no movement. Is he dream-
ing. or thinking? Or is lie just an inky
automaton, bowed by the weight of een-
HIGH INTEREST.
HEBREW IMMIGRANT: “Tiny give £5 bonus for each baby, you say? And how much does
it cost to yet married ?”
AUSTRALIAN: “Oh, say ten bob.”
ILL: “ Mine gracious! One thousand per cent.! Tot a glorious country!”
turies, cowed by the curses of fore and aft?
The boy has often wondered since. He
didn’t wonder then.
The bos’n comes blundering in an hour
or so later, with dripping oilskins and
streaming face, to drink coffee and suck a
pipe so atrociously rank as to outrage the
everlasting sea. He, too, drinks and sucks
in .silence.
“I’d better turn in now, Charlie,” says
the boy.
“Right.”
As they get out on the slippery, reeling
deck, a great sea-hardened paw grips the
kid by the arm and stays there till the hatch
over the cabin companion-way has been
slipped back.
“Good night!" grunts a grail voice, and
the cover jerks quickly over.
Good old Charlie! Dead long ago—but
where and how and when? Among men
rough and uncouth always, brutal not in-
frequently, and yet —amazing paradox—tor
many a day ashore and afloat the good friend
and mate of an always-timid boy.
The galley fire that night how warm, how
cosy, how cheerful —the brightest in all the
world !
A log fire is glowing at the end of the big
hut. Half a dozen unkempt men and a lad
are feeding at a table in the centre —boiled
mutton, potatoes, bread, treacle, tea and
duff. They feed with the eager deliberation
of hunger. Their work has been hard and
long. The meal is not a pleasant spectacle,
but there is only one possible connoisseur
there, and he is holding a chop in his fingers
so that he may tear the meat off with his
teeth. Old Peter finds the duff handiest on
the end of a knife. Andy drinks to the
accompaniment of sounds like a suction
pump suffering from asthma. The cook
gathers up the humble properties in a bucket,
seizes one of the two candles and goes out-
The others light up, pull their seats to the
lire, and make room in the middle for the
chop manipulator, who produces a book—“A
Tale of Two Cities.”
To-night’s instalment covers the rescue
from prison of Charles, and goes on to the
execution of Sydney Carton. The pipes go
out: the tire flickers lower; the eager faces
of the listeners seem to be moulded in bronze
as the youth reads on, page after page of that
noble, moving prose. They had never heard
anything like that before —only a couple
could read at all. “I am the Resurrection
and the Life....”
The boy closes the book. The audience
fades away to the tents in the scrub. The
reader is looking into the fire, alone —and
when he wakes only ashes are there.
There seems no warmth in this fire. The
man throws on another log and moves closer.
He is tired with travel and loss of sleep;
the hour is on the boundary between night
and daylight when all Nature shrinks trem-
blingly from the ordeal of waking. The snow
is deep outside. Another man comes in and
takes the opposite chair. “At any time
now,’’ he says- The other nods, with mute
acquiescence and a fantastic attempt to
guess at what a tree feels like on being sud-
denly pulled up by the roots. Then in a
moment the tire spurts up. Yes, it burns
warmly now—brightly like it did in those re-
mote days when he used to sit there by that
same fender with a sister, and a mil. hand-
some woman they called “Mother” told them
wondrous stories of splendid kings and
queens, of kind fairies and wicked gnomes.
of charity and virtue in castle and cottage;
who knelt with them to pray for mercy, poor
wee souls; wlm put them to lied and tucked
them in and kissed them—the one human
being around whom every incident of life ie-
SO TIRED.
“You remind me of a man I used to be very
fond of."
“A former sweetheart?”
“No —grandpa !"
volved —up to the morning he had gone away
and a dear, soft hand had clung round his
neck at the gate. Why, it seemed only the
other day that lie-
“Better come !*' The nurse beckons to the
door, and he follows, the doctor going first.
The lined face is'peaceful now under the
grey hair. The breathing so soft—so regular
that it hardly seems possible there was no
lt stops ! There are others there. Why
do they bow? he wonders. Why does the
white-bearded man who shook his hand as
he came in totter and have to be half-carried
away? Dead! “1 am the Resurrection and
the Life....” once more.
Back before the fire in the old room again.
It is burning low now, .as it sometimes did
when a boy listened to someone singing
“Abide with' me.” How long, long ago that
was! “Fast falls the eventide”
“Why. daddy, you’ve let the fire go nearly
out!, How cold you must be!” And a girl’s
warm little face snuggles down against his
ear.
“Yes, dear; yes—as cold as looking at the
lire makes one.”
“But I’ll build it up again”—and they
built it up again together, and the man
laughed. The cycle had not been broken.
MUJIK.
Gone Is My Youth.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I would it were not so,
But it is simple truth ;
My eyes have lost their glow.
Gone is my youth !
All, how he flattered me
In the soft words he spoke ;
Simply a boy was he
My heart awoke !
Youth set my veins a-thrill,
Gave me a thought divine.
If youth could love me still,
Youth still was mine !
Thus, for the hopes he raised
With his infernal art,
Plans of a life were razed —
Youth owned my heart.
Smiles the world scornfully ;
Folly my hope must seem —
’Twas but youth’s courtesy
Gave me my dream !
Now, I the wrinkles feel
That speak the cold, hard truth ;
Grey years upon me steal —
N.S.W.
Gone is my youth !

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Simplex”: Let a mere French novelist
put a mob of Australians right in the making of
clamper:—
Our bill of fare consisted of ham soup, some
haricot beans and damper. Damper is a cake of
flour and water flattened between the hands and
baked in hot ashes.
The authority is Alexander Dumas in “A Modern
Gil Bias,” a tale of the Californian ’forties.
WRONG, SEE!
“Hurt thumb—reckless driving."
“Car?"
“No — nail."
Lou : Up on the Olinda heights (Vic.),
where big rocks jut out from the hillsides, they have an
original method of clearing the slopes for cultiva-
lion. Alongside each big boulder a bigger hole is
put down, and when all is clear the obstruction is
tumbled, in. It s rather a risky caper, especially if
you don t. sink the hole on the uphill side.
“Axehandle”: “Mulga” (B. 5/8/T5),
who says no man ever saw a dingo run down on
horseback in timbered country, overlooked me. I
was mustering on a Monaro sheep-run south and
east of the Snowy River, in open forest country,
when my mate started a warrigal. The dingo had
100 yards’ start of the horseman, who, however,
before they’d gone half a mile, wheeled him in my
direction. I had no difficulty in heading him to
where the sheep-dogs, aided by a crack on the skull
from a whip-handle, put him out of business.
Either of our mounts would have to gallop hard to
be last in a Melbourne Cup. Again, the same mate,
jogging around his paddocks on a prad whose knees
betokened ardent religion, ran upon a full-grown
dingo and, giving chase, kept close enough to the
dog to chop pieces out of him with his whip. The
quarry finally took refuge in a hollow log, from
which he was dragged and given a dose of overproof
whip-handle.
“Billy Bine”: Re origin of tlie word
“jackeroo” (B. 12/8/’l5). Archie Meston (B.
18/5/’96) stated that the word came from the old
Brisbane blacks who called the pied crow-shrilce (Stre-
pera graculina ) “tchakeroo,” meaning a gabbling
and garrulous bird. They applied it also to the
German missionaries of 1838, because of their vab-
ber-yabber. Later, “jackeroo” became the synonym
of all white men. Stuart Russell, in his “Genesis of
Queensland,” tells of the blacks who reported the
invasion of a party of “jackeroos” to the Darling
Downs (Q.) in 1840. They referred to Patrick
Leslie and his brothers, Hodgson and others who
overlanded from N. S. Wales unknown to the white
settlement at Moreton Bay. The news, lacking-
nothing as it passed from tribe to tribe, was that
beyond the range there were “jumped-up” white men
with human bodies on six dog-legs. The abos.
wanted the military officer in charge at Moreton Bay
to sally forth and shoot these jackeroos.
“Solentes”: Permit me to introduce
another abo. monarch. He reigns at Bellinger Heads
(N.S.W.) and displays upon his breast a large
brass plate with the inscription: “Benelong, King
of the Urungas.” The ancient, whose head has been
well dipped in big-pfeller flour bag, has some quaint
flesh carvings on his body, his back being embel-
lished with a rude imitation of a branch of the
bangalow palm, scored deep into the flesh. With
his metal insignia and a glittering crown contrived
from a cake-tin he is a feature of the local agri-
cultural shows.
“Grasshopper”: While Abo. contributors
are hunting up remedies to keep horses out of hay-
stacks, and mice from jumping fences, some cove or
even bloke may find about him a remedy to stop a
dog eating eggs. My brute is an unholy terror for hen
harvests, and a cliook has merely to cackle once
and he is round to relieve her of any anxiety she
niaj feel that her labor of love will go unappreci-
ated. I have thought that perhaps hanging would
cure him, but lie is handy for rounding up other
things than eggs.
“Dajoor” : How is this for endurance?
At Nimmo station, Monaro (N.S.W.), after the heavy
snowfall of last May, a horse was found alive after
being 52 days embedded. On the same property a
mob of 13 draughts are still missing, and the spring
will reveal dumb-animal tragedies without parallel,
I believe, in these parts.
NOT MUCH OF A FRIEND.
COUNSEL: “Mas the deceased in the habit of talking to himself when alone?"
WITNESS : “I don't know.”
COUNSEL: “You don't know! Why, I thought you told us you were his oldest friend?"
WITNESS: “Yes, but I never happened to be with him when he was alone."
“Pick” : Another black monarch is Jacky
Mumbulla, of the Wallagans, South Coast (N.S.W.).
lie was decorated with the insignia of majesty in
l&ll at a corroborCe which many whites attended,
llis name does not appear on the signboard, which
previously was worn for 60 years by King Merri-
man, a noted South Coast blackfellow, after whom
was named Merriman Island, in Wallaga Lake.
Purity of race and not inheritance brought the
honors to Mumbulla. He is the only full-blood left
in a decadent tribe.
* *• * *
“Bilben”: “Devilfish’s” anxiety (B.
22/7/’l5) to know how fish arrive in isolated pools
of water where no fish have any apparent right to
be used to be mine in Scotland. Where I came
from there were a number of disused quarry-holes
which had filled up with rain-water and soakage,
and these swarmed with little fish. One high-brdw
explained to me that the fish had originally arrived
as spawn adhering to the outer garments of over-
landing aquatic birds, and had multiplied in the
usual manner. Seems feasible.
* * * *
“Santo”: Like the bower-bird, the satin-
birds and the gow-wows (alias dirty, dozen, alias
twelve apostles) have play-houses to be proud of.
The blue-black or bronze-green satin-bird has a
great fancy for anything blue—castor-oil bottles for
preference—when decorating the floor of and
approaches to his. house. The premises consist of
an avenue of sticks joined at the top like a dog-leg
fence. The proprietors skip through this with the
grace of a Genge. Although a most beautiful crea-
ture, the satin-bird is a pest to cultivators, his par-
ticular line of destruction being cabbages. The
play-nests of the gow-wow are not so elaborate. They
are built anywhere and at any time of the year,
merely, as far as I can learn, to provide an excuse
for a rough up and a scamper round ; for the gow-
wow with his ‘■‘Hullo! Hullo!” is the happiest fellow
in the bush.
*•**-.*
Manly (N.S.W.) had a call from a couple
of singulai visitors a week or so back. They were
fish (Regalecus banlesii) from remote depths of the
sea, and few human beings have ever looked upon
them. The two, which were cast up on the Ocean
Beach, were lift, and 10ft. long respectively; their
flat, ribbon-like bodies tapered to a point, and were
covered, with silvery slime. The heads were distinc-
tive, with a sucker mouth and a crest of red feelers.
Director of Fisheries Stead reported that the speci-
mens were uncommonly large and of a rare and
interesting character. He accounted for their distance
from home by supposing that they had somehow
generated body-gases which caused them to balloon
to the surface, whence they could not get back to
old Davy Jones’s cellar.
“Combo”: If “L.L.W.” (B. 5/8/’l5) is
itching’ to start a civil war he weiit the right way
about it when he pointed to the Richmond River as
the home of the water hyacinth, and asked why the
Clarence would not harbor the beautiful pest. Let
me tell him that the Clarence was the first river in
N.S.W. which provided a hyacinthine pathway for
pedestrians. Swan Creek, near Grafton, at one time
navigable for miles, could be walked across 15 years
ago, and not so very far back the steamer Orara
was unable to shoulder a way through the plant to
the wharf at Maclean.
“Moana”: The order of things has been
reversed, and Maoriland has started exporting local
products to rid other lands of pests, instead of im-
porting for the same purpose things that promptly
become pests themselves. The other day an order
NOT TO MINCE MATTERS.

up agen that Kaiser any time, just yon
let 'im know pretty straight out. what
he's thought, of up 'ere at Dog Flat."
arrived from the Cook Islands for a consignment of
native owl, or morepork, for use against a plague of
rats. How a wholesale supply of morepork is going
to be obtained the Lord only knows, for the animal
lives mostly in an inaccessible tree and only wakes
up at night. Collecting him will certainly require
a fresh loan.

ON THE SPREE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is joy and jubilation
On the Spree;
There’s a mad excited nation
On the Spree;
There are loads of joyous cables,
There are tons of giddy fables,
And a row of forty Babels
On the Spree.
There’s the maddest sort of Kaiser
On the Spree ;
He'll be yet a trifle wiser
On the Spree;
Now on Paris, now on Calais,
He's advancing in a sally,
Heaping crowns beyond a tally
For the Spree.
There’s a town beflagged for ever
On the Spree;
Of defeat it dreameth never
On the Spree ;
there s a subtle Press romantic
Drugs the people till they’re frantic
In a fury Corybantic
On the Spree.
There will be an end of scorning
On the Spree;
There will dawn a day of mourning
On the Spree;
And a town of plumes and hearses,
Broken down with black reverses,
Mill go down in bitter curses
On the Spree!
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.

HOW I DARKENED MY
GREY HAIR.
Lady (Jives Simple Home Recipe That
She Tsed to Darken Her Grey Hair.
For years I tried to restore my grey hair
to its natural color with the prepared dyes
and stains, but none of them gave satisfac-
tion, and they were all expensive. I finally
came across a simple recipe which I mixed
at home that gives wonderful results. I gave
llie recipe, which is as follows, to a number
of my friends, and they are all delighted
with it. To 7oz. of water add a small box of
Orlex Compound, loz. of bay rum and 1 oz.
of glycerine. These ingredients can be
bought at any chemist’s at very little cost.
Use every other day until the hair becomes
the required shade, then every two weeks.
It will not only darken the grey hair, but
removes dandruff and scalp humors, and acts
as a tonic to the hair. It is not sticky or
greasy, does not rub off and does not color
the scalp. [5

“The Pariah” : The barcoo sickness is not
peculiar to Australia. In the march across the
plains of North China to the relief of the legations
in 1900 the troops were attacked by it. The
generally accepted explanation was that it was due
to the effect of the endless plains on the optic nerve.
Sailors were the worst sufferers, though the land-
crabs came in for their share of the unpleasantness.
Dr. Justi, the bubonic expert, accepted this theory,
and as a counter suggested the wearing of smoked
or red glasses. As far as my personal experience of
the Australian variety went there was no time to
look for any sort of glasses.
“Hay”: Like “Boronia” (B. 17/6/’l5)
and many other children of the bush, I grew up
firmly believing that if a tree had been struck bv
lightning nothing would make it burn, r.ot even
soaking it in kerosene. One day I was sent out with
an empty dray, an axe and orders to bring home a
good load of dry firewood before milking time. In
the fascinating pursuit of wallabies and other bush
game time flew so swiftly that in order to keep my
appointment with Strawberry there was nothing for
it but to load up hurriedly with the lightning-
blasted splinters of an old dry red-gum that every-
body eke had carefully avoided. That night, to my
great surprise, mother remarked: “What splendid
burning wood! Where’d you get it? Yon must go
out to-morrow for some more.” The whole tree
was carted home and burnt to the entire satisfac-
tion of the household, but I never mentioned light-
ning. People hate to have their cherished traditions
knocked out.
* * * *
“Lott” : Talking of liorse-breakers, one of
the best in the Flinders (Q.) country was known as
Professor McGilivray. He was a wonder, though
he had St. Vitus’s dance pretty badly. His scheme
to cure a kicker was to cut open both sides of a
bag longways, roll it up nicely, fasten it to the back
of the saddle and then let it gradually unroll and
hang down over the hocks. The patient lashed out
for a while, but soon gave in, and seldom kicked
afterwards.
* * * *
Last year’s census of Black Brother in
N. S. Wales discovered a population of 6779, com-
prising 1730 full-bloods (745 male adults, 409
gins and 516 children) and 5049 shandy-gaffs (1261
male adults, 1029 lady adults and 2759 pics.). Com-
pared with the previous year there was a decrease of
131 full-bloods. The first census of abos. in the
State was taken in 1882, when the total of pure
breeds was 6540 —approximate, of course, owing to
the difficulty of getting at them. In other words,
while 73 out of every 100 full-bloods alive in 1882
have disappeared and none have arrived to take
their place, the half-breeds have increased from
2379 to 5049. It is a pretty shocking record to
face our own Civilisation with.

gizzard was that one of the mob booted the carpet off
me arf-a-quid lid, and another potted a new stook and
four jim that I was goin’ to weigh out for me new
clobber.

“F.H.” : This is the time of year to make
a list of Australian shrubs for the garden. The
weeping wattle, alleged to be one of the fastest
growers on the market, has a leafy habit from its
early youth onwards, and certainly moves along in
a hurry, filling out into a bushy mass, covered with
blossom, in about three years from the time it
leaves the pot. The black wattle (several kinds) is
a lottery. It may shoot up straight and rather
spindly, or fill out into a fat, squat,
desirable garden-shrub. The golden
wattle is worth while, having large,
shinv-green leaves, while its bloom
is the most magnificent of all. As
for the silver wattle (Cootamundra or
baileyana), it is beautiful when the
greenish-blue tops are sprouting, and
when in bloom ; but on the whole is
a somewhat dingy tree, particularly
when its first youth is past. The
Queensland sugar-gum is liable to
suffer from borer-troubles. But there
are six in this scribe’s garden, of
various ages up to three years, and
they are still going strong—the best
native shade-tree in Australia, and
good company on hot nights, when
the soft clashing of the leaves sounds
like the sea on a beach of shells. For
the acacia longifolia, all lovers of the
beautiful should stand *up and give
three rousing cheers. Its foliage is
of a peculiarly bright and vivid
green, and when its immense yellow
blossoms are crowding every part of
the tree the sight is well worth the
price of admission—ninepence per
plant, potted. The bastard maho-
gany is a galloping grower, with cool,
dark foliage. There are also a few
hundred others; but those mentioned
are commonly-stocked kinds. It
should be noted that the bigger the hole
dug and the deeper, the quicker will' be the
g.owth and the finer the tree. Whatever pruning
is done should be carried out after the tree has blos-
somed.

“Cheeri-wei”: Of all birds tlie favorite
pupil of the Devil must be the pink galah. In the
Vic. Irrigation areas just now there are myriads of
him, devouring fodder seed with the fierceness of
starvation. Never a bird comes along until cocky
sows his oats. Then the air is thick with feathers
and blasphemy. His nibs is too cunning for gun,
poison or trap, so the infuriated irrigators just
race frantically about heaving stones and language.
Where the pink gourmand comes from and why he
has arrived by the thousand are the great present
problems of the irrigation cocky’s existence.
* *• * *
“Sassafras” : I’ve listened to and watched
the lyre-bird from the Bulga to the Border for 17
years, and I know as much about him as most
bushwhackers; but I’m puzzled on one point. The
lyre can and does mimic the black cockatoo, the
jackass, a small waterfall, pit-sawing and many
other bush sounds, but I have never heard him
mimic the most common of his neighbors, the scrub
turkey and wonga pigeon. I don’t think it’s due
to inability, because any bushman can imitate the
call of those birds. What is it, then?
“Sammy ’ : My neighbor, I’hineas Hash,
should have been a military horse-buyer. He’s wasting
his time planting wheat. Hash had a horse with a
bad habit of stumbling and falling on its neck, and
occasionally on Pbineas’s neck. Wherefore he decided
to trade it. Another neighbor had a prad that he
wouldn’t mind getting rid of, so they decided to
do business. The other chap liked the look of Hash’s
horse and was satisfied. But Hash wanted a trial of
the other moke before he took delivery. lie got on
top, but hadn’t gone a hundred yards before .it
stumbled and fell. Hash came back all smiles. “Well,
I s’pose there’s nothin’ doin’ ?” said the other chap.
“Mr oath, there is,” said Hash. “He’s got a
better style of getting up than mine.”

“Rag" : Outback it frequently happens
that neither wood nor iron is procurable; but that’s
no reason for being without a snug, well-lighted
lean-to or caravanserai so long as enough dead
marines are obtainable. A layer of clay about Gin.
deep is put down first; then the bottles, with their
bottoms outward, are put side by side across the
clay. Then other layers of clay and the other in-
gredients are laid on alternately until the walls are
the required height. The advantage of clear glass
bottles over “dog” tins (which are also sometimes
used) is that a certain amount of daylight pene-
trates. Kerosene-tins cut and flattened out make a
good roof. At Niagara (W.A.) there is a township
built of “German bricks,” i.e., clayey loam which is
first puddled, then dumped into boxes (18in. x
9in. x9in.), and left in the sun to dry. So long
as they're protected from rain they’ll stand all
right.
* * * *
“Boronia”: A vegetable much abused in
parts of Westralia is commonly known as “blind
grass.” It grows to a height of about six inches,
and sprouts luxuriantly even in the most poverty-
stricken soil. Although not listed as a poison weed
it does much damage. Remaining beautifully green
throughout the summer months when other grasses
are withered, it tempts the stock; and within a
week after a sheep has had a good blow-out on it
a white film gathers over the animal’s eyes, and
causes permanent blindness. The wocllv-coats, as.a
rule, won’t tackle it unless they are badly needing a
feed. Horses and cattle, though not affected in the
same way, become very weak in the legs and
stagger over the scenery like the midnight
inebriate. Unless speedily and properly treated
they are likely to pass out.
* * * *
“Sarto”: I know an old cockatoo whose .
beak was fastened on to him upside down. That
is, the long part is underneath and curls upward to
meet the short part coming down. I’ve often
wondered if he might be an advocate of the tote
and had his nose put on that way as a guarantee- of
good faith. Anyway, he must be very uncomfortable,
as he has to eat sideways. He boards at Mulwala
(N.S.W.). I once had a magpie who broke a piece
off the top half of his bill. Seeing he couldn't
feed himself I filed the other half down and made
another point for him. He's still using it.

THE ISLANDS OF THE DRESSED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Resident Commissioner of the Gilbert ami
Rllice Islands in a report says that the old method
of lubricating’ the body with cocoanut oil was the
best possible protection from chill. The cotton
frocks for women and shirts for men, which in
the mind of the people seem now so indispensable
to professed Christianity, while reducing the endur-
ance of the skin, render it more susceptible to
chills. —Daily paper.
The heathen in his blindness once
In oil was neatly dressed ;
Until the missionary came,
And, gazing on the sight with shame,
Upon his brother pressed
The shirt of prim propriety,
And pants that stand for piety.
In sinful health the heathen lived
Until with singlets blessed ;
But Brother Chadband and his kin
Have raised the death statistics in
The Islands of the Dressed.
The heathen, heark’ning to the wowsers
Succumbed to an attack of trousers!
VICTORIA.

Why Sore Throat is so Prevalent
THESE three striking illustrations
reveal at once the reason why
Sore Throat, in all its varied,
painful and depressing forms, including
Tonsil itis, is so common a complaint ;
how it may be readily cured, on the one
hand, and effectually prevented on the
other.
Sore Throat and Tonsilitis, with
other infectious diseases, like Diph-
theria, Scarlet Fever, Measles, Mumps,
Whooping-cough and Consumption, are
due to germs. These float in the aii,
settle and develop in the throat. Tn
vigorous health they are destroyed by
the blood. If, however, the individual
is beloAv par the blood is not able to
contend against the rapidity with which
the germs multiply, and he gets the
disease whose germs he has inhaled.
During the winter disease-germs are
particularly prevalent, for the weather
conditions are then most favorable to
their growth. People with Sore Throat
and Tonsilitis, those sickening for the
diseases already mentioned, and con-
valescents from them, are constantly
passing through the streets, and going
to theatres, music-halls, concerts and
other public gatherings. Not only do
they exhale germs which infect the aii,
but they carry a great deal of dust with
them. The dust particles, like the
sooty particles in fogs, act as rafts to
float the germs into the throat.
It is obvious, therefore, that these
germs must he destroyed to prevent
them destroying us.
Their enormous number is suggested
by Figs. 1 and 2.
Fig. 1 represents a microscope plate
which was exposed for a short time to
the air and afterwards kept for two
1.
Fig. 1. How air germs develop in two days.
Fig. 2. How dust germs develop in two days.
Fig. 3. How Formamint prevents any development of air and dust germs.
days at the temperature of the human
body. The white dots represent the
growth of colonies of germs, seen under
the microscope. Each dot contains
thousands of germs.
Eig. 2 is a similar plate exposed to
flue dust and treated in the same way.
The colonies of germs carried by it are
seen as the white patches on the plate,
the blurred and fainter markings being
the particles of dust themselves.
Fig. 3 is a similarly-exposed plate
which was previously disinfected with
saliva containing Formamint, the pleas-
ant-tasting throat tablet which a phy-
sician, writing in one of the leading
medical periodicals, recently declared
was “Unquestionably the germ-de-
stroyer of the age.” The power of
Formamint Tablets is adequately proved
by the fact that neither from the air
nor from the dust were any germs able
to develop. In other words. Forma-
mint completely killed all the germs.
It is therefore, a trustworthy cure for
Sore Throat, and a complete preventive
of diseases whose germs multiply in the
throat.
The highest medical testimony en-
dorses these statements of the efficacy
of the preparation. The Chief Medical
Officer of one of our largest Infectious
Diseases’ Hospitals states in one of the
leading medical journals, “I have never
had Sore Throat myself since I began
to use Formamint ’Cablets, although I
suffered periodically before.”
Another physician writes in the
Practitioner: “Having tried all the
British Pharmacopoeia lozenges, and
most of the well-known proprietary
antiseptic lozenges, I have become re-
duced to one, and one only (for sore
throat), namely, Formamint.” Over
ten thousand doctors have have also
written describing the remarkable
results they have obtained in curing the
various forms of Sore Throat and Ton-
sil itis, and preventing the diseases men-
tioned above, by Formamint Tablets.
