Of course the “National” Government in
Maoriland was formed “for the good of the
Empire.” But listen :
Premier Massey : Had it been possible to carry on
even with a slender majority, 1 should have advised
my party to do so.
Ward: The only alternative to a National Govern-
ment was a dissolution, and then it was obvious that
the result would have been uncertain to both sides.
Now if the uncertain brown majority of one
had only been a certain white majority of
two, how about the Empire?
“Jaded Reader” : As' the months clatter
past and the situation becomes more
crowded and involved, the possibility of a
succession of clean military knock-outs gets
dimmer and dimmer. The world is now
flaming with war from pole to pole ; and, like
a burning warehouse, it will presently start
to die down. Then there will be nothing left
but a bad smell in the dark and millions of
voices crying “Who won?” With the blazing-
eye of the minor prophet I can see old, old
men pottering about with systems for finding
out the result of what we are now enduring.
They will score points for fortresses taken
and take off points for retreats, and on
special evenings they will all differ tooth-
lessly but with great dignity before learned
societies. At present all these faddists are
occupied with rattles and feeding-bottles;
but they will come into their own in due
course and make a deuce of a dust about it.
I hope I am not alive to see the beginning of
the historical riots.
“Mujik”: This is not extracted from the
letter of a Russian prisoner-of-war in Ger-
many :
It was five o’clock, raw, cold and damp. “Come
on,” somebody called, and a rough hand shook his
shoulder. “Time to get up.” He turned wearily
over and sleepily dropped his feet to the floor,
struggled into a pair of pants, and, half-dazed by the
waking transition from slumber, staggered outside.
“Plunk ! ” His foot went up to the ankle into mud.
“Plunk!” the other followed—and so, plunk, plunk,
unwashed and unfed, he reached the shelter of a
shed. There he worked for two hours, with bowed
head and bent back. Then another hour fetching
and carrying, and, at last, breakfast and a trudge
of three miles to school carrying a midday meal of
bread and jam. Before leaving he was warned to be
back in good time for the same job again before tea.
Who was lie? Only an Australian kid!
Where? Anywhere! When? Any time
where Cow is King.
Claude Jean Baptiste Eteveneaux shuffled
off at Akaroa (M.L.) the other day, close up
90. Jean was the earliest settler of the
place, having landed from the Comte de
Paris in 1840. It was he and his comnanions
who caused the annexation scare. But most
of the romance of the race for settlement
between the French and the British warship
has been knocked on the head by Robert
McNab, who shows that the French ship-
load of emigrants only wanted a quiet place
in which to settle. So they sailed into
Akaroa the peaceful, put their tents up, and
became some of the best of Maorilanders.
The War Aphorisms of “F. Born” :
Let us speak respectfully of Kultur. We should
temper the wind to the shorn sham.
The attempt to crush Servia was a “little wrong.”
Little wrongs become big ones when they crush little
people.
The wild hog never fights so furiously as when he
is bleeding to death.
Tirpitz’s hiding of his fleet in the Kiel Canal is
the Teutonic naval equivalent of the fighting in land
trenches. The Teuton must have his ditch.
One of the ugliest paragraphs in “The
War Book of the German General Staff,”
lately translated into English by Prof. Mor-
gan, of University College, London, relates to
treatment of civilians in conquered cities
A still more severe measure is the compulsion of-
the inhabitants to furnish information about their
own army. The majority of writers of all nations
are unanimous in their condemnation of this measure.
Doubtless it will be applied with regret, but the
argument of war will frequently make it necessary.
The exact nature of the measure of “com-
pulsion” which all writers, save the German
variety, “unanimously condemn,” is not
specified. The rack and the thumbscrew,
as Wilfrid Shadbolt remarks in “The Yeo-
man of the Guard,” are fine devices for con-
verting obstinate silence into impassioned
eloquence. The threat to spit a house-
holder’s best baby on a bayonet,- or cut off
its limbs one at a time, is quite likely to
make the fond parent say the things he
ought not to say. There is, in fact, a wide
selection of possibilities in torture, and the
War Book of the German General Staff
leaves the method to the discretion of its
students.
The first Westralian to gain the D.C.M.
is Captain Ayling, who, although only 2J,
has seen service now in two countries, ana
is an old hand at soldiering. In 1908, with
the Army in India, he fought in a frontier
war, where he was decorated. Later he
returned to Australia and went on the land;
then answered the Call to go on the track
of the Ottoman. The D.C.M. came to him
for his rescue of a brother officer.
John Terry, who reached Tasmania in
18.18 with sons and daughters and a ship-
load of truck and set up the Lachlan flour
mill at New Norfolk, left a good legacy to
the island State. Forty-one of his descend-
ants are now looking for trouble at the
Front and one has been decorated for being
a little more Australian than usual.
Ten are Terrys, the rest descendants of one
or other of John’s nine daughters.
When Sylvester Browne went seeking a
new fortune in Westralia he took a cook
with him. Browne was a large person and
fond of the right kind of food properly pre-
pared ; but he regarded his cook merely as
a lieutenant, having himself a captain’scapa-
city among the pots. A lady-cook previously
employed by the Gormanstons in Tasmania
held sway for some time in the Browne
kitchen when, later on, he settled with his
family at Toorak. Soon the dame was made
famous by Browne’s praise—she had intro-
duced a method of seasoning roast-goose
quite superior to anything known heretofore
by her boss! Browne, by the way, never
ate onions unless they were incorporated
with other foods, and to that he ascribed the
susceptible palate which he preserved.
A mining identity in the Oberon (N.S.W.)
district passed out recently as the result
of a kick from a horse. Old Tom Deegan,
one-time proprietor of Dad’s Millions mine,
now known as Mount Baring, hung on to
that property for a surprising number of
years, and was responsible for a still more
surprising amount of cash being expended
by different parties on the proposition, into
which so far much has gone and extremely
little has returned. An interesting old person-
olity, born at Ballarat, he was able to tell
unending yarns about the early days of
Araluen and contemporary fields. He was
“Dad” of the “Millions” ; and after all these
years of hanging on to the show he won’t
be there when the battery now being erected
drops its stamps. Just as well, perhaps;
for however wed it may turn out it won’t
turn out as well as “Dad” said it would.
“Scallywag”: Griffith, N.S.W. Minister for
Public Instruction, has been exhorted by a
meeting of irate dairy-farmers to drop the
question of child-labor and attend to the
business of his office. lie ought to take
the advice. The Education Department has
for years been aiding and abetting child-
slavery. From any school in a cow district
you can at three o’clock in the afternoon see
a troop of youngsters wending their way
home, having left a half, three-quarters, and
sometimes a full hour before the regulation
time for closing the establishment. These
children lose from one-fourth _ to one-fifth of
the total hours available for instruction. Not
only do they lose the latter portion of the
day’s school work in order to go home to
milk, but many of them arrive an hour late in
the morning, having been detained to feed
calves, clean the separator, and so forth.
Yet on the school roll these mites are marked
as being in attendance for the whole day!
Therefore let Griffith take the kindly cookies’
advice and mind his own business, by insist-
ing on the provisions of the Education Act.
being complied with. I had charge of a
school of 30 pupils some years ago on the
South Cowst. A third of the pupils never
arrived before 10 a.m., and the school was
always empty at three —every child had left
to go home, to milk anything up to 10 cows
before dark. Griffith can get a heavy punch
in on the Legrees of the cowbails by insisting
on regular attendance at school., and if he
fails to do it then it is a fair thing to ask
whether he really wants reform or merely
something to talk about.
Though George Reid has been told that
if he likes to leave the High Commissioner’s
job in October it will be all right, the Fed-
eral Ministry keeps up the fatuous old gag
that the appointment of a successor lias not
been even momentarily considered. If this
is true, Cabinet ought to resign at once and
place Yawn Murray in charge of things. If
it is not true why all this pretence, whereby
Fisher has made himself look unusually
stupid ?
Brethren, a sermon on Blither : —-
Thus far 1765 lessons have been learned from
the war ; and the 1766th, which The Bulletin offers
for consideration this morning, is that it should be
made a felony, indictable before a court composed
of undertakers, for any' person of any height, color,
sex, beauty or rotundity to speak of war except as
an offence in the sight of Heaven ; or for any person
soever to go to sleep bemused by the delusion that
officials and politicians can ever be trusted to look
five minutes ahead unless they live in constant terror
of being found out and hanged; or for any person
to be a blithering optimist. If this has not been
one of the first lessons of this war and every other
war since the Assyrian came down like a wolf on
tlie fold, then Armageddon is a mere rumor and
tlie shortage of shells part of the Allies’ strategy.
Above all things particular attention should be
directed to exterminating that truculent optimist,
the loud, blitherer who in British countries drivels
about Agincourt and thin red lines, and bursts into
Jingo song on the smallest provocation. These
blatant ignoramuses have been greater enemies of the
British people than the whole barbarian brotherhood
of Potsdam and Vienna. Every nation has been
cursed with them, but none more damnably than our
own. To their sinister influence can easily be traced
a condition of affairs in Britain and Australia which
may now never be corrected; a situation which, if
redeemed, will only be so at terrible cost and
misery.
Therefore, if all goes well with Australia, there
should at the first opportunity be created a Depart-
ment for the Suppression of Blither. For 10 years
the war now devastating Europe was inevitable. But
Hell broke over an Empire doped by ignorance, blind-
folded by folly, trusting with pathetic somnolence
to officials and politicians, and comforted by the
fuddled dream that at the critical moment some-
body would wake and confound all our enemies by
shouting “Up Guards and at ’em.”
THE COWARD CLOAK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In an alley someone stirred,
From the shadows someone spoke,
Wove a garment word by word,
Wove and wore the coward’s cloak.
Are you fools that you do fight,
Are you mad that you do give,
Battle for-an Empire’s right!
Is not yours the right to live!
Shall your shattered bones be strewed
Bleaching on a far-off shore —
You who do not own one rood
Of the land you’re fighting for?
But the dust-brown warriors came
Marching through the busy street,
Passed and jeered at him by name,
Stamped his words beneath their feet.
All you women passing near,
Can a monarch give redress,
Dry a lonely mother’s tear?
Will he heed the fatherless?
Shall he hold your joy in fee?
Surely ’tis a scant reward
That the ties of Love should be
Sundered by the murder-sword!
Then a widow drawing nigh,
Weeping with her vital smart,
Straightway to the passers-by
Rent the cloak and bared his heart l
And a hundred hands with scorn
Pointed at his pallicThead:
“Nationhood through Death is born!
We are living in our dead!”
M AORI LAND.

"Y.Z.”: 1 am acquainted with a man of
■Caledonian extraction who is so pathetically
mean and bitterly miserable that it breaks
his heart lo have to buy bread, and breaks
it again to have to eat the bread he has
bought. Pitying his sad state the other
day, I wondered that he continued to live
when, obviously, he must see so much to his
advantage in being dead. 1 recommended
poison. “Aye, yon’s verry true,” he said.
“Monny’s the. time I thocht o’ endin’ it a’.
But Aw’ve bin talkin’ wi’ a chemist laddie,
and he’s for tollin’ me drugs will be cheaper
aif ter the war.”
“Tenaculum”: About decimals (B.
12/8/T5). How long will it be before the
clumsy and cabalistic British mode of pro-
scribing medicines gives place to the vastly
more simple, uniform and accurate Conti-
nental method? Components are given in
decimals of a gramme, and in ordinary
figures, thus dispensing with our archaic
quantity-signs and the Roman notation, ad-
mitting of infinitely minute subdivision, and
practically eliminating chances of error.
Thus 1.0 stands for 1 gramme (roughly,
15| grains) : 0.1 for 1 -10th gramme (14
grains) ; 0.01 for 1-100th gramme (l-6tli
grain), and so on. The decimal points—to
make surer —are represented by a strong,
perpendicular line running from top to bot-
tom of the prescript.
"Billy Blue”: Apropos Brickfield Hill
and Sydney’s old hay market (B. 12/8/ 15).
The old market was on the block facing
George-street, between Campbell and Hay
streets. An ugly old barn faced George-
street, built in the first half of the year
1834. of red bricks under a shingled root.
The hay carts were marshalled in six rows
extending back to Pitt-street. From 1 itt-
street to Castlereagh-street were a set ol
stock saleyards. One of the pens, at the
corner of Campbell and Pitt streets, was the
public pound. In 1875 the George-street
building, having lost its use, was demolished,
and the clock in the tower, which ran up in
the front of the old barn, was removed and
now shows a smiling face from the summit
of the fish-market in Woolloomooloo. The
weighbridge, by the way, has been offered
to the Australian Historical Society as the
first machine of the kind erected in Aus-
tralia.
“Tekelo”: Amidst the present blither
about “capturing German trade,” suppose
we have a look at things as they are. To-
day Sydney is warehousing many accumu-
lated tons of copra, awaiting freights to
Europe. There are also large stocks at
Island depots. Anything wrong with Aus-
tralia turning those cocoanuts into butter?
Modern margarine is as nutritious as butter
and almost as palatable. It costs only half
the price. The Australian prejudice against
margarine lias been not altogether without
reason, seeing that most of the stuff hitherto
retailed in the Commonwealth has been the
product of animal fats. But margarine
made from vegetable oils is in another class
altogether. Let the copra be used in mak-
ing this wholesome and nutritious sort of
margarine, and there will at. once be an
exportable product available, with work and
wages for Australians.
One of the most encouraging symptoms is
the hysterical indignation of the Hun when-
ever he thinks of England. Some ass of a
paper wrote recently: “We confess the blood
flies to our heads when we consider the
wickedness of this shameless people.” Now
the Berlin correspondent of some other foam-
ing rag says of Bethmann-Hoilweg, tne self-
confessed swindler of Belgium, "It was as
if the Chancellor was filled with physical
loathing when he spoke of Sir Edward Grey’s
‘frivolity’ and ‘blood-guilt.’ ” People don’t
go on like that when they have looked at
their cards and see a winning hand.
“Masarcus”: Mention by “Stephen Me”
(B. 12/8/T5) of the black boy who fell 60ft.
from aloft on the training ship Nelson re-
calls a fall by Lock Ward, now shipping re-
porter on the West Australian, from the
yard of a sailing vessel upon which he was
an apprentice. No man ever tore through
(;oft. of atmosphere more rapidly. Ward
landed on deck feet first, but he couldn’t have
done much more damage to himself if he
had arrived any other way. Months of hos-
nital and the end ot a seataring career were
his reward. Another meteoric drop, unat-
tended by ratal results, occurred at the ( utrio
buildings, in Adelaide, some years ago, when
a youth fell headfirst on to the roadway, 40ft.
below. Tie liad leaned out to keep an eye on
a street-fight. Injuries—-broken leg, collar-
bone and sundry ribs; six months hospital.
And he missed the most interesting part of

the man w i lu dropped 130 ft. from a new
building in Sydney, broke his journey in a
net . wor k of telephone wires, and thence

‘ Billy blue : Apropos Judge Callaghan,
whose widow has just died near Sydney
(B. 10/8/ lo). The Judge was one of the
earliest promoters of Australian manufac-
tures. In 1844, vlmn lie published,
ia tvro volumes, a collection of the
Acts and ordinances in torce at that
date. ~ with notes, he had the whole
thing printed from type made in Sydney,
and bound in Sydney by Australian workmen
using material produced in the colony. lie
exhibited a set at the London Exhibition of
1851, and was awarded a bronze medal. It
was probably the first prize ever given at a
big exhibition for a factory product from
Australia.

THE Overland is a car of remarkable
safeness and endurance because it has
extraordinary strength in every part.
We supply forgings in the Overland at
many points where castings are used in
other cars. Forgings are stronger and
lighter. They cost the average smaller
manufacturer so much more that he cannot
afford to use them. They cost us no more
because we make so many Overland cars
that we can afford to own and operate our
own drop forging plant.
In many cars the front axle is made of
two pieces, welded together. The front
axle of the Overland is drop forged from
a single piece. This greatly increases
strength.
In like manner, the Overland is
designed and built throughout to withstand
greater strains than it will ever receive. It
is not an “assembled ” car, made of parts
built by various other manufacturers.
Almost every part of the Overland is built
by The Willys Overland Company. Its
strength and unusual endurance have been
amply proved by years of use on the worst
of roads.
The Overland is on display at the local
show rooms.

“How I Fooled the Beauty Doctors.
Removed all Wrinkles Over Night.”
After Plasters, Creams and Appliances, Steaming Pots, Massage, Prescriptions,
Apparatus, and all Advertised Methods Had Absolutely Failed.
A Remarkable Interview who for the first time re vealB to Sydney
“Bulletin” Readers Full Particulars of Her Recent Discovery. Nothing lake ,t Mas Ever
Used or Even Heard of Before.
By BLANCHE GORDON.
It lias remained for a woman unskilled in
scientific research to solve, quite by acci-
dent, the problem that has baffled all
science and the entire medical profession
the world over. In a recent interview
Mine. Lawton explained to the writer
why no one need patronise expensive
Beauty specialists now, for any woman can
easily accomplish, in the strict privacy ot her
own home, the seeming miracles which aie
causing physicians and Beauty experts
everywhere to stand aghast in startled amaze-
For the first time Mine. Lawton has gener-
ously consented to reveal how all the network
of lines and furrows left by ten years ot ill-
ness, worry and care were swept from her
face in a single night, as though by the wand
of some fabled
magician. Slie
explained t o
me the embar-
rassment and
humiliation she
felt when her
fa c e became
seared and
seamed by my-
riads of dis-
f i g u ring
wrinkles, how
deep creases
appeared where
once the dim-
ples were, and
li o w she,
though still
young, had the
li e a rtrending
experience of
being looked
up o n wi t h
pity, as a pre-
maturely o 1 d
woman totally
devoid of all
personal at-
tract iveness.
For, after all,
the w o r 1 d
judges a wo-
her appearance, and if her face remains y <)U * h" [
ful the mere passing of years means notnin„
to her. Hut if her face proclaims her old a |
pitiless world sees only the disfiguring marks
of Time and old age, so bids her step aside
for a younger generation.
Mine. Lawton described with deep feeling
the sadness with which she thus viewed the
passing of her own youth, and said it was tue
most bitterly tragic period ot her whole life.
She was able to afford any expense, so could
command the services ot the best specialists m
the land, and she frantically tried massage,
which stretched the skin still more, electrical
treatments that were torture, medicines that
made her ill, suction cups, masks, irritating
lotions and worthless advertised methods
sralore.’ After wasting a fortune in this way,
however, she was compelled to give up in abso-
lute despair.
Imagine, then, her ecstasies of joy and de-
light, the indescribable relief that filled her
soul to overflowing, when, quite by accident,
she one day discovered an almost incredibly
simple process, involving a totally new scien-
tific principle, which quickly banished every
trace of line or wrinkle from her face. Light
lines and erowsfeet disappeared within an
m
I
hour, in a single night the deepest creases were
gone, and in less than a week her face was
soft, clear and smooth as a young child s.
Mine. Lawton found a way to tighten the
loose, sagging, wrinkled skin, and to build up
Ann healthy tissue underneath where the
wrinkles were, so the skin rested on an even
surface and became virtually wrinkle-proof.
Now after nearly two months, I saw with my
own eyes the face of smooth, delicate contour,
the beautiful, velvety skin and complexion like
a rose, where formerly were the deep lines and
furrows that added twenty years to her age.
To describe the method here in full detail
-would somewhat overrun the space allotted to
me for this article, but Mine. Lawton explained

from lier ac-

in a small

ited number of

to be printed,
ha d just ar-

MAGNESIA FOR DYSPEPTICS.
SPECIALIST RECOMMENDS IT
INSTEAD OF DRUGS.
“Duly those in constant touch with suffer-
ers from indigestion and dyspepsia can fully
realise the harm done by the improper use of
drugs and artificial digestants,” remarked an
eminent specialist recently. “Personally I
rarely advocate the use of drugs in the treat-
ment of digestive or stomach troubles, for in
practically every instance I have proved the
underlying cause to be excessive acidity of
the stomach and consequent fermentation of
the food contents. Therefore, in place of the
once widely-used drugs I invariably recom-
mend the use of magnesia to neutralise the
acidity and stop the food fermenting, and
the wonderful results I have obtained during
| lie past three years convince me that there
is no finer treatment for indigestion, dys-
pepsia, etc., etc. It must, of course, be
clearly understood that I do not employ or
advise the use of such forms of magnesia as
citrates,, acetates, sulphates, etc.—these
would often do more harm than good; no-
thing but pure bisurated magnesia—the form
prescribed by physicians—should ever be
used to neutralise stomach acids. This is
not at all difficult to obtain —in fact, I find
that most chemists now stock the prepara-
tion in tablet form in addition to the ordin-
ary powder. Half a teaspoonful of bisurated
magnesia in powder form or two compressed
tablets taken with a little water after meals
will usually be found quite sufficient to in-
stantly' neutralise the acidity and prevent
food fermentation, thereby ensuring painless,
natural digestion for even chronic sufferers."*
POLITICAL
POINTS
POLITICAL SUICIDE BY AUTO-
BIOGRAPHY.
George Reid hopes to devote his leisure, during
his leave of absence from the High Commissionership,
to the completion of his autobiographical remini-
scences for publication by Cassell’s in May.— Cable.
GEORGE (reluctantly): “Well, here goes;
there seems to he nothing else for it.”
Much grief has been expended of late
over the revelation that many little children
affected with syphilis are treated at Sydney
hospitals. Quite right! But what about
the multitudinous Australian kid that has
not materialised because of the ravages
caused by this disease? The healthy child
we haven’t got must be counted in with the
smitten one we have; and if these in chorus
can't cry loud enough to get the attention
of Health. Ministers and their colleagues,
then politics are hopeless.
B. McC. Anderson’s report on the mis-
management in the P.M.G.’s department is
startling only to such as never do business
or try to do business with a huge Govern-
ment concern. A more staggering document
would be a report on the shortcomings of
the Defence Department, with a list of
commercial anecdotes attached. The tales
of the firms that solicit orders, obtain con-
tracts or get inquiries from the Depart-
ment make a mass of amazingly corrobo-
rative evidence. The Commonwealth De-
fence Department, like the Postal Depart-
ment, was erected on a heap of ancient
States’ muddles, and after the war it will
have to be pulled down and rebuilt on a
fresh foundation.
THE REPORT.
The Vienna Academy is taking advantage of the
presence of Russian prisoners of many faces to insti-
tute a special research into the various anthropologi-
cal characteristics of the different peoples. A pro-
fessor of anthropology has been voted £170 to pro-
secute the work.
DER HERR PROFESSOR: “A most degenerate
rave; nod a sign of kultur among dan,
and nod a man in der lod calable of gill-
ing a vomans or a paBy .”
B hen Finlayson, of Brisbane, urged in
the House of Heps, the other day that the
business affairs of the Defence Department
should be taken out of the hands of
military and naval men and entrusted to
business people, somebody promptly bel-
lowed that the Bananalander was “vilifying
men who were unable to defend themselves.”
The same clod was thrown at Orchard in
connection with another matter. If Finlay-
son is earnest and sincere he will take no
notice of assaults of this kind, and will refuse
to be howled down by party marionettes.
He will go on steadily affirming the grave
dangers of the present system and
insisting that a soldier’s business is to
fight and to train men to fight. As things
are now the soldier called on to run a huge
business concern is placed in an impossible
position, and the people are compelled to
run dreadful risks.
“Temporary” clerks are about as numer-
ous in the N.S.W. Public Service as in the
Postal Department, where Anderson, the
Government’s commissioner, tripped over
them everywhere. They are so frequent,
iu fact, and are alleged to be doing such
important jobs, and many of them are such
ancient institutions, that a bill has been
introduced to give them a status as per-
manent hands. If Parliament were game
to do it, the right thing would be to first
appoint a man of Anderson’s stamp to find
out how many thousands of men in the
public service do nothing worth . mentioning
except on pay-days, for the very good reason
that there is nothing for them to do. In
spite of the existence of a Public Service
Board, which is supposed to bar political
influence, there probably isn't a depart-
mental head who hasn’t had men unloaded
on to him, and who hasn’t sent them down
to the stores or some other convenient loaf-
ing place to get them out of the way.
N. S. Wales State taxation per head has
more than doubled in seven years; and that
is where a lot of it has dribbled.
“Bob” Anderson, the alert commercial man
who is telling the Federal Ministry things
about its departments, met numerous unex-
plained imported officials in the Defence
Department a few months ago, and has since
then been finding large numbers of even more
unexplainable imports in the Post Office. The
policy of the postal service seems to be to
discourage the training of its own engineers,
and then, when vacancies occur, to dash off
to another country and bring along men who
are strangers to local conditions.
The Arbitration Amendment Bill, which
has been passed by the Federal Par-
liament, puts the big A.W.U. and some other
large organisations right. Early this year
pastoralist Ivillen took a case to the courts
praying for the wiping-out of the A.W.U. as
a registered organisation. Though he failed
generally, the union was told that its rules
must be. amended so as to limit membership
as provided by the Arbitration Act. which
says, that members must be employees in the
particular industry covered by their union.
The amending Bill makes it possible to form
and register a composite union under the
Act, and thus permits the A.W.U. to hold
all it has.
The chairmanship of the new
committee of three, to investi-
gate and report on the whole
telegraph system of Australia,
goes to Broad, of Adelaide.
Laird, of Sydney, and Bennett,
of Ballarat, are the other two.
Broad is an organist and choir-
conductor in his leisure mo-
ments. He and Laird have
worked together on telegraph
matters before. The new com-
mittee starts its inquiries in
Sydney.

bition awards just received in
Australia . Thomas Hodgson
figures with an “honorable
mention” in the Transportation
Section for “models of motor-
car suspension springs.” Tom
Hodgson is a Fitzroy (Vic.)

of motor-cars and a mechanical
bent. When the first Austra-
lian shock-absorber was put on
the local market. Hodgson had
a look at it, and reckoned, lie
could do what the other in-

. 7 , , . a Pal going to the U.S.,
handed him his models and left the
rost Providence. Considering that
(he L.S. market, not to mention the F.S.
record of patents, is heavilv loaded with
bump-resisters and jolt-killers, Providence
seems to have managed its share of the busi-
ness rather well. Hodgson is an Australian
product himself.
GERMANY'S REPLY.
BERLIN, August 18, 1915.— Dear Uncle Sam, — In
reply to your note of some weeks ago, Yours as ever,
Germany.
The war and the need to suppress party
feeling did not prevent Joe Cook from attend-
ing a “Liberal Hally” at Parramatta; nor
did they stop him from saying to loud Liberal
applause that “Liberal and Labor principles
were being tested by the war, and Liberal
principles were winning out.” Winning out!
Of course they are! Liberalism fought
against the establishment of the Australian
navy and something approaching a Defence
system, and look how the war has justified
hostility to both those movements!
God is “strafing" one part of the British
Empire all right—the stupid part which was
always sending its jobs away and paying
somebody else to do them. Just now it is
the Lyttelton (M.L.) Harbor Board which is
frantically calling from the soup tureen.
STRENUOUS WORK.
Mr. Holman stated that the Government did not
propose to introduce a Toalisator Bill as a Govern-
ment measure.
THE COMFORTER: “ Brother, fear not, hut sit
tight.”
About IS months ago it ordered a 50-ton
crane from England, instead of handing the
job to one of the many excellent engineering
shops in Loan Land. But the contractors at
the other side had hardly started on the
thing when the War Office ordered them to
drop everything and get busy making cranes
for J. Bull. So Lyttelton will get its weight-
lifter “when the war is over” if it has any
money left after helping to provide local
unemployed engineers, fitters, etc., with soup
tickets.
Last week a beautiful piece of machinery
a 70-li.p. engine—was taken from work-
shops in Melbourne and fitted into an Aus-
tralian-made aeroplane at Point Cook
school, ami is now being given
its trial flights. When the idea
of making these things in Aus-
tralia was suggested, every
Stinking Fishite in the country
sneered. It was absurd; slap-
dash Australians couldn’t make
machines demanding such pre-
cision : they must be imported.
But Captain Harrison, of the
Flying School, thought other-
wise, and pressed his opinion.
Tarrants’ tendered for three
engines, made the necessary
drills, dies, cutters, etc. ; tested
metals and alloys ; made every
part in duplicate: tested each
duplicate to the breaking
strain, and finally turned out
an engine with the maximum
of strength and the minimum
of weight. And they say it
would be equally possible to
turn out machine guns.
Victoria’s new early-closing
law came into force on July 7.
and from that date to August
24 there were 1201 persons
charged with drunkenness at
Melbourne City Court as
against only 1006 for the same
period last year. This in-
crease of about 20 per cent,
looks too large to be accidental,
and seems to indicate that since
the grog sold after 9.30 is now
sly-grog, it is the bad and ex-
cessive grog that sly-grog
almost always is. The most obvious
remedy is not to shut up all pubs
at six or eight or nine o’clock p.m..
but to shut up the mere drinking dens at
seven a.m. and keep them shut throughout
the 24 hours.
THE CURSE OF WILHELM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Aou who have taken your own small voice
For the thunder of God,
You who have made the battle your choice,
Till the blood-drenched sod
Cries from the east and west a curse so loud
That it falls on your ear,
Menacing, out of the front of the grey death-cloud
With its burthen of fear—-
How long shall it be till the fires you lighted return?
If the grain of the world be scorched, shall the chaff
not burn?
For I heard a singing wind go by:
“Who draws the sword by the sword shall die!”
And another voice from a death-cold lip:
“Who stands in blood upon blood shall slip!”
And the dead years rolling took up Hie refrain:
“As it was with us shall it be again.”
Oh, an old, grey, dying man,
Tortured for the brutal mirth
Of your soldiers who o’erran
Ilis one rood of bloodstained earth,
Spake a curse a king might fear
More than gun or Cossack spear.
ou who have challenged the truth with a sword
Nor heeded the cry
Of those the feet of your battle horde
Crushed and passed by,
lou that the world's red lightnings seem to obov
For a mad, black hour,
1 orbing and orbing to make your greatness play
Through the hot blood shower,
How long shall it be till the full blind horror and
shame
Of the hate you wakened breathe on your blasted
fame?
For I heard a singing wind go by:
“In his traitor blood shall the traitor lie!”
And another voice from the sun-kissed height:
“The night for him who has mocked at light !”
And the dead years rolling took up the refrain:
“As it was with us shall it be again!”
Oh, a little Flemish child,
Dying for want- of food.
And a pale French girl , defiled
In the war-rush wild and rude,
Spake a curse you well may fear
More than gun or Cossack spear!
v.s.w,
DAVID McKEE MRICHT.

NOTIONS
Jeff Smith was ill-advised to drag his dis-
qualification into the Courts in Sydney. He had no
defence to the fouling charge. If he had, there was
about £50,000 to be picked up in libel damages
i from the innumerable papers which, practically
unanimously, denounced him for having deliberately
: and ‘ repeatedly hit Darcy low last month. There
are at least three recognised ways of losing a fight in
the U.S.A. when the pug is squared, frightened m or
satisfied that his chances are hopeless. The boxer
may quit, have his towel thrown in, or foul. Of
these by far the most popular; is to foul, since the
Yankee public has no use for the quitter in ahy
shape or form. Jeff Smith was as much horrified at
Darcy’s methods in their first fight as in their second.
The American fouled on both occasions. The first
time Harald Baker missed the blow and disqualified
Darcy for lying down ; the second time he saw it —
at the fourth attempt—and disqualified the Ameri-
can. That was the Stadium case in a nutshell, and
if it had failed it would have been good-bye to decent
boxing so far as Australia is concerned. The mis-
guided "Lippe faction tried to establish that the
Bakers, having imported Jeff Smith at great expense,
boomed him as a champion, and in general invested
money on him, proceeded then to enter into a con-
spiracy to destroy him as a money-maker, because
Harald Baker had once called Lippe “a swine,” or
words to that effect. The position is that fair referee-
ing has been vindicated ; that A] Lippe has been
given a chance to pour discredit on the Baker bro-
thers ; and that Jeff Smith has paid. So if the boxer
is satisfied no one else is likely to complain.
Fighting Billy Murray, who calls himself
“middleweight champion of the world,” meets Darcy
at Svdnev Stadium next Saturday night, and accord-
ing to the local Yankee section he will win. On
his form in his only appearance here he could not get
a decision in an amateur tourney at Newtown; but
it may well be that he was then badly out of sorts.
A marvellous alchemist is form or condition. Take
the Darcy-McGoorty fight. One still hears on all
sides stories tending to show that it was a hollow
fake, that the Australian was backed in the American’s
camp, and so forth. This may have been so, and
yet it does not prove that the fight was crooked. The
result was largely a matter of form. As this paper
has always contended, McGoorty is not a genuine
middle-weight. To get the weight the day before
the fight he had to be on the road for many hours.
His condition was probably known to his associates
for days before the contest. What wonder that they
laid against him ? The only engagement worth dis-
cussing between these two men would be at catch-
weights, It would draw the house of a century, and
McGoorty might win, though the awful hiding he
took from the Maitland youngster won’t have done
his nerve and confidence any good.
*'-.*.-.*•*
One might have known that Harry Stone
would not let the. greatest war in history come to
an end without getting an aflvt. out of it. The
lurid yarn which was cabled to Australia about his
having had two legs blown off by a German cruiser,
despite which handicap he sivam a quarter of a mile to
a waiting boat and then died, turns out to be
“dope.” The astute young Yid is as alive as ever,
and is ready to box anyone, but preferably Freddie
Welsh, to prove it,
Fred Kay beat Red Watson on points at
Sydney Stadium last Saturday night. Watson’s only
hope of success lay in knocking Kay out, and, the
latter being about as elusive as a seagull, he failed
to do it. Watson, was dug up at the last moment,
Billy Kramer, who was billed to fight, having fled,
without warning, aboard the Sonoma. This practice
of suddenly bolting on the eve of combat is becom-
ing chronic with a certain class of pug. One neuro-
path left for the Mountains in similar circumstances,
and was not found for a week ; another departed, as
Kramer did, and has been cursing this country in
the U.S.A. ever since.
* * * *
Melbourne Stadium staged a very willing
and entertaining lightweight contest last Saturday
night, with Leo Coyle and Jim Duggan as principals.
The pair fought every inch of the full 20 rounds,
and each led in turn. Coyle, at 9st. 1241 b., had an
advantage of 21b. ; also he had more science, and was
in better condition. On the other hand, Duggan was
equipped with a heap of strength, a heavy punch,
and the characteristic ginger of a Duggan. He
fought for a knockout, and in the eighth round
nearly accomplished his desire with a solid left to
the face-hinge. Coyle survived, and, as Duggan tired
from the thirteenth, took command and wore down
the enemy, while the house deputationised Providence
to furnish him (Coyle) with a woodener that the
sacrifice might be stretched for the count-out of the
presiding high-priest. The prayer went unheeded, so
the high-priest took matters into his own hands, and
flattened Coyle out on points.
Harold Hardwick is being tempted to
Maoriland bj' an offer of cash from the Northern
Boxing Association of Auckland to meet A. Pooley,
the M.L. champion heavyweight, for the title. The
shindy is expected to take place at an early date.
Cecil Hartt writes from somewhere between
here and Constantinople: “Everybody in our crowd
is ‘stouslr’-struck. When we have a concert ‘stoush’
is the principal item. Last night we had a sing-
song, but those who weren’t actually bribed to cut
out their turns heard all about it. I did some
lightning sketches under difficulties, and managed
to do. a get-away before the yell of ‘No more!’
shook the ship. It’s the mere -truth to say that it’s
quite fashionable to wear boxing gloves at meals
and discolored eyes between times.”
If the Webb-Hannan sculling match for the
Maoriland championship on the Wanganui River on
September 26 takes place, it will be very different
in one feature from the race between Webb and
Arust for the world’s championship on the same
stream. Fogwell, of N. S. Wales, was Arnst’s philo-
sopher and guide on that occasion, and during the
struggle he was perched up on the mast of the
umpire’s steamer, wherefrom he had a great advan-
tage in signalling his principal how to keep the
course. That has been specially provided against in
the Webb-Hannan articles. It is expected that
Charlie Towns will challenge the winner of the
event.
McGREGOR.
Carlton's centre
(Vic.).
Recent recruits include two fine Sydney
scullers —Tom Rodrick (Enterprise) and R. Symons
(Leichhardt). Rodrick holds about the longest amateur
rowing record in Australia, including No. 3 in
N.S.W. 1913 crew, a seat in two championship
eights, two championship fours, seven senior fours,
senior eights, and a host of others. Symons’s log
shows a place at the bow for N.S.W. in 1913 and
1914, and in two senior eights.
The Victorian Amateur Athletic Associa-
tion, which controls foot-running, cycling, boxing
and wrestling in the Cabbage State, has practically
shut up shop for the time being. So many of the
best amateur cross-country and track runners are
at the Front and in camp that championships run
for the “left” behinds” would be unfair when not
farcical. The big 10 miles C.C.C., which was won
for the first time by E. M. Wilson in 1892, and at
which 300 starters have faced the barrier, is held
over for the first time since that date.
Altogether about 840 names are on the
list of men at the Front or in training compiled by
H. D. Smith, sec. of the V.A.A.A. _ Amongst them
are .a number who have shone in Australasian
championships. For instance, H. H. Hunter, ex-
100yds. and long jump (killed in action) ; L. H.
Kelly, high jumper; L. R. Walker, pole-vaulter;
R. H. Watson, 1-mile flat and 1-mile hurdles ; and G.
A. Wheatley, half and one mile. State champions at
wrestling, boxing, running and other field sports are
also on the roll, which is lengthening.
* * *■ K
R. F. Cripps, who won the school cup and
championship at Newington College (Sydney) on
Saturday, is a promising athlete. He got away
with the firsts in the 100yds., 220yds., 440yds. and
broad jump; seconds in 120yds. hurdles and high
jump ; and third in SBoyds.
*- *- *•
R. J. A. Massie, the Sydney athlete, has
made another record. Two former claims to fame
were that he was Australia’s best fast bowler and
N. S. Wales’s champion heavyweight amateur boxer.
To these he has added the fact that he has been
wounded for the third time while fighting in Galli-
poli.
* * * *
Rugby Union play in Sydney came to an
end for the season with a knock-out competition on
the Sports Ground on Saturday ; and in face of the
fact that there has been more military exercise by
Unionists than football practice during the past
months, the standard of plav was surprisingly good.
Each game lasted two 10 minutes’ spells; and where
scores were even, the side which forced down the
oftener to save took the knock-out. This put u
premium on speed and daring 1 , and wouldn’t do the
R.U. game any harm if it were permanently intro-
duced. Eastern Suburbs and Mosman played off
the final, the former winning try to nil.
The N.S.W. League commenced a series of
games for the Sydney Oity Cup. The principal
scramble was between Eastern Suburbs and South
Sydney on the Agricultural Ground, and resulted in
the occasionally disappointing Southerners being
handed down by 0 points to 3. The big yell of the
afternoon was when the fly-away Horder snatched a
pass in his home lines, and, flashing through the
Eastern Suburbs’ backs, delivered himself of a try
at the opposite end of the field. Other games: Glebe
33, North Sydney 12 ; Newtown 24, Western Suburbs
C ; Balmain 17,” Annandale 3. This leaves Glebe,
Balmain, Eastern Suburbs and Newtown in the semi-
finals.
* X- * *
N.S.W. Rugby League 1.915 premiership
siftings : Balmain’s undefeated run in 14 matches (two
drawn) and its win in three grades go on the slate as a
double record. Best team scores: Glebe, 58 tries ;
Balmain, 50 goals. Highest individual scorers: P.
Burge (Glebe), 19 tries; A. Bolewski (Glebe), 30
goals; W. Messenger (E. Suburbs), five tries, 29
goals (73 points), highest double effort. Balmain,
232 points for and'7l against, has been bettered only
three times in eight years’ premiership matches, and
the difference in the f. and a. points (161) has been
beaten but twice in the same period. Worst record
for season: N. Sydney, 12 games lost- out of 14;
points scored, 83 ; points lost —but this is no time
to raise dissension amongst ourselves.
Shocking weather, combined with the
probability that Carlton would easily down Mel-
bourne, spoilt the attendance at the Vic. League first
semi-final on Saturday, and a mere 14,413 people,
worth £382, passed the turnstiles. Yet the game
was a cheerful interlude in a drab week, and showed
the “home” club at its best, kicking off the mud
accurately and working the ball through the goal
with neatness and despatch. In the first term the
lied Legs ran up 10 points more than Carlton; at
the interval they were four points in arrear ; at the
last change they had three points in reserve, but by
a series of determined rushes the Blues swept over
them, and won by 11 points—ll goals 12 behinds to
10 goals 7 behinds. The stronger team won, but
the other was far from outclassed. Under “Dookie”
IvlcKenzie’s training Melbourne has improved out of
knowledge. Still, you can't build a premier 18 in
one season. The club has gained this much,
though ; it hasn’t been so near the top of the com-
petition tree since 1900, when it was first.
* * * *
The shutters went up on Queensland
Rugby League premiership last week, when Western
defeated Bulimba by 13 points to seven.
Half-time scores were five all; but in the second
spell Wests made mincemeat of the opposition, and
won with six points to spare.
Maoriland heard of the death of three of
its international footballers last week at the Front.
One was the strenuous and burly forward, Charlie
Savory, whom Eastern Australia knew best as a
member of an Australasian League team that toured
England. At one time lie was amateur heavyweight
champion boxer of Maoriland. Another to go was
T. M. Grace, a half-caste, who played in Sydney
with one or two M.L. teams. Finally, J. E. Cuthill,
of Otago, an exceptionally dashing back player (five-
eighths, centre or full). He was a member of the
M.L. team that cleaned up California in 1913.
Probably because football is ‘‘in,” aud his
absence is thus made conspicuous, the toeballer
moving Gallipoiiwards from Australia has been get-
ting all the limelight. Contrariwise, cricket being
“out,” the departing player of that game has re-
ceived less notice than he deserves. But that has
not deterred him from doing his share, and over 750
representatives of the N. S. Wales Junior Cricket
Association are honorably mentioned as having shoul-
dered arms. A spirited appeal has now been issued
by the committee for further recruits from the junior
ranks: “The call comes to us very clear and decisive
. . . .the example has been set for all free men to
emulate. In this dire hour of peril we feel sure that
our juniors will supplement the already magnificent
effort and crown themselves with much honor and
glory.” Good talk!
* * tt *
R. H. Spooner, the Lancastrian amateur
batsman (according to Australian Eleven men one
of the best they ever met), is engaged in hitting the
Germans to leg in Flanders. Spooner and F. S.
Jackson were the two great players who would never
visit Australia, "and no English team was thoroughly
representative without them. The former has played
a fine game at the Front, and is already Captain
Spooner, not out.
Eric Benjafield, a Hobart cricketer, has
made seven attempts to enlist, but ha%jbeen turned
back by the doctor each time. He says lie’ll get
where he’s made up his mind to go if he has to wear
out every stubborn doctor in the Service.

“Snaffle”: Poaching, mentioned in The
Bulletin (19/8/’l5) as the flier of the North or
thereabouts, and the winner of the fat Metropolitan
Handicap, was picked up for £4O. But that was
nothing to the bargain that Rue Victoria, the winner
of the last Brisbane Cup, was. That speed-beast was
given away with a pound of tea, in a manner of
speaking. Eight pounds was the actual price. Also,
Ngatoa, the horse that won the Grand National
Steeples in Maoriland this year, was bought by a
man in Auckland at an auction sale for £l5. E. W.
Alison, a prominent sport of that town, saw him,
liked his looks, and paid £4OO for him on the spot.
Then he won the biggest steeplechase stake in the
Loanlv Isles.

“Outsider”: Though we get the tote to-
morrow, don’t let us scorn the bookmaker utterly.
In a sense we should all he “bar Vons” now.
Considering the smallness, of the popula-
tion of Maoriland some pf. the racing clubs splash
money around in an amazing fashion. During the
past season the Auckland R. O. handed out £41,450
in stakes, which is' an increase of £5710 over any
previous season. It also shelled out £1856 to vari-
ous local war funds—all the result of employing the
betting machines for which the Holman Govt, and the
bookmakers have so much brotherly hatred.
That great horse Stepniak holds the
Maoriland record as the sire of the greatest number
of winners of place money in any one season. Twice
he was represented by over 60 places. The stallions
who have sired over 30 winners in a season in the
history of the M.L. turf are: Stepniak 66, Soult 56,
Birkenhead 51, Merriwee 41, St. Leger 36, Martian.
36, Charlemagne 11. 34, Vanguard 33, and Seaton
Delaval 32.
“.Twosp” : A fine array of sevens attracted
shoals of backers to the right tote ticket at the
latest big trotting meeting in Christchurch (M.L.).
In the Speedway Handicap, the seventh on the pro-
gramme, it was discovered that of the 14 horses
entered seven had been ineligible, seven had been
handicapped, seven accepted, and seven started.
Waterlog, No. 7, on the 7sec. mark, was too good a
thing to miss in the face of that combination. He
romped home, and returned handsome profits.
Tasmanian 1914-35 racing season figures-
-270 events were run on 48 days; stake-money’
£10,695; George Bennett (18 races) heads the win-
ning owners with £ll6l, C. Youl (£800) being
next best; totalisator turnover, £167,641. The
Appleland season’s labors put together don’t come
up to the A.J.C. Spring Meeting at Randwick, at
which £29,550 will be paid away in 24 races.
Tasmania has dropped into line with the
other State clubs and added a Derby to its list of
racing fixtures. The event— £ 5 sweepstake and
£2OO added money—will be run over a mile and a
quarter on Boxing Day, and only three-year-olds
domiciled in Tassy at least three months before the
race will be eligible.
The world’s motor maniacs, who are striv-
ing- to drive 500 miles in five hours, almost did the
trick in the Chicago Derby, with which the new
Chicago Speedway was recently opened. D. ltesta
got home three minutes ahead of Porporato in the
staggering- time of shrs. 7min. 26sec. It was a
hair-raising struggle, the second man taking shrs.
lOmin. 50sec. and the third shrs. llmin. ssec. The
wooden surface proved a great success, being splen-
did to drive on and exceptionally easy on tyres.
Six of the cars to finish did not change a tyre in
the 500 miles. Resta, who stopped five times,
showed phenomenal speed when running. He aver-
aged 97-} miles per hour, and brought the feat of
driving at 100 miles an hour for five hours within
the realm of possibility. Someone will probably
accomplish it before the racing season closes in
U.S.A.
Resta’s whiz is absolutely- the world’s best
to date. It is a full nine minutes inside the time
put up by himself, Guinness and Chassagne when
they- took turns at the wheel of a Sunbeam on
Brooklands track in May, 1914, and fractured the
500 miles and 1000 miles records. The Chicago race
was particularly sultry- from the jump:—

And still the world cries: “Faster!”
The curtain lias just rang down on the
slowest bowling season that N.S.W. has experienced
—hardly to be wondered at, considering the war.
However, the old chans showed their patriotism by-
settling- an example in generosity. All the kids
under 40 were shooed off the greens to carry a rifle,
knit socks or peel spuds for soldiers, and the balance
collected or stole 10,000 sovereigns to help pour balm
on the wounded who return or assist the relatives of
those who don't.
* * * *
The wrestling game struggles on among
the coal-miners of the N.S.W. South Coast to keep
what is practically its last hold on Australian soil.
Down Wollongong way regular bouts of more or less
minor importance are maintained. Last week the
bill was a match between A. Holmer, as the light-
weight champion of the Commonwealth, and O.
Glover, ex-champion of Queensland, under catch-as-
catch-can conditions. The first session went half an hour
without a fall, but in the.next two sets falls went to
Holmer in seven and 20 minutes respectively.
Tom Tracey, N.S.W. amateur champion
road cyclist, put up a great ride in the Dunlop
teams’ .championship semi-final at dusty Homebush o:i
Saturday-, when he covered 24 miles in 64min. 27sec.
—4min. ahead of the ether wheelers in the heat and
nearly smin. faster than W. Lee’s time in the first
heat. It was a splendid effort against wind and filthy,
conditions.
Tom Curran, ex-superintendent of Vic-
torian police, died at Hawthorn last week,
a young man at 75. He had been in the
police-force for 36 years when he retired on
a pension, and enjoyed life for 15 years
after retirement. There was a reason for
his failure to pine away on leaving his job.
He never took too much armchair exercise,
but was- always ready to act on a sports
committee or “start something” for a good
cause, and thus he retained a good grip on
life.
“Optic”: Another big steamer, badly
holed, is ashore on Bramble Cay (Torres),
and somebody will be required to
explain. No other frequented waterway in
the world is so bristling with dangers,
except maybe the Dardanelles; and the
Commonwealth Lighthouse Department,
which was going to do so much there, has
done decimal nought. The Lighthouse Act was
passed in 1911, and though the Government
has not forgotten to grab the dues imposed
by its provisions, and though the payment
of officials’ salaries has been promptly
and religiously attended to, the luckless
mariner, groping along the dimly-lighted,
badly-marked Barrier Reef with half a
million in property under his sea boots, has
been unanimously overlooked. With the
war in full blast, no one expects to see a
dado of first-class lights spring up along the
N.Q. coast; but as the said war doesn’t stop
the Federal Government from collecting over
£lOO on every big vessel that goes through
the Straits, it is not unreasonable, to ask
for some of the much-needed protection.
A FORBIDDEN SPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Ludus” writes: There is a movement to restrict
football, cricket, bowls, golf, racing, boxing and
drinking. Might I suggest that everybody connected
with these sports should voluntarily cut down things
one-half.— Sydney Evening News.
I’d love to get dreadfully, terribly tight!
I’d love to go out on the spree,
And reel home with crayfish some still summer night
Proclaiming my infantile glee!
1 am splendidly upright and honest and true,
And I’ve never been out on a burst;
But I don’t in the least mind confessing to you
That I’m short of the requisite thirst.
I do not set up for a model—not me!
A saint is a thing I despise.
No, I only refrain from the jag and the spree
On account of my stomach and eyes.
There are times when I’m mad, and I’d love to be
drunk,
To escape from myself and dull care ;
But on every occasion from drink 1 have slunk
When my liver has bellowed “Beware!”
Thus I amble alone on the lemonade track,
Stopping only for dishes of tea,
While the drunk howls with rage at my wowserish
knack
Of avoiding the alcohol he
Finds so good for his heart, and his liver and mind,
And he hates me for shunning Three Star.
If he only once guessed how 1 long to get “blind,”
And to blither at large in a bar !
It is rotten to be a compulsory saint,
The .poor slave of one’s stomach and eyes!
If I only could drink and rise up fresh as paint
To contend' with the sober and wise !
But I can’t do these things! I’m -a' poor,-puny
thing! j
When my thirst has been quenched I must yield.
While the heroes drink on till they blither and sing,
I retire like a beast of the field.
N.S.W.
THE RELIGJOUS EDITOR.

The Millaquin Sugar Co. (Brisbane) :
To Ke- Re-
June, Profits. Dividends. serves. serves.
1912. . £55,892 6 p.c. = £30,000 £25,892 £25,892
1913.. 30,394 5 „„ 25,000 5,394 31,280
1914.. 72,441 S „ ~ 40,000 32,441 03,727
1915.. 47,401 8 „ ~ 40,000 7,401 71,128
Among the big properties left on the Q.X.
Bank when the tide went out were Milla-
quin and Yengarrie, of Bundaberg. The
estates might have been cut tip and jobbed
off: but the mills, distilleries and other asset s
and interests were much more difficult
wreckage to salve. The bank nursed them
for years, and things gradually came the
bank's way. Sometimes you hear people
expressing surprise that general-manager
Ralston should have so much truck with
Prime Minister Fisher and the Labor party.
But can you wonder at itV It was the most:
violent anti-Labor party that Queensland
ever knew (and that is saying a good deal)
which smashed the Q.N. Bank and half the
other concerns in the State ; and, years after-
wards, the abolition of the kanaka, which
almost everybody but the Laborites declared
would ruin the Queensland sugar industry,
was followed by prosperity such as the in-
dustry had never known before.
Having seen the business restored to a
highly profitable position, so that they could
capitalise it at a respectable figure, Ralston
and his directors decided to put Millaquin
and Yengarrie into a separate co., no doubt,
with the object of gradually unloading the
shares. The announcement was made in the
beginning of 1911 and a few months later
the co. was formed in 50,000 shares of £lO
each, all issued to the Q.N. Bank, with
another 25,000 in reserve. I luring the first
year the co. bought Hummock and Winder-
mere plantations and Windermere mill from
the Bank of N. S. Wales for £75,500. and
that deal put the co. into debt. (It had
started off scratch.) But partly by the sale
of the Windermere mill and accessories (on
which the co. reckons it made a profit of
£6553 in 1914), the whole of this debt has
now been wiped out. Here is a comparison :
June, 1912. June, 1915.
Assets other than fixed assets
and plant .. .. .. £165,473 £173,725
Liabilities 36,160 24,875
Surplus £129,313 £145,850
So approximately the co. has added £19,500
to its more or less liquid assets in the three
years, besides making additions to plant and
paying for whatever it has hung on to of the
properties it lias purchased. Meantime the
div. has averaged 6 : ;- p.e. p.a. Certainly one
can’t tell from the foregoing whether much
or little property has been sold, just as one
can't tell from the published Profit and Loss
account whether deoreciation has been
allowed for apart from the reserved profits.
On the face of it, however, it looks all right.
If the co. could last year, which was a very
poor year, make 91 p.c. after providing for
all ordinary replacements and renewals, it
doesn't look as though the thing was over-
capitalised to start with. This current
year will be lean, too; but things will be
somewhat improved for the poor sugar-
grower and miller by the commandeering of
the 1915 crop at a figure in excess, it seems,
of the price at which the Commonwealth
Government thinks the goods should be sold.

The PETiioi.ETJM Would, of London, pub-
lished in the interests of the oil rings, heads
its leading article for July, “What Shall We
Do With ItV" and proceeds to discuss the.
future of the Papuan oilfields which the
editor ‘looks forward to with some anxiety” !
The Commonwealth Government has spent
about £lO,OOO in locating petroleum in
Papua, and quite properly has forbidden
private exploiters to interfere with the de-
velopment of the new oil territory. This
organ of the oil rings now suggests that
“only the Standard Oil. the Dutch-Shell
Co., Lord Cowdray's group, or the Burmak
Oil Co. can possibly properly develop this
great oil-bearing area.” The chief thing to
be thought of. it seems, is “our Imperial
need.” And what about Australia’s imperi-
ous need of oil for her own navy and her
own mercantile marine? “The British Em-
pire and Sir Marcus Samuel want just such
an oilfield as we believe Papua will be.” Of
course they do —especially Sir Marcus
Samuel, of the Dutch-Shell Co., which is
already paying 27-1 p.c. per annum! “What
shall we do with it?” Come to think of it,
vvliat shall ire do with the PETROLEUM
World? And with Sir .Marcus Samuel's
Borneo and other concessions? The ques-
tion, if Australia asked it seriously, would
be no more impudent than the one the
Petroleum World propounds.

To the end of May the Broken Ilill Prop.’s
iron and steel plant at Newcastle had cost
£1,237.500. and the land and buildings
another £46.000. “The whole of the output
of the plant to Ihc end of August, 1916, has
already been disposed of.” the directors say.
“the orders including rails for the Federal
Government and the Governments of N. S.
Wales, Vic. and S. Alls.” In spite of the
war. this well-organised co. ran everything
to time-table. “It. is most pleasing to be
able to report,” says general-manager Del-
prat. “t hat each plant started at its proper
time, without hitch of any kind, and at once
commenced smooth and continuous work.”
And there is this good word for the Austra-
lian workman: —
Regular work is now proceeding in all departments.
Although the work is new to most of the men they
have shown remarkable adaptability and intelligence
in acquiring the necessary skill for operating the
various appliances.
But that is characteristic of Del prat, who
is less nervous about the Australian working
man than 99 out of every hundred bosses
you can meet. It is an advantage sometimes
not to have been born British.

In view of the excellent market Meudell has

An Australian Invention.
History of the “Rawson Moody" Fence.
EW Australian inventions have won
1 so much favor in so short a period as
has the Rawson Moody All-Steel
Fence. Three years ago it lived only in the
live minds of two men ; to-day. in its finished
state, it encloses hundreds of acres of farm
lands and scores of acres of country and
suburban homes. And its popularity is
rapidly and widely growing. The West
Australian farmer hastens no less rapidly
than his brother farmer of Queensland and
of New South Wales and Victoria, to speak
in high terms of praise of what is undoubt-
edly a fine Australian achievement. Like
all important and substantial inventions, the
fence has evolved from small beginnings.
Rut, fortunately, the fence was lucky in its
early friends. I'erhaps it was tin 1 wise
foresight of Mr. Moody. At any rate, those
who were invited to see the first finished
article are now its finest advertisers. For
on the completion—after very exact care —
of the first twenty chains of fencing Mr.
Moody went to the trouble and expense of
giving a thoroughly practical demonstration
of it to representatives of the different Aus-
tralian Governments, farming associations
and town-planning bodies. Whether it was
this careful handling or whether the value
of the fence was immense and important,
certain it is that the sales have exceeded all
expectation. So true is this that Mr.
Moody, in addition to his large workshop in
Kent Street, has now purchased a big tract
of land in the suburb of Ruslicutter’s Bay, in
which a larger and more comprehensive work
factory will he built. At the Kent Street
works interested and intending purchasers
may see the fence as it appears when erected
on farm and suburban areas. And no matter
whether a sale be effected or not, everyone
who sees the invention is forced to admit
that the day is not far distant when it will
enclose the land of almost every careful
farmer in Australia.
Untiring and enthusiastic, yet withal
intensely practical, Mr. Rawson Moody
talks interestingly of his invention. But
this is scarcely true, for he carefully explains
that the germ of the idea was supplied by
a friend from New Zealand, who persuaded
him to make steel droppers. For these
there was a tremendous sale. Then followed
steel standards and straining-posts. Again
there was a rush of buyers. Not satisfied,
Mr. Moody played with the idea of an
entirely all-steel fence to take the place of
the old wooden structures on farms and
smaller properties. But this was not a pro-
ject to be taken up in a day. There was the
question of price, and of ease of erection.
The new fence would require to be stronger
in every way. It would require to defy rust,
and it would need to be everlasting. To oust
tlu- old fence the new article Would have to
be guaranteed. The process of the straining
of wires would require to be simplified, and
when once they were in their place it would
need to be a simple matter to keep the wires
tightly and strongly placed. Also a light-
weight fence was an essential. And, im-
portant beyond all, the straining-post had
to be a big consideration. After two years
of studious care the whole of these difficul-
ties were overcome. And two years ago
Mr. Moody offered for sale his perfected
article. The result has been magnificent.
During those two years of planning and
devising, buyers were searching the world
over for the requisite materials. From all
manner of different metals the right kinds
were selected. To-day these, are mostly
manufactured in Australia. Evidencing the
strict care taken by Mr. Moody, it need only
be said that no fewer than four hundred
patent fencing records were closely searched
to avoid mistakes and entanglements.
Experts agree and practical users admit
that this all-steel invention has no equal for
price, durability, strength, simplicity and
effectiveness. The seven important features
which it is said lift this fence above all
others are: —
(1) The price is less than that of any
other—a man and a boy can erect half a
mile a day.
(2) Its weight is only three tons to the
mile, as against 10 tons per mile for the
wooden article.
(3) The straining-post, which is recog-
nised by all as the life of a fence, is made of
high-grade steed, and is for its weight marvel-
lously strong, and has no parallel. Properly
fitted in the ground it takes an octoous-like
grip with its anchor feet and stays, and will
stand any strain required in fencing.
(41 The standards or intermediate posts
are designed on original and simple lines.
There being only one slot for each wire,
which securely retains every wire, wedges,
clips, ties and staples are entirely done away
with, allowing the wires to strain through
readily in either direction. This gives a
self-locking action, as one half the wires lock
the other half. These, too. have anchor
feet, giving perfect uprights and true align-
ment.
(51 The dropper is considered by experts
lo be the simplest yet strongest thing of its
kind ever offered to the Australian market.
-V five-wire dropper can be put on the wires
permanently in as many minutes. This im-
provement. is also self-locking, the same
method being used as that of the standards.
This again allows the wires to readily strain
through in either direction.
(G 1 Another new design is the wire-
straining ratchet, which is simple and easy
in its operation, each wire being provided
with a ratchet, making it simple to tighten
or slacken the wire as desired from time to
time.
(71 Another strong point is that any
standard brand of good fencing-wire is suit-
able for use in the straining-posts, standards
or droppers.
Air. Mocdv claims that the farmer of
Australia, like his prototype of England.
Belgium. France or America, will one day
fence his farm with a view not only to
strengthen his fences, but also to beautify
his property. 'Which means that the ugly,
cumbersome old wooden fence must go. The
neater, immensely stronger and much more
reliable Rawson Moody All-Steel Fence
will take its place.
Write for catalogues of illustrations and
complete information to Rawson Moody
Manufacturing Co,, 343 Kent St., Sydney.

Dr B. Allen’s (USA)

GREY r
hair;
BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beauty,”
will be pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Queries should be accom-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, and directed to “Jeannette,” c/o “Before the Mirror,”
43 Gray’s Inn Road, London, W.C., England.
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over the
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few —the more stoical amongst
us —can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the birth-
right of every woman, gome of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present,
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Some Hints about the Hair.—An Excellent
Shampoo. —Few women realise how all im-
portant a really good shampoo is if the. hair
is to be kept in perfect condition. To shampoo
the hair properly, shake it down, comb it out,
then dip the fingers into a little pure olive or
almond oil. and thoroughly massage the scalp.
Then, mix a teaspoonful of stallax granules
in a cup of warm water. Fill a basin with
warm water, into which the ends of the hair
can fall, and-shampoo the stallax mixture into
the roots in the usual way. Rinse, and dry
by fanning, or iii the open air, if the weather
permits. Should the hair be exceptionally
greasy, the oil massage of the scalp should be
omitted. Stallax can be bought at any chem-
ist’s in quarter-pound sealed packages ; it keeps
indefinitely, and one package is sufficient for
twenty-five or thirty■ shampoos.
A Tonic for Thin or Falling Hair. —When the
hair falls unduly, or has become thin and im-
poverished, a tonic should be applied every
night for six or eight weeks. To make a
simple and inexpensive but most efficacious
lotion, at home, take a quarter of a pint of
bay rum and add to it one ounce of boranium.
Shake thoroughly, and leave for thirty
minutes, then strain, and add sufficient water
to make half- a pint. Every night, massage
the scalp for ten minutes, going over the
entire head, then dab the mixture into the roots
with a soft sponge. Regular brushing for a
few minutes every morning will also help to
improve the condition of the, hair.
The Question of Grey Hair. —The arrival of the
first grey liair is always a worry to a woman,
for, while silver hair is undoubtedly beauti-
ful. iron grey or streaked tresses are far
from being so. and they are not only unbe-
coming. but a sure tell-tale of age in these
days of eternal youth. It is not generally
known that one ounce of tammalite, procur-
able at the chemist’s, mixed with four ounces
of bay rum, makes a most satisfactory lotion
for restoring grey, hair to its original color.
All that is necessary is to apply the lotion
night and morning with a soft tooth-brush.
In a few days the hair will be found to be
gradually regaining its proper shade.
To Wave the Hair. —Never wave the hair with
hot irons—if you value the beauty of your
tresses. The heat makes the hair harsh and
brittle, and in the case of fair or auburn hair
entirely spoils the color, while it undoubtedly
1 astens the advent of grey hairs. To give a
wave to stra : ght hair it is only necessary to
damp it with silmerine, and either plait it
tightly, tying the ends, or arrange it in wav-
ing pins for a short time. A couple of ounces
will last quite a long time.
A Creasy Skin and Blackheads.—A greasy,
oily skin with blackheads and enlarged pores
is a sore trial, and many a girl goes through
life with her appearance entirely '.spoiled with
these unsightly blemishes, which may now be
so quickly and effectively removed with but
little trouble. To remove blackheads, and
the dull, dirty look of the skin which accom-
panies them, dissolve one stymol tablet in a
glass of hot water. Allow the effervescence
to subside, then dip a small, soft sponge into
the liquid and bathe the face. Leave on the
skin for a few minutes, then dry with a towel,
and the blackheads will come right off. For
a greasy skin, bathe the face tliree times a
week with stymol, and spray with cold water
every morning, using a vulcanite throat spray.
The Secret of a Good Complexion.—The secret
of a good complexion is so simple, that were
it only more widely known there would be
no such -thing as a rough, blotchy skin.
Nature lias decreed that the outer cuticle, when
it shrivels and dies, shall be removed, but so
many women fail -to realise this. To remove,
by absorption, the dead outer skin, and pre-
serve the complexion in all its pristine beauty,
mercolised wax is recommended; ordinary
creams and lotions being quite ineffectual for
this purpose. Smear the wax over the face
and neck, rub it gently into the skin, and
leave for the night. In the morning wash it
off, using a good soap, such as Pilehta, and
all the dead skin will he removed with the
wax. Then apply a lotion to remove any
trace of greasiness and brace up the skin,
making it firm and white. The lotion call be
made at home quite easily, by mixing one
ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of
hot Water. Bottle, and, when cool, apply to
the face with a soft sponge or the tips of the
fingers, allowing it to dry on the skin.
To Remove Superfluous Hair.—Superfluous
hair is the bane of many an otherwise pretty
woman’s life. Sometimes it is due to hereditary
tendencies; with others, it is the result of bad
soap or greasy face creams; but be the cause
what it may, the result is always disfiguring.
To remove the growth, apply a little powdered
pheminol. First dust the skin with powdered
alum, then mix the pheminol with cold water
to a paste, which should be spread on the
skin with a bone knife. Leave on for two
minutes, then wash off. About one ounce
should be sufficient for the most stubborn case.
Powdered pheminol can he obtained from any
reliable chemist, and its use not only re-
moves the growth but eventually kills the
roots permanently. [1

“Blank File”: I’m getting tired of that
little remark that will creep into the most
friendly conversation —“Why doesn’t he go
to the war?” I have heard it asked of men
who seem to be free and fit but are neither.
It is one of the little defects of the voluntary
enlistment ideal which works so noisily and
with such patchy results. Heaven ' only
knows how many indignant strangers and
semi-strangers have wondered why I don’t
go to the war! They can’t be blamed either,
for I don’t wear a placard on my back stat-
ing that I am married with two children
and have a gallery of false teeth in my head.
The grand old voluntary sham is only being
upheld at the cost of mutual suspicion. For
aii our detestation of German spies, in this
matter at least we are all spying on one
another and frequently criticising ignorantly
and cruelly. It must be a great source of
secret merriment to-the Huns who are still
at large amongst us.
The allegation of the shiftless party which
says the States should borrow and borrow
again is that the money, by developing the
great natural resources, earns more than its
keep. Well. Westralia and N. S. Wales
are the champion pawners of the Common-
wealth. Westralia, whose nublic debt 10
years ago was £67 9s. 4d. per head, now
owes £lll 14s. per head, so the development
of the great natural resources of Westralia
has presumably been something extra. It
has been so extra, in fact, that the Perth
Labor Federation spent a night recently
hunting up odds and ends of work. It
decided, for example, that, to give the
plumbers something to do. all owners of
metropolitan property ought to be com-
pelled to sewer their premises; and, so that
some unemployed carpenters might have a
job, no more prison labor ought to be em-
ployed in preparing for the military camp at
Garden Island. As these citizens are still
unhappy, though paying interest on £llO
per head, it seems a fair thing for John
Scaddan to mention how much more he
reckons it will take to do the trick.
TRUE LOVE'S WHISKERS.
As he languished alone on the merciless billow,
He sent her his whiskers to make her a pillow.
But she swooned right away as a matter of course
When her husband just used them to bed down his
. horse.
Away ! Away ! O’er hills and vales
To Sydney City—New South Wales.
’Tis there ’mid George Street’s busy din
Abides The Sydney Bulletin.
Seek out that ogre in his lair,
Who answers correspondents there:
Present these lyrics that I wrote,
And o’er them let the monster gloat.
