IN BANANA LAND
A certain Presser, of South Queensland,
who recently recruited, walked 15 miles to
present himself for examination. He has a
wife and five children, and he tells how he
stopped five times on his journey to ponder
whether lie was doing the right thing. Ap-
parently it was once for each youngster;
and every youngster said “Go !”
“Barcoo” : The Fitzroy River at Rockhamp-
ton has the peculiar legend attached to
it that it takes its toll of victims in tripli-
cate. The sequence is simply uncanny.
Immediately a case of drowning occurs the
inhabitants begin speculating as to when
and who the next two will be. The writer,
a short time ago, was having a nightcap
with a young fellow-lodger. There had been
a drowning case —the second that week—
and he remarked. “I wonder who is going
to make the usual third?” Two hours after
sunrise next morning he was fished out of
the river, dressed just as lie was when he
went, upstairs, ostensibly to retire for the
night. The thing had evidently got on to
the weak spot in his brain.
"P.F.”: Is there a more generous spot on
this wide earth than the Barkly Tableland,
which raised £BOO for the Patriotic Eund by
a two-days’ carnival? The population,
roughly speaking, is 100.
Seafaring men are noticing the unusual
freedom from wreck and casualties of late
along the Queensland coast. But some years
you can,go anywhere and do anything down
that seaboard with impunity. A few years
ago the captain of a China boat sailed" into
Rockingham Bay, near Cardwell, over the
Barrier Reef, and then, having discovered
that this was not Rockingham Bay (W.A.),
sailed calmly out again. Only the skipper
who knows that spot can appreciate that
captain’s feat.
The standing joke of Enoggera camp is
about a man who left with the last batch of
infantry for the Front. He was a bushman-
drover, this chap, and knew a horse from
nose to tail. Naturally lie was rejected on
offering for the Light Horse on account of
his riding, but got into the boot brigade.
Home weeks later, the officer who rejected
him put up a prize for the best rider of
Skuthorp’s buckjumpers. The reject en-
tered and won the cash and an officer’s
warm congratulations on his horsemanship.
REINFORCEMENTS.
The -Order of Merit has been conferred on Prince Rupprecht to mollify him, after a quarrel with the
German Crown Prince, because the latter received reinforcements intended for Prince Rupprecht, who
was hard pressed in the Argonne.— Cable.
THE SUBALTERN : “Reinforcements urgently wanted, sir! Our line is being cut to pieces.”
THE GENERAL: "Sorry we’re running short of reinforcements! But help yourself to an
Order of Merit and half a dozen Iron Crosses.”
Colonel Rankin, of the Assembly, is the
first Northern legislator to volunteer for the
Front. Politicians of all parties gave a
kindly send-off to the stiff Tory but unde-
niably sound patriot.
From an interview between Queensland
Minister for Railways and a deputation:—
Mr. Adamson: Some 16 railways were passed by
Parliament last session, and there was not a bean
left in the Treasury.
Mr. Bodge: You admitted yourself there was
£1,700,000.
Mr. Adamson: That money was for other railways.
There were railways to cost 2i millions passed last
session, and not a bean to meet the expenditure.
And on this kind of rotten basis does Party
Government rest! Anything is promised to
gel support, and everything is repudiated
when the time comes for fulfilment. Even
the present Government, with the full know-
ledge of the financial position, has been
making promises and half-promises of fur-
ther railway extensions for months past!
Much of the credit for the proclamation
of the Lamington Plateau as a National
Park is due to a young Queenslander, R.
Lahey, a B.E. of Sydney Uni. Lakey spent
the time between his first refusal for the
Australian forces on account of wanting an
inch in height and his later acceptance, in
lecturing on the beauties of the plateau and
the need of its reservation. While he was
doing this, amazed yokels expressed aston-
ishment at a kid taking on such a job. Then
when the place was gazetted, young Lahey,
believing that the people could be relied on
to prevent alienation (Brisbane’s usual
course seems to be to take park-lands for
railway yards), decided to help keep the
Huns from getting their clutches on the
place.
Stemming the Tide.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gallant Russians, dead in myriads, strew
Galicia’s blackened plains;
Half a million Russian corpses swell the
rivers in the rains ;
Down the reddened waters floating, past old
Warsaw’s famous town,
See them drift in corpse battalions, faces up
and faces down ;
And the nations, ever watching, ask with
anxious, weary cry :
“Will the Russian win and save us? Will
lie fail and let us die?
Will he balk the fell invaders who his
country over-ride?
Will the battered, bleeding legions of the
moujik stem the tide?”
And in Britain tens of thousands who have
never touched a gun,
Never slept a night in trenches, seen a hold-
ing lost and won r
Pallid folk who say: “We’re guarded quite
securely by the sea,
And our battleships will answer that Our
Land is safe and free,”
Read the papers every morning with an
interest close and tense,
Thanking God .that they, of all men, sleep
within the Ocean fence,
And remark, between the tea-sips: “Surely
Heaven is on our side,
And the Lord will aid (lie moujik when he
goes to stem the tide!”
Men who boast of this our Empire, of our
wide dominions free,
Is it just, amid the carnage, that ye shout
exultantly
At the bravery of the Belgian, at the cour-
age of the Gaul,
At the daring of the myriads who in Flan-
ders fight and fall.
While ye whisper as ye shelter in your trim
and guarded field :
"For our land there’s little danger, for their
prowess is our shield”?
Does your manhood never prick you. if it
have not wholly died,
That ye leave the battered moujik to the
stemming of the tide?
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 2S, 1915.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Although Governor Goold-Adams is still
indisposed all entertainments have not been
cancelled. The Methody centenary has been
thrilling us all the week, the live wire being
an “Evangelistic bishop”—one H‘oss —who
came all the way from U.S.A. to see what
kind of emus we are.
And then there was the National Council
of Women and the Eternal Comp. The
Council has been holding its annual corro-
boree, and during the proceedings a dame
exclaimed, “Poor dears!” When one of the
local papers quoted her as saying "Poor
devils!” the row was deafening.
Major T. J. Logan has been killed in
action at Gallipoli. He was 38, and a
typical Australian soldier. During the Boer
war he fought with the First Contingent,
and was an extremely popular officer. To
do his share this time he left for the Turkish
Front with the 2nd Light Horse and was
in command when the end came. A widow
and six little Australians are left. a.
younger brother, Private J. M. Logan, has
been wounded, and Corporal A. J. Logan
is in hospital at Malta. Still another
brother, holding, a captain’s commission, left
recently with Light Horse reinforcements.
Captain Colin Douglas Austin, killed at
the Dardanelles, is an old Brisbane resident
who was aide-de-camp to Henry Wiley Nor-
man when that warrior and knight was
Governor . (1889-’95). Captain Austin
fought against Brother Boer and afterwards
.did responsible work in a military capacity.
About six years ago he left Africa, made 'a
trip to the Solomons, and thence to Sydney,
where he had dug himself into business when
the Hun broke out of his cage. Captain
Austin’s young wife is a Sydney girl.
Dr. G. I’. Merz, of Ballarat, the young
Australian aviator, missing somewhere along
the Persian Gulf, was engaged to a-Brisbane
girl, nurse Dora Rowe, daughter of chaplain
George Rowe, who returned from Egypt on
Thursday night.
J. Tyson Doneley, of Tyson Manor, Too-
woomba, passed over on Thursday after-
noon. He was one of the best-known men
in Southern Queensland, and black-trackers
couldn’t have found him an enemy. His
eldest son.. W. 11. Doneley, is in America;
the rest of the family is in Bananaland.
Miss Betty Cockburn passed the festive
scone in honor of Miss' Helen Barnes, of
the Y.W.C.A., last Saturday afternoon. The
Cockburns live at Yeddo, New Farm, the
Japanese house tjjat the late Judge Paul
transplanted bodily from the land of the
‘cherry blossom and set up on a prosaic
block of land in one of our "best” suburbs.
The hostess, who has a beautiful contralto
voice, distributed it sweetly. Lena Ham-
mond also poured out melody, and Gladys
Frost put the piano through its paces.
The Nikko Maru. which left here on Wed-
nesday, carried off a number of Queens-
landers, on holiday bent. Among them were
Mr. G. C. Cory, of Toowoomba, and his
much-travelled daughter, and Mr. .Donkin,
the Belgian Consul. Harry Bell, once part
owner of Noorindoo station, and now of Syd-
ney, also was on board.
To-day the whole town was on holiday in
the interests of various patriotic funds. A
procession about five miles long went out to
the Exhibition grounds, and the chink of the
money-box was like a tambourine accom-
paniment. All the boys from the Expedi-
tionary forces now in camp were in the
march, and several motor-cars, laden with
veterans of the Mutiny and Crimea and our
own lads from Gallipoli, showed the way. A
competition for the Queen of the Day was
won by a nice girl from Finney’s.
“To the Day,” Miss Nora Gpllisson’s. play,
was a big success last night. Mrs. I\ j.
Symes, who represented Britannia, deserves
a cargo of eulogies for her compliment t<j
that lady, as well as for her work in train-
ing over 100 amateurs. The girls who took
part were excellently coached, and the show
was one of the best of the kind seen in
Brisbane. Proceeds (over £800) go into
patriotic coffers.
Phyllis Scholes, a popular nurse at the
General Hospital, was married recently to
her soldier boy, who is now on his way to
Shrapnel-land.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Mulga” :j I’ll tally-o and raise “Viking”
(B. 19/8/T5) one about foxes in the North.
I have seen red-pelt in the. CloncuiTy dis-
trict, and I’ll send “Viking” a copy of the
language of a couple of sheep-owners there-
abouts concerning the brutes on receipt of
an asbestos 1 stamp.-
Ex-Treasurer Barnes was some time ago
appointed arbitrator in a* dispute between
the Maryborough Town Council and one Ei
J. Redmond. Barnes’s “bill of costs” ran to
£1 145., for out-of-pocket expenses. What
an insult; to Bill of Costs to call this con-
temptible thing by such a name !
The good old Chinese custom of ripening
bananas by sleeping among them still flour-
ishes. One Charlie Lee, of Brisbane, had a
three-roomed domicile so arranged that the
middle apartment was packed with fruit
while the two on either side were packed
with sleeping Chows. Fruit ripened under
these conditions has a Celestial flavor-
unique and unforgettable. (Fine, £2 ss. 6d.)
J. R. Taylor, the latest Brisbane press-
man to volunteer for the Unpleasantness, is
a son of R. S. Taylor, who is filling the gap
in the Coubier’s leader-writing staff left by
the departure for the same uncertain destina-
tion of Col. R. Spencer Browne. Other Bris-
bane journalists who have taken rifles out of
the Commonwealth are Bruce Macgregor, B.
White and R. Clay.
The Queensland Government’s alarmed
talk about the need for conserving its scanty
timber lands recalls the respect that Warri-
gal showed for his ancestral forests. A
blackfellow who cut down a tree on the land
of another tribe might be killed when caught,
and many a white man on the River Mary
found a spear in his ribs as a punishment
for having laid a sacrilegious axe into the
butt of a bunya pine.
Even a Legislative Council can be terri-
fied into yielding. The Sugar Acquisition
Bill, which goes the whole hog in at least
temporarily nationalising the industry from
the point at which the cane leaves the hands
of the grower, passed the Council with less
than two hours’ debate, and with only half
a _ dozen negative votes. The most deter-
mined expression of hostility to Ryan so far
raised by the House of Last Say has been a
failure on one occasion to provide a quorum.
11.. L. Le Fanu, of Brisbane, will shortly
hang an apron above the gaiters which he
now wears as an Anglican archdeacon, Arch-
bishop Donaldson having appointed him
bishop-coadjutor of the Brisbane diocese.
The position has been vacant since Stretch,
who now bishes at Newcastle (N.S.W.), left
Brisbane some 15 years ago. Le Fanu is an
Irishman, whose importation was one of
Donaldson’s first acts after his own arrival
from the Cold Country. Though not dis-
tinguished as a preacher, in his 11 years at
Brisbane he has come to be looked upon as
the business man of the diocese, and his new
appointment will give him a further chance
of displaying the powers of organisation of
which his friends accuse him. In Church
politics he is reckoned “moderate High”—a
good deal “higher” than Donaldson. Le
Fanu has twice been left in charge of the
diocese for a year, during Archbish. Donald-
son’s absence “at home.” On the first occa-
sion—now some years back —he raised
£lO,OOO to pay off the debt on the completed
portion of Brisbane Cathedral as a surprise
for the Archgaiters on their return.
When Joe Lindsay, >of Morwell (Vic.),
lay dying at Alexandria of a wound in the
head, lie dictated a letter for his mother
to the Presbyterian chaplain, who duly
posted it. Lindsay died at the end of the
month, and the chaplain sent another letter,
telling how the boy went out. The chap-
lain’s letter arrived; hut Joe’s dictated
message didn’t. Joe’s parents badly want
that letter —want it worse than anything
else in the world. Perhaps some Bulletin
reader may happen on it, among nis mail
from Bill and Jim in the trenches. If so he
will know what to do with it.

He Heard the Bugles
Calling.
There s an .old red mill at the foot of the hill.
Hear the mill-wheel turning, turning,
To the drip of tears, through the long, long years.
Of my heart’s relentless yearning.
Oh I the tender note of the catch in his throat
Oh I the tear that he dried with laughter ■ —..
" I’ll be back some day.
Mind the mill while I’m away,’’
And he waved one last kiss floating after
Gone is the miller boy,
Gone from the mill;
Gone up the winding road,
Gone o'er the hill:
Gone with the drum beat up over the hill.
Where he heard the bugles calling.
There's no grist for the mill or siller for the till.
But I've kept the mill-wheel turning.
To the rumble and the beat of a million marching feet.
And my sad heart's muffled yearning.
Oh I the road his brave feet trod, lit with glory
up to God,
Oh I the courage of his call shames my sorrow :
’’ I’ll be back some day,
Mind the mill while I'm away,"
And I caught one last kiss for to-morrow
Gone is the miller bov,
Gone from the mill;
Gone up the winding road.
Gone o'er the hill;
Gone with the drum beat up over tine hill, 6 s
Where he heard the bugles calling. J*
Carey C. V. Briggs
LEFT BEHIND.
The tramp of feet, the crowded street.
The clash of steel, the long array
Of dust'dad men, and then again
The rise and fall of feet that say.
You hale of limb, infirm of mind.
You’re left behind, you're left behind.”
You stout supporters of the post
That rounds the corner to the bar.
You stalwart men who hear the charge
For “Just one more, then catch the car
Can you not hear it down the wind ?
You’re left behind, you're left behind '
By heavens, old chap, you nearly cheered.
Look out I Don't waste your breath like that:
You'll want it yet to sup your beer
And say. “ I .bet old Bill a bob
He'll stop a bullet with his nob;
Ain't he a mug to lose his job ? "
Yet Heaven send that Bill returns.
His duty done, his conscience clear
You’ll line the street to greet the “mug'
(You’d sooner greet a mug of beer).
Let's hope he’ll spurn your proffered hand
You'll understand, you’ll understand
When peace returns and plenty reigns
And two-up schools are in recess,
And |obs are offered to the man
Who helped his country in a mess.
Let s hope this answer you will
find,
“ You' re left behind, you re left
behind.'
The throbbing footfalls die away.
The gazers and the crowd depart.
And still those echoes haunt my brain
And wring... the teardrops from my
heart.
Too old 1 Too blind I Still down
tjjie wind
I catch it—“Left behind: behind.'

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).

perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Tenaculum” : It is not easy to understand
why the Germans (the same yarn, by the
way, was told of the Japs) should have tried
to corner Australian grass-tree gum for the
sake of its picric acid. Picric —otherwise
tnnitro-phenoi—is freely begotten of phenol
(carbolic acid) by nitric acid, the sire of all
these potent “substitution-products.” Lack of
nitro-compounds is not likely to hobble the
Huns, which is one of the main reasons for
cutting off the supply of the indispensable
base-stuffs —notably cotton. Mightily-boosted
German chemical science, by the way, seems
to have overlooked a very ordinary sub-
stance, which the present writer has found
to possess tremendous explosive possibilities
—wheat-starch, treated with nitric and sul-
phuric acids, and subjected to a further pro-
cess which for the present I wouldn’t tell
you for twice the money you’ll pay me for
this paragraph.
“The Pariah” : The passing of the Pacific
Mail Co.’s Hag from the Pacific at the end
of the year will mark the close of a strenu-
ous effort by. Uncle Dollars to get a whack
of the increasing trade of that ocean, and
build up his mercantile marine. The line
has never paid well, but J. B. Harrington
kept it going out of the immense profits of
his railroad. He was the pioneer of the big
ship in the Pacific, the Minnesota and
Dakota each being something over 20,000
tons. They were a dead loss from the jump,
and the co. must have been relieved when
the Dakota piled up in broad daylight out-
side Yokohama. These boats were the best-
appointed on the run ; but the Jap came up
smiling with two 18,000-tonners which beat
them for speed. Harrington got some con-
solation from the fact that a British-built
ship, the old Empress of India, showed the
faster of the two Japs her heels. The vessels
met in mid-ocean out of Tokio, and the
Canadian challenged Nippon to a race to
Hongkong. Both arrived and left Yokohama
together, and the Empress was first to
Shanghai. But when leaving there she ran
on a sandbank, and roosted there while the
Jap went cock-a-doodling southward. Some
time later the handsome Clvde-built boat got
clear and was driven furiously with a special
supply of-Caledonian oaths until she passed
her big opponent and won to berth with
20 minutes to spare. It was a great day
for McAndrew.
“W.A.8..”: Any Government that is
anxious to help the wheat-grower should
investigate the jute-broking business. I
know a few brokers in that trade who
started not long ago with no capital except
a glib tongue, and to-day are worry-
ing themselves how to dodge the in-
come tax. Every turnover in jute means a
ha’penny a dozen, or Is. Id. on a bale, of
cornsacks and 2s. 2d. on “brans,” all of
which comes out of the farmer’s pocket and
goes to the broker. Thousands of bales of
jute do. somersaults daily on paper without
a farthing changing hands —except for
brokerage. Is anyone going to put a foot on
this business? Or do these Governments
which profess great concern for the farmer
think that threepence a bushel is a fair
thing for farmers to pay for the mere
wrapper that goes round their wheat?
* * * *
A beak over at Nelson (M.L.) was so
moved by the beauty of the law’s ears the
other day, when the animal stuck its head
in the court window, that he immediately
called it brother. A baker was charged with
selling light-weight bread. It was shown
that before purchasing a light loaf the pro-
secuting official had weighed the bread in
the presence of another person. On hearing
this the vigilant beak decided that if the
individual knew he was buying insufficient
weight, there could be no misrepresentation
on the part of the vendor. Case dismissed,
and all the local bffkers impressed by the
majesty of British Justice.

“0.K.”: In the name of the Prophet, no
figs! None, that is, that are enemy pro-
ducts, as most of them fire. I thoughtlessly
bought some the other day without noticing
the word “Smyrna” on the box.' But the
rest of the inscription was consoling, for the
figs were entitled “Monarch Brand,” and a
portrait c f King George was burned into the
I’d. Evidently their German exploiters have
been teaching the, Turks how to cater for
the export trade.

THE LAND OF PEACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
According to the Washington correspondent of
The Times, President Wilson is seeking a conference
with the six leading South American States, with
the object of endeavoring to discover a policy of
pacification in regard to Mexico. For the U.S. alone
Mexico expresses contempt.
General Carranza, one of the revolutionary leaders,
is losing no opportunity of snubbing the States,
while newspapers report a statement by General
Villa telling the U.S. A. Government to “go to
Hell.”
Can Uncle Samuel take offence,
And storm with shot and- shell
The place of Villa’s residence?
No ! He can go to !
The Kaiser scorns his friend likewise,
Or so the cables tell;
The meaning of his slow replies
To Notes is: “Go to 1”
Poor Uncle Sam! To men of blud
Three other letters spell
Thy name to-day. ’Tis Uncle Mud,
Whom they consign to .
But, whilst the fighting nations take
The deadly goods you sell,
Enough of dollars you will make
To pave the streets of .
Rejoice to think, whilst scorning vice
And doing very well,
That men ,of peace-at-any-price
Can always go to .
VICTORIA.

ity, so that the use of an opening medicine

Have You Had Influenza?
There arc certain disorders such as influ-
enza that especially debilitate and make the
body an easy prey for more dangerous dis-
eases. Ask those who have had the influ-.
enza regarding the present condition of their
health and most of them will answer : “Since
I had influenza I have never been well.”
They still have profuse perspiration, the per-
sistent weakness of the limbs, the disorgan-
ised digestion, shortness of breath and palpi-
tation of the heart caused by the thin-
blooded condition in which influenza .almost
always leaves its victim after the fever and
influenza have subsided. They are further-
more at the mercy of relapses and compli-
cations, often very serious. In an attack
of influenza there is a rapid thinning of the
blood, and not until the blood is built up
again is complete health restored.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills quickly make
the blood rich and red, drive out the linger-
ing germs from the system, and transform
despondent influenza victims into cheerful,
healthy men and women.
Try the pills for any form of debility
cause by thin blood.
All chemists sell Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Get a box to-day and begin at once to regain
your health.
Write to-day to the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co., Sydney, for the free book, “Build-
ing Up the Blood.” If you have stomach
trouble ask for a diet book also.*

Australian Childhood
7/6
FOR EVERY
PHOTO USED.
Portraits
Fifth Series.
THE United Commercial Travellers’ Association of
1 Australasia, Proprietors of the National Annual, Aus-
tralia To-day, encouraged by the success of their
four last yearly competitions, when many hundreds
of photos, were entered from every State in the
Commonwealth, wish to include in their coming -
Christmas issue at least 50 of the most attractive
portraits of Australian children (boys and girls). They
want them from every State in the Commonwealth,
and will pay 7/6 for every photo, used, while all
the others will be promptly returned after judging,
post paid. Ages of children to be from .youngest
babyhood up to 15 years, and photos, must have been
taken during the last 12 months. Previous entrants
may compete, but the photo, must have been taken
during the last 12 months. The help of all Aus-
tralians is cordially solicited in making these pages
of Australian child life worthy of Australia. They
will be circulated all over the world, and among our
soldiers at Gallipoli. No objection to receiving more
than one photo, from same family. Photos, must
be accompanied by name and address
and must reach the Editor of “Australia
To-day,” Box 496, G.P.0., Melbourne, by
September 27, 1915.

Walter Beaver, the late rector of Mans-
field (Vic.), who enlisted in July, despite the
general warning of his Archbishop, is one of
the military-camp victims of meningitis.
Beaver was a third-generation Australian,
unmarried, and 39. He had been at Mans-
field five years, succeeding parson Ernest
Mallett, an English friend of the Archbishop,
who was translated to the snug suburban
vicarage at Glen Iris.
The British Imperial Oil Co. (which is
understood to grow more British and less
alien as time goes on) will soon be starting
a new industry in Sydney. The co. is spend-
ing £65,000 on buildings and plant for the
making of tins and cases wherein to sell the
petrol imported in bulk. The 8.1. already
has factories in Melbourne and Adelaide for
tinning and packing kerosene, but the Syd-
ney house will be its first to deal with petrol.
The employees of the co. in Australia are
Australian, and out of 100 of them who
volunteered for the Front more than 70 have
been accepted.
Maoriland is agitated about the case
of Alice Parkinson. She was a domestic
servant at Napier when she “got into
trouble’’ and gave birth to a child. It died,
and her lover did not honor his promise of
marriage. She upbraided him bitterly, and
at a final interview —to which he brought a
mate, who took a hand by knocking the em-
bittered woman down—she shot him dead.
The jury found her guilty of manslaughter
and added a strong recommendation to
mercy. The reply of Chief Justice Stout
was to sentence the prisoner “for the term
of her natural life!” Since then Maoriland
spends much of its spare time arguing furi-
ously which, if either, is the dangerous
savage—Mary Parkinson or Robert Stout.

Recall “Nil’s” singing verses, “The Lach-
lan,” printed in the Christmas Bulletin ot
1904: —•
Bessy Brown! Bessy Brown!
Have vou quite forgot the Lachlan,
In the' time before the banks burst and the squatters
came to grief?
Aii! —green the days and gold the nights we spent
at Bell’s Willandra,
When the billabongs were flowing,
And the clover-burr a-blowing,
And we all were lords and ladies.
(And who but you the chief?)
Bessy Brown 1
“Bessy Brown” was, of course, Sylvester
(“Vessy”) Browne, Rolf Boldrewood’s
bigger brother, who died in Queensland the
other day and left a million friends- behind
him.

Mrs. Katherine Andrews, wife of the secre-
tary of the Melbourne Hospital, who lately
died at Caulfield (Y'ic.), once spoke and
wrote a good deal on political and literary
subjects. Iler ambitions soared highest in
the novel, "Stephen Kyrle.” published by
tjoo. Robertson. The book is based upon the
more-or-less real life of a prisoner at Rent-
ridge who had a wonderful gift for model-
ling. A specimen of the man’s work can be
seen in the fountain at the rear of the Cor-
don statue, opposite the Melbourne Treasury
Buildings.

The Turk is beginning to sub-edit the
Alters staff. Two of its former member;.
Sergeant A. N. Usslier and Private .T. Gurry
(“Stoiu- Riuldell” of many Bulletin pars),
have been blue-pencilled in Gallipoli. Usslier,
a most likeable chap of 2.“. who bad dist'n-
guished himself scholastically and in athletics
before lie joined the Auers, was sent back
from the trenches wounded. Recovering, he
dared the groat death anew —and lost ilr
game, lie was a g r od rifle-shot, and of the
physique which has so impressed Aslmiead
Bartlett. Curry was a South Australian lad
who. picked up out of an auctioneer's office,
served his apprenticeship to the Tnky Way on
the Barrier Miner at Broken Hill. A bril-
liant youngster, he was capable of great
work; but be was a rover, too, and the best
things be ever did for The Bulletin were
written while he was on the wallaby with
no other cares in the world but to fill liis
tucker bag and keep bis nap dry.

Adelaide Register lias a humorous kink
which S. Australia is used to and only smiles
at. In the days when the paper was sorcech-
ingly eager to make itself solid with the Fat-
men of the State, who would give their advts.
to Bonython’s Advertiser, it left off calling
the Labor party by that name and vowed
that henceforth every Laboritc should be
known as a Socialist. In S. Aus. nobody
minds; but it is a mystery why the S. hi.
Herald and papers in other States which get
their S. Aus. misinformation from the
Register should allow themselves to be
inoculated with the Register’s hydrophobia.
The other day, for instance, the S. M.
Herald announced that “a socialist lias
been returned unopposed for the Adelaide
No. 2 district of the Leg. Council.” Some
morning an angry Labor politician from S.
Aus. will surprise “Mr. James” horribly by
firing in a writ claiming damages for misde-
scription, whereupon “Mr. James” will wire
in haste to the Register to leave off making
an ass of itself.
* * * *
“0.K.”: Let us rejoice to know that press-
men are now forbidden the run of the
gardens at Federal Parliament House. Their
keen sense of propriety will thus be safe-
guarded. Wandering around the lawns under
the impression that everything in the garden
was lovely, they might be shocked to dis-
cover an ardent politician engaged in close
canoodle with a lady friend, as happened
one sunny day recently. The newspaper re-
porters were spared the sight, but it was a
demoralising experience for the members of
the Parliamentary staff. An al fresco can-
oodle in the morning may be defined as a
broad-daylight suggestion of what happens
after dark. #
Even Aslmiead Bartlett apparently didn’t
know until recently that there is a wide sea
between Australia and Maoriland. In his
big story of the storming of the cliffs of
Gallipoli the Maorilanders were not men-
tioned once from start to finish. They were
glorified as Australians until the “copy”
reached headquarters, when the necessary
sorting out was done. General Godley, who
is authority for this, states that Australia s
correspondent Bean has much pleased him
bv the way he has endeavored to give the
M.L. men (whom Godley is leading) a fair
show.
JUST LIKE HOME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I got a letter the other day from a scribbling,
sketching pal of mine,
In a foreign country, far away —somewhere out m
the firing’ line.
II < 0 emx the censor won’t let them say where they
are bearing the battle’s brunt,
So he dates, in the good Australian way, from “Some
Old Place at the Blanky Front.”
He says it stinks, and he says it's Ilell, and there
seems no hope of earthly release;
But somehow the scream of ~a passing shell carries
him back to the Days of Peace.
Where the soldiers howl in the camp at night, and
the groaning and cursing wounded come,
lie says “it’s no use trying to write — it’s just like
truing to work at home!”
I wanted to go to the Front myself to write a book
on the war of wars,
To stand on many a learned shelf, and be translated
in Helsingfors;
But I’ve funked it now, though you need not tell (you
never know how the news might roam),
For I’m perfectly sure that it must be llell if “its
just like trying to work at home.”
God help the woman 1 She docs not know the glori-
ous heights that our minds can scale —
The Inspirations that come and go while her life is
dead and her home is gaol.
The Poet and Artist booze and swear, and wander at
will ’neath the sunlit dome;
She must struggle and pinch and be worried there
—and no man ever should “work at home.”
N.S.NV.
HENRY LAWSON.

Best for Children and Grown Folk too.
DR. SHELDON’S NEW DISCOVERY has won the confidence of the
parents and the love of the children by its wonderful curative effect upon
the throat, chest and lungs. Children are particularly susceptible to
coughs and colds, and the medicine ivhich always protects them from all
dangers is the one mother recognises as best.
It is a wise mother who prepares for trouble in time of peace. She
places Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery on the shelf, and rests secure against
all winter dangers, knowing well she has the remedy which will protect
her little ones against the vicissitudes of cold weather.
ONE MOTHER SAYS:
“My little girl, who is aged eight years, had a nasty hard cough on her chest
for some time,” writes Mrs. McCulloch, 114 Campbell Street, Surry Hills, N.S.W.
“It seemed to be settling on her lungs; she also suffered with shooting pains all
over her body. She became so bad that I had to keep her home from school. She
was very feverish, so I put her to bed. -I tried numerous cough remedies, but
they seemed to have no effect whatever, and I was beginning to despair when a
lady friend advised me to give her Dr. Sheldon s New Discovery. I took her
advice, and bought a large bottle, and after taking this her cough was completely
cured. Whenever she has the slightest sign of a cough I give her a dose of Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery, and she is quickly well again.”
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery is the mother’s helper and the children’s friend
through all the winter months. It can be given to the youngest child and to the
sturdy school children, to the college student or to the father of a family. Whenever
there is a cold, Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery is the shortest pathway to good health.

PURE WOOD FIBRE
The Red Mist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHE thinks aloud as she sits alone,
And the magpies call in the evening
grey—
Oh, sorrow to her with the heart of stone
Who stole my lover away, away!
There is no peace in the light of the moon,
And little enough in the shine of the sun ;
And it's grieving and grieving that darkens
the noon,
And troubles me sore till the salt tears
run.
There’s Joyce.with the red cheeks says to
me,
Herself as gay as a crowned young queen :
“It’s pale you are, and it's sick, maybe ;
And what is it ails your heart, Noreen?”
At that I say, with a laugh in my voice
(For grief is an ill, dark thing to show) :
“It’s you with your tricks and your capers,
Joyce?
And the imp in your eyes that makes me
so.”
There’s one and another from near and far
Who come with their kind, sweet neighbor-
speech :
“It’s sick you look, and it’s pale you are ;
And what have you done with your bloom*
of the peach?”
1 cannot answer, I may not tell;
As an actor plays I play my part;
It’s little they’d care (as my heart knows
well),
If they but knew the hurt of my heart.
And even Joyce, who is kind as kind,
Would make a jest of my pain, perchance ;
For a feather afloat on an idle wind
Means more to the world than a spoiled
romance.
If I were a man I would do so much—
Be brave, make light of my weight of care;
Bring ease to my mind with a master-touch,
And find fit food for my heart elsewhere.
Yet soft and shy as a cooing dove
(’Twas thus I seemed to him —seem so
yet)
I cannot rest for remembering love,
Nor dream of a time when I may forget.
I think of his kisses that warmed like wine
When the low night-winds in the pine-trees
played;
And the roses white in the white moonshine
A startling light in the garden made.
f think, of his voice in those honeyed hours,
And wonder if words are flowers some-
times ;
All scent and color —are chiming flowers
That thrill the blood with their golden
chimes.
And then, the thought of the Other comes,
Her wiles and wonder and luring lies,
And the blood in my ears is a throb of drums,
And a red mist glimmers before my eyes.
And I go to the place where the thing lies
hid,
And its blade takes fire at my burning
touch ;
And I say to myself: “If the world were rid
Of three lives more, would it matter much ?
“Would it matter much in the big world’s
sight
If the sorry farce to the end were played,
And three ghosts trod through the Outer
Night—
The Loved, the Lover, the Love-Betrayed?
“If one that suffers and two that sing
Were made cold clay, were it well or ill?”
And I grasp the haft of the jewelled thing.
And stand in the lamplight pale and still.
And then I shudder, and sob and sink,
And lie, eyes bid, with the light turned
low,
Like one a-poise on an awful brink,
Wild-eyed, and trembling, and breathing
slow.
And then comes Joyce with her joyous cries:
“Come out in the night, come sing with
me!”
And I smooth my hair and I soothe my eyes,
But I know what the end of it all shall he.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Mollie Maguire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A BARE horn- before sunrise in the
main street of Jackanbill, and Mollie
Maguire, fresh and bright-eyed, and
accompanied by an under-sized, weak-chinned
individual in a motor dust-coat and goggles,
hurried up to the front gate of Phil Des-
mond’s motor garage.
“There you are,” she cried, “I knew there
was a motor shop here.”
The goggled one was pessimistic. “What’s
the use of it?” he growled. “It ain’t
open.”
Mollie looked at him in despair. He
pushed the goggles off his eyes and peered
through a crack in the fence.
Don’t see anybody about. Not a
machine in the yard. Looks as if the place
had gone bung. That settles it. We’re
copped.” He shrank visibly until his head
seemed merely a peg from which a motor coat
flapped idly.
“We’re not! Get goin’,” cried Mollie, in-
flating her chest and giving a coo-ee that
startled the birds on the other side of the
township.
“How can we get goin’?” he piped.
“Ain’t the bloomin’ tyre blowed out .an’ the
front kerridge in a t’ree-fut ditch l Git
goin’? I only wisht I could. Gripes, if I
ain’t the unlucky bloke.”
“Billy!”
“Well! ain’t I?....Here I snaps you up
under the very nose of your dad, an old Ivat-
zenjammer, honks out your ol’ toy, red
ottermobile; cuts orf wiv yer like a bloke in
a picter show....an’ this ’appens. This,
w’en wif ornary luck you’u been Missus
’Uxley ter-morrer mornin’.”
“You say that again and I refuse to go a
step farther.”
“Yous don’t sye !”
“I do, and I mean it. I am not going to
marry you now. You are only taking me
to Sydney.”
“O’ri, o’ri,” admitted Mr. Huxley, feeling
in his vest pocket for a cigarette. “ ’Ave it
your own wye....l’m on’y takin’ you to
Sydney—l don’t think !”
“And then you lose your head and let the
car run into a rabbit burrow.”
There was a gleam of vindictiveness in
his small red eyes, and his thin lips drew
back from his yellow teeth in a snarl. "So'd
you lose your head if you was bein’ chased
by two bloomin’ maniacs in a car that was
doin’ two miles to your one.”
“Kindly remember,” said Mollie in icy
tones, “that the maniacs, as you call them,
are my father and my prospective husband.
If your daughter and the lady you were
desirous of marrying had stolen your motor-
car and was running away with a perfect
stranger you’d be a maniac too.”
“Ach, don’t gas so much,” remarked the
faded hero William. “Try and get a move
on this bloke. DES... .Desmond’s Garage
and Agricultural Implement Depot. Mebbe
he'll lend us a steam plow or a reaper and
binder if we flash ’arf a quid at ’im. Hi,
hello, hell-o! They’ll be ’ere in two jiffs,”
he explained to Mollie, “an’ then hup goes
the bloomin’ donkey.”
“And you’ll go up, too,” chirruped Mollie.
“Don’t forget that.”
A side door in the gate opened, and Phil
Desmond, wearing his motor coat and cap,
smiled and bade them “Good morning.”
They rushed him with a conversational
duet.
“Say, mister”....
“Our car’s broke down”....
“There it is, at the bend of the road”....
“We want to get to the railway”. .. .
“Can you take us there, quick?”....
“N0w.”....
“Before they cop us”. . ..
Desmond hesitated.
“We are in such a hurry.” This was
Mollie’s solo. “We’ve just got to catch the
train. Can you please hire us a car? Or, if
you wouldn’t mind driving us to the siding
yourself. 8i11y....1 mean Mr. Huxley,
isn’t a very good driver.”
“I am a good driver,” Billy cut in. “One
of the best in Sydney, and I’ve got my
license to prove it.”
“I mean, he doesn’t drive very well in
the country.”
Phil caressed his chin and strove to under-
stand the comedy that was being enacted
before him.
“ ’Urry up,” growled William.. “We
gotta keep movin’.”
“Exactly,” said Desmond. “I was think-
ing. You see, I have only got one car and
that’s engaged.”
“So’re we,” announced William defiantly.
“We are not,” repudiated Mollie, stamp-
ing her foot. “We've given it up.” Des-
mond was beginning to realise the situation
and the next words made it almost clear.
“We are, we are,” he snapped, “an’ don’t
yous back outen it, jest because the bloomin’
car busts up. Look ’ere, mister, this job’s
worth a fiver to you. Git us to the siding
in time to flag the express, and the money’s
yours. It’s a soft thing, but yous can
have it for once.”
Sparring for time, Desmond prevaricated.
“It's the district holiday. I’ll get fined
ten pounds if I do.”
“An’ I’ll get 10 years if you don’t.”
“No, Billy,” gleefully explained Mollie.
“Not 10 years.... Two for abduction, one
for stealing the car, six weeks for furious
driving, and 10 pounds for killing the sheep.
Only about two and a half years alto-
gether.”
Billy refused to be comforted. “Dry up,”
he hissed. “Yous fair gives me the shivers,
you do. Can’t you see, boss, that I’ve gotta
get away. They’re only five minutes be’ind,
an’ they’ll ’ave me as sure as ’ell.”
“Who will?” asked Desmond, directing
his question at Mollie.
“My father and Mr. Sommerfeldt. They,
that is, he” ——
William cut in and stepped between her
and Phil.
“ ’Ere,” he said, “that’s ernuff. Do
you wanter put the ’ole show away? Do
you wanter lay everybody onto me? What’s
it gotter do wiv him, anyhow? ’E’s on’y a
bloke what ’ires us motor-cars. See here,
mister; fergit all her little song an’ darnce.
See?”
Desmond’s brows contracted, but he spoke
with suave mildness.
“It will be much more difficult to forget
yours. And if I am only a bloke what
hires you cars, remember, I haven't hired
you one—yet.”
Billy blinked and opined that there was
no need to get hot about it.
“There now!” shrilled Mollie, “you are
going to fight!”
Billy assured her that her fears were
groundless. It would be another 48 hours
before the battle started. Desmond reckoned
that a low estimate. There was a
flurry of dust beyond the trees. “Gripes,”
yelled Billy, “ ’ere they are.”
Mollie saw it, and Desmond noted the
sudden pallor of her cheeks.
“Come,” he said, “there’s only one way.
If that’s Karl Sommerfeldt's car it’s a
faster one than mine.” Billy spat the stub
of his cigarette on to the road and ground it
into the dust with his heel. “My car’s in
the yard. Jump into it” (He caught Billy
by the coat collar as he was dashing past.)
“Don’t take the road, Miss. Be sure of that.
Follow the track round the belt of trees.
Keep following it....don’t leave it, what-
ever you do. it will bring you” (Mollie noted
the emphasis on the personal pronoun, but
he repeated it again to make sure) —-“it will
bring you out all right. I’ll stay here and
delay them as much as I can.”
Billy had disappeared through the door,
therefore, he did not see Mollie throw her
arms round Phil’s neck in an excess of
gratitude, and kiss him, and call him “a
dear darling man” in between. Thil
(Continued on next page.)

was dazed. He smiled, lie laughed as he

holds the trump card he can afford to wait
for his opponent’s lead. He could hear them
arguing....a thin, Celtic crescendo and a
deep Teutonic guttural. He turned into
his shed and, picking up a handful of tools,
emerged to fall into the grip of a plethoric
and breathless German.

There was a sound of approaching wheels.
A motor horn tooted awkwardly within the
yard, and presently Desmond’s car broke
solemnly through the gate and chocked its
wheels on the debris. Mollie at the steering
gear stepped out. Paddy jubilantly folded
her in his arms and kissed her behind the
ear. Sommerfeldt hovered in the offing, and
Phil waited developments with equanimity.
Blushing like a morning rose, Mollie dis-
engaged herself from the paternal embrace
and said, "I suppose you know my friend
Mr. Desmond. Daddy?”
“Oh. jist to pass the time o’ the day to.”
“That's all right then,” she said, crossing
over to Phil and glancing shyly up at him.
Paddy winked to himself and towed Karl
over to inspect the new car.
"AA T e took that track of yours.” said
Mollie.

The Arras.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
r COULD have sworn that the arras
moved,
That curtain fringed with the fleur de
] ys ;
lou thought wo wore safe,, who snatched
. . . .and loved,
That a watchful eye could not pierce to
me;
Yet over the sweep of the purple fold
(Like an ocean door in a moonless night)
A ripple ran to the hem of gold—
One petal hid and one leapt to light.
I could have sworn that the arras moved!
You think it is locked in our hearts to-day.
That throbbing moment we clung and loved
is sealed forever and shut away
AY here the jealous eye cannot note and
brood
On loss or profit in provingi shame.
The right of spilling a gallant’s blood.
The right of branding a woman’s name.
To-day we met in the gallery.
You bowed full coldly and passed me by
And bravely went, for the world to see
No lip-betraying, no softening eye.
’Twas well that the gown was tightly laced
Over my heart, and its stung desire.
Over the breast where your kisses placed
The rose of love with its thorn of fire.
A sword-point clashed on the polished floor;
Two heels clicked sharply ere you were
gone.
But I stepped as proud as I went before
By the line of the courtiers all alone.
Yet.... 1 remembered the arras moved
And shaken trust in a world a-reel
Convulsed the air like a faith disproved.
Who dr opt the sword and who clicked I lie
heel ?
I have smoothed my bodice and dressed my
hair;
The snow of my bosom naught reveals.
Was it only fancy that one lurked there.
Kneeling to spy, as a traitor kneels?
Was it only fancy? You kissed so warm,
Your kiss was all for a space I knew;
And the pulse’s riot will jest at harm.
And fear sinks drugged in a glance of blue.
What heart pays toll for its secret sins?
So each heart feareth a torch’s shine,
And up in his tower he sits and spins
Perhaps a snare for your life and mine.
....The King was gracious to me to-day
And the Queen (that model of virtue
proved)
And my lord sent jewels to make me gay....
Yet I wish I knew why the arras moved!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
